MODULE 1

How Computers Work

5h lecture + 3h lab
Source: Skill Guide 11 — Chips, Computers, Silicon
1.1 — The Five Components
Learning objectives
  • Name the five core components of every modern computer and state what each one does
  • Trace how data flows from storage to the processor and back when you run an AI model
  • Explain which component is usually the bottleneck for local AI, and why
  • Read a spec sheet and know immediately whether a machine can run a given model

Every computer you will ever deploy — a $599 Mac Mini or a $400,000 GPU server — is built from the same five components. Master what they do and every hardware conversation for the rest of this course becomes easy.

1. The CPU (Central Processing Unit) is the general-purpose brain. It executes instructions one after another, very fast, and it is brilliant at tasks with lots of decisions: running the operating system, handling network traffic, parsing files. A modern CPU has somewhere between 8 and 32 "cores" — independent mini-brains that each work on their own task. CPUs are flexible but they process work mostly in sequence, and that matters in a moment.

2. The GPU (Graphics Processing Unit) was invented to draw video-game graphics, which requires doing the same simple math operation on millions of pixels at once. It turns out AI needs exactly that: a neural network is mostly multiplying enormous grids of numbers (called matrices) together. A GPU has thousands of small cores that all work simultaneously — not 16 brilliant workers like a CPU, but 10,000 simple ones marching in lockstep. That is why AI runs on GPUs.

3. RAM (Random Access Memory) is the workbench. It is fast, temporary storage that holds whatever the computer is actively working on, and it empties when the power goes off. Here is the single most important fact in this lesson: an AI model must fit in memory to run. A model is a giant file of numbers (its "weights" — you'll learn these in Module 2), and every one of those numbers has to be loaded into RAM (or the GPU's own memory, called VRAM) before the model can answer a single question. A model that needs 24 GB will not run on a 16 GB machine. This is why the first question you ask about any client machine is "how much memory does it have?"

4. The SSD (Solid State Drive) is the filing cabinet — permanent storage that keeps its contents when powered off. Your models, documents, and the operating system live here. SSDs are fast compared to old spinning hard drives, but they are still 50–100× slower than RAM. The SSD determines how many models you can keep installed and how fast they load; the RAM determines whether they can run at all.

5. The Neural Engine (NPU — Neural Processing Unit) is the newest member: a small specialized chip block designed only for AI math, optimized for low power. Apple's Neural Engine, Qualcomm's Hexagon, and Intel's AI Boost are all NPUs. Today they handle light AI tasks (photo recognition, dictation) while the GPU still does the heavy lifting for large language models — but the category is growing fast.

Now trace the flow when a user asks your deployed AI a question: the model's weights sit on the SSD → they load into RAM/VRAM (once, at startup) → the CPU receives the user's text, prepares it, and hands it off → the GPU/NPU does billions of multiplications against the weights → the answer streams back through the CPU to the user's screen. The slowest link in that chain sets your speed — and for AI, the slow link is almost always how fast memory can feed the processor, not the processor itself. Hold that thought for Lesson 1.3.

Walkthrough — read a real spec sheet

A client forwards you a listing: "Mac Mini — M4 Pro, 14-core CPU, 20-core GPU, 16-core Neural Engine, 64GB unified memory, 1TB SSD." Decode it: 14 CPU cores run the system and apps; 20 GPU cores do AI math; 64 GB of memory means models up to roughly 40–50 GB can load comfortably (you always leave room for the operating system and the conversation itself); 1 TB of SSD holds 15–25 typical local models. Verdict: this machine runs mid-size open models smoothly — a strong Phase 1 deployment box.

KEY TERMS: CPU · GPU · RAM · VRAM · SSD · NPU / Neural Engine · core · weights

Common mistakes
  • Confusing storage with memory. "My Mac has 512 GB" usually means SSD. A 512 GB SSD with 8 GB of RAM still cannot run a 20 GB model.
  • Buying CPU speed for AI work. For local inference, memory size and memory speed beat CPU clock speed almost every time.
  • Forgetting overhead. A "16 GB model" needs more than 16 GB free — the OS, the chat interface, and the conversation context all take their share.
Knowledge check
Why do GPUs beat CPUs at AI even though CPUs are "smarter"?
Answer: AI is mostly the same simple math (matrix multiplication) repeated billions of times. A GPU's thousands of simple cores do that in parallel; a CPU's handful of powerful cores work largely in sequence.
A client has a PC with 8 GB RAM and a 2 TB SSD. Can it run a model that needs 12 GB of memory?
Answer: No. Models must fit in RAM/VRAM to run. The big SSD only means they can store many models — not run them.
In the data-flow chain, which link usually limits local AI speed?
Answer: Memory — specifically how fast memory can feed weights to the processor (memory bandwidth), not raw processor speed.
1.2 — Binary and Computation
Learning objectives
  • Explain what bits and bytes are and convert between common units (KB, MB, GB, TB)
  • Describe how transistors and logic gates turn electricity into math
  • Connect number precision (32-bit vs 8-bit vs 4-bit) to AI model size — the foundation of quantization

Everything a computer does — every chat reply, every video frame — reduces to electricity switching between two states: on and off, written as 1 and 0. One such on/off value is a bit. Eight bits grouped together make a byte, and a byte is enough to store one character, like the letter "A" (stored as 01000001). From there the units just stack by roughly a thousand: ~1,000 bytes is a kilobyte (KB), ~1,000 KB is a megabyte (MB), ~1,000 MB is a gigabyte (GB), ~1,000 GB is a terabyte (TB). An AI model file measured in gigabytes is literally billions of bytes of numbers.

How does "on and off" become math? Through the transistor — a microscopic electrical switch with no moving parts. A modern chip packs tens of billions of them, each smaller than a virus. Wire a few transistors together cleverly and you get a logic gate: a tiny circuit that takes one or two bits in and produces one bit out by a fixed rule. An AND gate outputs 1 only if both inputs are 1. An OR gate outputs 1 if either input is. A NOT gate flips its input. These three rules sound trivial, but chain enough of them and you can build an adder — a circuit that adds two binary numbers. Chain adders and you can multiply. Chain multipliers by the million and you have a GPU doing matrix math for a neural network. There is no magic layer underneath: it is switches, all the way down.

Numbers with decimal points (like a neural network's weights) are stored in a format called floating point. The format has sizes: a 32-bit float (called FP32) uses 4 bytes and is very precise; a 16-bit float (FP16) uses 2 bytes with a little less precision; an 8-bit integer (INT8) uses 1 byte; a 4-bit number uses half a byte. Here is why a consultant cares: a model's file size is just (number of weights) × (bytes per weight). A 7-billion-parameter model stored in FP16 is 7B × 2 bytes = ~14 GB. Store those same weights in 4-bit form and it shrinks to ~3.5 GB — small enough for a laptop. That trick — trading a sliver of precision for a huge memory saving — is called quantization, and it is the single technology that makes local AI on normal hardware possible. You will use it constantly from Module 4 onward.

Last concept: the chip's clock. Every gate in a processor marches to a metronome ticking billions of times per second — 3 GHz means 3 billion ticks per second. Clock speed matters, but as you learned in 1.1, for AI the question is rarely "how fast can the chip compute" and almost always "how fast can memory deliver the next batch of weights."

Walkthrough — size a model with arithmetic

You want to know if an 8-billion-parameter model fits on a 16 GB MacBook. Do the math at three precisions:

FP16:  8,000,000,000 weights × 2.0 bytes = 16.0 GB  → too big (no room for the OS)
INT8:  8,000,000,000 weights × 1.0 bytes =  8.0 GB  → fits, comfortable
4-bit: 8,000,000,000 weights × 0.5 bytes =  4.0 GB  → fits easily, fastest to load

Rule of thumb you will use for the rest of your career: a 4-bit quantized model needs roughly (parameters ÷ 2) GB of memory, plus 1–3 GB of overhead.

KEY TERMS: bit · byte · transistor · logic gate · floating point · FP16 · INT8 · quantization · clock speed

Common mistakes
  • Mixing up bits and bytes. Internet speeds are sold in megaBITS per second; file sizes are megaBYTES — an 8× difference. A "500 Mbps" connection downloads about 60 MB per second.
  • Assuming quantization ruins quality. Going from FP16 to 4-bit typically costs only a few percent in accuracy — usually imperceptible in chat — while cutting memory by 75%.
  • Thinking GHz is the whole story. Two chips at the same clock speed can differ wildly in real AI performance because of memory bandwidth and core counts.
Knowledge check
Roughly how much memory does a 14-billion-parameter model need at 4-bit quantization?
Answer: ~7 GB for the weights (14B × 0.5 bytes), plus 1–3 GB overhead — so it runs on a 16 GB machine.
What is a logic gate, in one sentence?
Answer: A tiny transistor circuit that takes bits in and produces a bit out by a fixed rule (AND, OR, NOT) — the building block all computation is assembled from.
Why does quantization matter to your consulting business specifically?
Answer: It lets capable models fit on affordable hardware — it is the reason a $1,500 Mac Mini can run a model that would otherwise demand a $30,000 GPU, which is the entire economic basis of Phase 1 deployments.
1.3 — Apple Silicon Architecture
Learning objectives
  • Explain what a System-on-a-Chip (SoC) is and how unified memory differs from a PC's split memory
  • Rank the M-series tiers (base / Pro / Max / Ultra) by AI capability
  • Use memory bandwidth numbers to predict local inference speed
  • Justify, to a client, why a Mac is often the best Phase 1 AI server

In 2020 Apple stopped buying Intel processors and started shipping its own chips — the M-series. The design choice they made turned out, almost accidentally, to be the perfect architecture for running AI locally, and it is the reason so many of your Phase 1 deployments will be Macs.

A traditional PC is a collection of separate parts: a CPU with its own RAM sticks, and a graphics card with its own separate memory (VRAM) on the other side of a relatively slow connector called PCIe. When a PC runs AI, data constantly shuttles across that connector, and the model is limited by the graphics card's VRAM — often just 8–16 GB on consumer cards, no matter how much system RAM sits idle nearby.

Apple Silicon is a System-on-a-Chip (SoC): the CPU cores, GPU cores, and Neural Engine are all printed on one piece of silicon, and — this is the key — they all share one giant pool of unified memory. There is no "CPU RAM" versus "GPU VRAM." If a Mac has 64 GB, the GPU can use essentially all of it. That means a Mac Studio configured with 128 GB or more of unified memory can load models that would require multiple $30,000 data-center GPUs to hold in VRAM. No consumer PC graphics card comes close on memory capacity per dollar.

The second advantage is memory bandwidth — how many gigabytes per second the memory can deliver to the processors. Recall from 1.1 that generating text means streaming the entire model's weights through the processor for every single token produced. So token speed is roughly (memory bandwidth) ÷ (model size). The M-series tiers are really bandwidth tiers: the base M-chip delivers on the order of 100–150 GB/s, the Pro roughly 200–300 GB/s, the Max roughly 400–550 GB/s, and the Ultra (two Max chips fused together) 800+ GB/s. Divide bandwidth by model size and you get a ceiling estimate of tokens per second: a 4-bit 8B model (~4 GB) on a Max-class chip (~500 GB/s) tops out near 500 ÷ 4 ≈ 125 tokens/sec theoretical — real-world results land at a healthy fraction of that, easily fast enough to feel instant.

The lineup you will spec from: Mac Mini (base/Pro chips — the budget Phase 1 box), Mac Studio (Max/Ultra chips, the highest memory configs — the serious office server), and MacBook Pro (Pro/Max — your portable demo machine, the one you close sales with in Module 11). Macs also idle at very low power, run silently, and need no special cooling — practical virtues when the "server room" is a closet in a dental office.

The honest limits: Macs are superb at inference (running models) but weak at training them (Module 2 covers the difference) — training still belongs to NVIDIA. And in Phase 3+ deployments serving dozens of simultaneous users, dedicated GPU servers with vLLM take over (Module 10). Know where the Mac story ends; it makes you more credible when you tell it.

Walkthrough — estimate speed before you buy 
Token speed ceiling ≈ memory bandwidth ÷ model size in memory

Mac Mini M4 (≈120 GB/s)      + 4 GB model  → ≈30 tok/s ceiling  → snappy chat
Mac Mini M4 Pro (≈273 GB/s)  + 9 GB model  → ≈30 tok/s ceiling  → bigger model, same feel
Mac Studio Max (≈546 GB/s)   + 20 GB model → ≈27 tok/s ceiling  → 30B-class models, smooth
Mac Studio Ultra (≈800+ GB/s)+ 40 GB model → ≈20 tok/s ceiling  → 70B-class models, usable

Humans read at roughly 5–8 tokens/second, so anything above ~15 tok/s feels fluid. This one formula lets you spec hardware on a napkin in front of a client.

KEY TERMS: System-on-a-Chip (SoC) · unified memory · memory bandwidth · tokens per second · M-series (base/Pro/Max/Ultra) · PCIe · inference

Common mistakes
  • Speccing by chip name instead of memory. An M4 with 16 GB is far less useful for AI than an M2 Max with 64 GB. Memory size and bandwidth outrank chip generation.
  • Promising training on a Mac. Fine-tuning small models is possible; serious training is not. Set expectations correctly in the sales call.
  • Ignoring the bandwidth tier. Two Macs with 36 GB can differ 4× in speed (base vs Max) because bandwidth, not capacity, sets tokens per second.
Knowledge check
What is unified memory and why does it matter for AI?
Answer: One memory pool shared by CPU, GPU, and Neural Engine on the same chip. It matters because the GPU can use nearly all system memory for a model — capacities consumer graphics cards can't match.
Estimate the token-speed ceiling: 273 GB/s bandwidth, 9 GB quantized model.
Answer: 273 ÷ 9 ≈ 30 tokens/second — comfortably faster than reading speed.
Name the two jobs Macs are NOT the right tool for.
Answer: Training/heavy fine-tuning of models, and high-concurrency serving for dozens of simultaneous users — both belong to NVIDIA GPU servers (Modules 2 and 10).
1.4 — NVIDIA and the GPU Revolution
Learning objectives
  • Explain CUDA and why it gives NVIDIA a moat beyond hardware
  • Describe what Tensor Cores do and why data-center GPUs differ from gaming cards
  • Summarize the H100/Blackwell class of hardware and the "AI factory" concept
  • Decide when a client engagement calls for NVIDIA instead of Apple Silicon

If Apple owns the local-AI desktop story, NVIDIA owns everything else — and understanding why protects you from underestimating it in front of a technical client.

NVIDIA's real moat is not chips; it is software. In 2007 they released CUDA (Compute Unified Device Architecture), a programming platform that let ordinary developers use GPUs for general math instead of just graphics. For nearly two decades, virtually every AI framework, research paper, and tool was built CUDA-first. Today the entire training ecosystem — PyTorch, the libraries on top of it, the optimizations underneath — assumes NVIDIA. A competitor can match the silicon; matching twenty years of software gravity is the hard part. This is called the CUDA ecosystem lock-in, and it is why NVIDIA holds the overwhelming share of AI training.

On the hardware side, modern NVIDIA chips add Tensor Cores — circuits that do nothing but matrix multiplication, the core operation of neural networks (Module 2 makes this concrete). Where a regular GPU core multiplies numbers one pair at a time, a Tensor Core multiplies whole small grids of numbers in one tick, at reduced precision (FP16, FP8, even FP4). The result is staggering throughput for exactly the math AI needs.

Know the product split. Gaming cards (GeForce RTX line) are consumer products: fast, but capped at modest VRAM (typically 8–24 GB) and licensed for desktops. Data-center GPUs are a different species: the H100 generation (Hopper architecture) packs 80 GB of ultra-fast HBM memory ("High Bandwidth Memory" — stacked directly beside the chip, delivering 3,000+ GB/s, several times any Mac), plus NVLink, a private high-speed bridge that lets eight GPUs in a server pool their memory and act as one giant accelerator. Its successor generation, Blackwell, raised that to 192 GB (B200/GB200), and the currently shipping B300 "Blackwell Ultra" packs 288 GB of HBM3e at roughly 8,000 GB/s per GPU — with rack-scale systems (GB300 NVL72) fusing 72 GPUs and 36 of NVIDIA's own Grace CPUs into a single liquid-cooled computing fabric. Prices run $25,000–40,000+ per GPU, and demand still outruns supply.

Scale that up and you reach the AI factory (or "mega factory") concept: warehouse-sized buildings holding tens of thousands of GPUs, consuming as much electricity as a small city, training frontier models for months at a time. Training one frontier model is estimated in the hundreds of millions of dollars of compute. This is the world your sovereignty pitch quietly leans on: your clients cannot build that — but they don't need to. Open-weight models trained in those factories are released for free, and your job is putting them on hardware the client owns.

When does NVIDIA enter your engagements? Three triggers: a client needs to serve many concurrent users (Phase 3–4, where vLLM on NVIDIA shines — Module 10); a client wants serious fine-tuning on their own data (Module 10); or the client already owns NVIDIA infrastructure. For a 5–25 person office chat deployment, Apple Silicon usually wins on cost, power, and simplicity.

Walkthrough — Apple vs NVIDIA decision in 60 seconds 
Client: law firm, 12 users, document Q&A, privacy-critical
  → Mac Studio, 64-128 GB unified memory. Done. (Phase 1-2)

Client: insurer, 300 users, internal chatbot + nightly batch processing
  → 2-4× NVIDIA GPUs (H100/Blackwell class or workstation RTX 6000-class),
    vLLM serving, Kubernetes. (Phase 3-4, Module 10)

Client: med-device firm wants a custom model tuned on 50k support tickets
  → Rent NVIDIA cloud GPUs for the fine-tune (days), deploy the result
    locally on Mac or NVIDIA. Hybrid play. (Module 10)

KEY TERMS: CUDA · Tensor Core · HBM · NVLink · H100 / Hopper · Blackwell / B200 / GB200 · AI factory · VRAM

Common mistakes
  • Pitching gaming cards as servers. A 16 GB RTX gaming card cannot hold serious models, and multi-user serving will choke it. Quote workstation/data-center parts for real concurrency.
  • Dismissing CUDA when a client's dev team pushes back. Their ML engineers live in the CUDA ecosystem; acknowledge it and position local Mac inference as complementary, not competing.
  • Forgetting power and cooling. One 700W GPU server can demand more electrical planning than ten Mac Studios. It belongs in your site survey (Module 7).
Knowledge check
What is NVIDIA's deepest competitive moat, and why?
Answer: CUDA — the software ecosystem. Nearly two decades of AI tools were built on it, so even equal hardware from a competitor faces a massive software migration barrier.
What do Tensor Cores accelerate, specifically?
Answer: Matrix multiplication at reduced precision — the single operation that dominates neural-network compute.
Name the three triggers that move an engagement from Apple Silicon to NVIDIA.
Answer: High user concurrency (Phase 3–4 serving), serious fine-tuning work, or existing client NVIDIA infrastructure.
1.5 — The Semiconductor Supply Chain
Learning objectives
  • Outline the journey from raw silicon to a finished AI chip
  • Explain what TSMC does, what photolithography is, and what "3-nanometer" actually means
  • Describe the geopolitical concentration risk and why it matters to AI hardware prices and availability

You sell sovereignty — owning your own compute. To tell that story with authority, you should know where compute itself comes from, because the supply chain behind every chip is the most concentrated and geopolitically fragile industrial process on Earth.

It starts as sand. Quartz is refined into 99.9999999% pure silicon, melted, and grown into a single flawless crystal cylinder, then sliced into mirror-polished discs called wafers, about 300mm across. Onto each wafer, a fabrication plant (a fab) prints billions of transistors using photolithography: light is projected through a stencil-like mask onto light-sensitive chemical coatings, etching patterns layer by layer — sometimes over 80 layers, taking three to four months per wafer. The most advanced step uses EUV (extreme ultraviolet) lithography, machines that fire molten tin droplets with lasers 50,000 times a second to generate light with a wavelength small enough to draw features a few atoms wide. Exactly one company in the world, ASML in the Netherlands, can build EUV machines. Each costs $200–400 million.

When you hear a chip is built on a "3-nanometer node", treat the number as a marketing-flavored generation label, not a literal measurement — but the direction is real: each new node packs transistors denser, which means more performance per watt. Apple's M-series and NVIDIA's flagships always queue for the newest node.

Who actually manufactures? Almost nobody designs and builds anymore. Apple and NVIDIA are "fabless" — they design chips and outsource manufacturing to a foundry. The foundry that matters is TSMC (Taiwan Semiconductor Manufacturing Company), which produces roughly 90% of the world's most advanced chips, overwhelmingly in Taiwan. Samsung (South Korea) and Intel (US) compete at the leading edge but hold a fraction of the share. Read that again: nearly every advanced AI chip on Earth — Apple's, NVIDIA's, AMD's, Qualcomm's — is born in a handful of buildings on one island that sits at the center of US–China strategic tension.

That concentration is why governments now treat chips like oil. The US CHIPS Act and similar programs in Europe and Japan are paying tens of billions to build fabs elsewhere (TSMC Arizona, Samsung Texas, Intel Ohio), and US export controls restrict the most advanced AI GPUs and tooling from reaching China — a running theme sometimes called the silicon war. New fabs take 3–5 years and $20–40 billion each, so diversification is slow.

What does this mean for a consultant? Three practical things. Availability: GPU supply moves in waves; a geopolitical scare or a demand spike (like every frontier-model release) can stretch lead times overnight. Pricing: hardware quotes should carry validity windows — lock prices when a client signs. The pitch: supply fragility is itself an argument for sovereignty. A client who owns a paid-for Mac Studio is insulated from cloud price hikes and GPU shortages in a way an API-dependent competitor never is.

Walkthrough — the chain in one view 
sand → 99.9999999% silicon ingot → polished 300mm wafer
  → photolithography at the fab (TSMC/Samsung/Intel, 80+ layers, ~3-4 months)
  → wafer cut into individual chips ("dies"), tested, packaged
  → onto boards (Apple SoC, NVIDIA GPU) → into your client's server

Chokepoints: ASML (only EUV maker) · TSMC (~90% of advanced chips) ·
             advanced packaging capacity · US export controls

KEY TERMS: wafer · fab · foundry · fabless · photolithography · EUV · process node · TSMC · ASML · CHIPS Act

Common mistakes
  • Reading "3nm" as a physical measurement. It's a generation label; compare transistor density and performance-per-watt instead.
  • Thinking Intel makes Apple's or NVIDIA's chips. Both are fabless; TSMC manufactures their leading parts.
  • Quoting hardware prices without an expiry date. Supply shocks are normal in this industry; protect your margins contractually.
Knowledge check
What is the difference between a fabless company and a foundry?
Answer: A fabless company (Apple, NVIDIA) designs chips but doesn't manufacture them. A foundry (TSMC) manufactures chips for others. TSMC makes ~90% of the world's most advanced chips.
Why does ASML matter so much for the entire AI industry?
Answer: It is the only company on Earth that builds EUV lithography machines — without which no leading-edge chip can be printed. One company is a chokepoint for the whole supply chain.
Give one way supply-chain fragility strengthens your sovereignty sales pitch.
Answer: A client who owns their hardware outright is insulated from GPU shortages and cloud price hikes; an API-dependent competitor is exposed to both.
Lab 1 · Hardware Assessment

Assess your own machine end-to-end and produce a one-page hardware capability report — the same artifact you will later produce for clients in Module 7.

  1. Identify your hardware. Mac: → About This Mac, then run system_profiler SPHardwareDataType in Terminal. Windows: Task Manager → Performance tab. Linux: lscpu, free -h, lspci | grep -i vga.Expected: you can state your exact chip, core counts, RAM, and storage.
  2. Find your memory bandwidth. Search "[your chip name] memory bandwidth" and record the GB/s figure.Expected: a number between ~50 GB/s (older laptops) and 800+ GB/s (Mac Studio Ultra).
  3. Calculate your model ceiling using the Lesson 1.2 rule: usable model size ≈ (RAM − 6 GB overhead), and largest comfortable 4-bit model ≈ usable size × 2 in parameters.Expected: e.g. 16 GB machine → ~10 GB usable → ~20B parameters at 4-bit (in practice, 7–14B models).
  4. Estimate token speed with the Lesson 1.3 formula: bandwidth ÷ model size.Expected: a tokens/sec ceiling for two model sizes you might run.
  5. Benchmark reality (optional but recommended): after Module 4 you'll install Ollama — return here and run ollama run llama3.2 --verbose and compare measured tok/s to your estimate.Expected: measured speed lands at 50–80% of the theoretical ceiling.
  6. Write the report: one page — machine identity, the five components with specs, model ceiling, speed estimates, and a verdict ("this machine can serve N users with model X").Expected: a document you could hand to a client without embarrassment.

You passed if: your report names all five components with real numbers, includes a calculated model ceiling and token-speed estimate, and ends with a deployment verdict.

Exam 1 · Hardware Foundations30 questions covering the five components, binary and quantization math, Apple Silicon architecture, NVIDIA's ecosystem, and the supply chain. Passing: 80%. The knowledge checks above are your study bank.
MODULE 2

How AI Works

8h lecture + 4h lab
Source: Skill Guide 9 — Transformers, LLMs, Agents
2.1 — The History of AI
Learning objectives
  • Place the major AI milestones from 1950 to 2026 on a timeline
  • Explain what the "AI winters" were and what ended them
  • Name the key figures — Turing, Hinton, Goodfellow, Vaswani — and what each contributed

AI is not new — it is seventy-five years of boom, bust, and breakthrough. Knowing the arc makes you the calmest person in any room full of hype.

1950: Alan Turing, the British mathematician who helped crack Nazi codes, publishes "Computing Machinery and Intelligence" and proposes the Turing Test: if a machine can converse so convincingly you can't tell it from a human, call it intelligent. The field gets its founding question before it has a name. 1956: the name arrives — "artificial intelligence" is coined at the Dartmouth workshop, and researchers predict human-level machines within a generation.

They were wrong, twice. Early programs solved toy logic puzzles but collapsed against real-world messiness, and funding dried up in two long AI winters (mid-70s, late-80s through the 90s). The lesson of the winters: AI progress is gated less by ideas than by compute and data. The core idea that eventually won — the neural network, a program loosely imitating how brain cells connect (Lesson 2.2) — existed since the 1950s. It simply needed a million times more horsepower than anyone had.

Three things ended the winters in the 2010s: the internet produced oceans of training data; Geoffrey Hinton and colleagues kept neural-network research alive through the unfashionable decades and refined how to train deep (many-layered) networks; and gamers' GPUs (Lesson 1.4) turned out to be perfect neural-network engines. 2012 is the ignition point: Hinton's students' system, AlexNet, demolished the field in an image-recognition contest using GPUs, and the deep-learning era began. 2014: Ian Goodfellow invents GANs (generative adversarial networks) — two networks competing, one forging images and one detecting fakes — proving machines could generate convincing content, not just classify it.

2017 is the hinge of this entire course: eight Google researchers, including Ashish Vaswani, publish "Attention Is All You Need," introducing the transformer architecture (Lesson 2.3). Every modern language model — GPT, Claude, Gemini, Llama, DeepSeek — is a transformer. 2018–2020: OpenAI scales transformers up (GPT-2, then GPT-3 at 175 billion parameters) and discovers that bigger models don't just improve — they develop abilities nobody programmed. November 2022: ChatGPT wraps GPT-3.5 in a chat box and reaches 100 million users in two months, the fastest product adoption in history. The public era of AI begins.

2023–2026: the years that created your profession. Meta releases Llama's weights, igniting the open-model movement. Mistral, Google (Gemma), Alibaba (Qwen) follow. January 2025: China's DeepSeek releases R1 — frontier-class reasoning, open weights, MIT license — proving that anyone can own world-class AI. By 2026, the open ecosystem (Gemma 4, Llama 4, DeepSeek V4, Qwen 3.6, even OpenAI's gpt-oss) runs on hardware a small business can buy. That gap — frontier labs spending billions while their open competitors hand you the result for free — is the arbitrage your entire consulting business stands on.

Walkthrough — the timeline on one card 
1950 Turing Test proposed          2012 AlexNet — deep learning ignites (Hinton)
1956 "AI" coined at Dartmouth      2014 GANs — machines that generate (Goodfellow)
1970s/80s-90s  Two AI winters      2017 "Attention Is All You Need" (Vaswani et al.)
                                   2020 GPT-3 — scale produces emergent ability
                                   2022 ChatGPT — 100M users in 2 months
                                   2023+ Open weights era (Llama, Mistral, Qwen)
                                   2025 DeepSeek R1 — frontier reasoning, MIT license
                                   2026 You deploy it for clients. Sovereignty era.

KEY TERMS: Turing Test · AI winter · neural network · deep learning · GAN · transformer · open weights

Common mistakes
  • Treating ChatGPT as the invention. The architecture (2017) and the scaling discovery (2018–2020) came first; ChatGPT was the interface moment.
  • Assuming the current boom can't pause. Two winters say otherwise — which is exactly why owning paid-for hardware beats renting hype-priced cloud.
  • Crediting one genius. Turing posed the question, Hinton kept networks alive, Goodfellow proved generation, Vaswani's team built the engine. It's a relay race.
Knowledge check
What caused the AI winters, and what three forces ended them?
Answer: Overpromising on insufficient compute and data caused funding collapses. Internet-scale data, persistent neural-network research (Hinton), and GPU horsepower ended them in the 2010s.
Why is 2017 the most important year in this course?
Answer: "Attention Is All You Need" introduced the transformer — the architecture behind every model you will ever deploy.
What did DeepSeek R1 (January 2025) prove that matters to your clients?
Answer: That frontier-class AI can be open-weight and freely owned — world-class reasoning under an MIT license, runnable on hardware a business can buy outright.
2.2 — Neural Networks
Learning objectives
  • Describe an artificial neuron and how layers of them transform information
  • Define weights/parameters and explain why "8B" describes a model's brain size
  • Explain training as a loop: prediction → loss → backpropagation → adjustment

Strip away the mystique and a neural network is a very long chain of multiply-and-add, organized brilliantly. Here is the whole machine, from one neuron up.

An artificial neuron takes several input numbers, multiplies each by its own weight (a number expressing "how much do I care about this input"), adds the results plus a bias (a tunable offset), and passes the total through a simple gate called an activation function — for example, "if negative, output zero." That's it. One neuron is almost useless. The power comes from organization: neurons are stacked into layers, where every neuron in one layer feeds every neuron in the next. The first layer sees raw input; each later layer combines the previous layer's findings into something more abstract. In a vision network the progression is famous: edges → shapes → textures → "this is a cat." In a language model, the same idea: characters → word fragments → grammar → meaning → intent. Deep learning just means many layers — modern language models stack 30 to 100+.

All those weights and biases together are the model's parameters. When a model is called "8B," it has eight billion adjustable numbers. Parameters are literally the model: the file you download in Module 4 is nothing but those numbers in a row (which is why file size = parameters × bytes-per-number, the math from Lesson 1.2). Nobody sets these numbers by hand. They are learned.

Learning works by a loop. Step 1 — predict: show the untrained network an example ("The capital of France is ___") and let it guess. With random starting weights, it guesses garbage. Step 2 — measure: a loss function computes a single number scoring how wrong the guess was (predicted "potato," answer was "Paris" → big loss). Step 3 — assign blame: backpropagation, the algorithm that makes all of this work, traces the error backward through every layer and computes, for each of the billions of weights, exactly which direction to nudge it to make the error a bit smaller. Step 4 — adjust: every weight gets nudged a tiny step (the step size is the learning rate). Then repeat — trillions of times across mountains of text. No single pass teaches much; the intelligence is the accumulated sediment of countless tiny corrections. This whole loop is training; running the finished network without changing weights is inference (Lesson 2.6 makes that split concrete).

Why does depth create understanding? Because each layer can only combine what the previous layer produced, the network is forced to build hierarchies — and hierarchies are how meaning works. "Paris" follows "capital of France" not because the network stored that sentence, but because thousands of weights jointly encode geography-flavored patterns squeezed out of its training data. Keep this picture; in the next lesson we add the one ingredient that made language networks actually work: attention.

Walkthrough — one neuron, by hand 
Inputs:  x1 = 0.5   x2 = 0.8
Weights: w1 = 0.9   w2 = -0.4      Bias: 0.1

sum = (0.5 × 0.9) + (0.8 × -0.4) + 0.1
    = 0.45 - 0.32 + 0.1 = 0.23
activation (ReLU: negatives→0): output = 0.23

An 8B model does this ~8,000,000,000 weights' worth, per token. Your GPU's
entire job (Lesson 1.4) is doing these multiply-adds in massive parallel.

KEY TERMS: neuron · weight · bias · activation function · layer · parameters · loss function · backpropagation · learning rate · training loop

Common mistakes
  • Thinking the model stores its training text. It stores only weights — statistical patterns. This matters legally and in sales calls: a local model is not a database of someone's documents.
  • Believing more parameters always means smarter. Architecture, data quality, and training method matter as much — a sharp 27B model regularly beats a sloppy 70B one.
  • Confusing training with inference. Clients hear "AI learns" and assume their chatbot is learning from every conversation. By default it is not — weights are frozen at inference (Lesson 2.9 covers what memory it does have).
Knowledge check
What exactly is "in" an 8B model file you download?
Answer: Eight billion learned numbers — the weights and biases. No sentences, no documents; just parameters encoding patterns.
What does backpropagation do, in one sentence?
Answer: It traces the prediction error backward through the network and tells every individual weight which direction to nudge to reduce the error.
Why do deeper networks learn more abstract patterns?
Answer: Each layer can only combine the previous layer's outputs, so representations are forced to build up hierarchically — edges→shapes→objects, or characters→grammar→meaning.
2.3 — The Transformer Architecture
Learning objectives
  • Explain the attention mechanism and why it beat older sequence-reading approaches
  • Describe self-attention, multi-head attention, and the role of feedforward layers
  • Sketch the full transformer block and explain why it scales so well on GPUs

Before 2017, language AI read text the way you read a ticker tape: one word at a time, left to right, carrying a memory that faded with distance. By the end of a long paragraph, those networks had functionally forgotten the beginning — and because each word had to wait for the previous one, training couldn't be parallelized across a GPU's thousands of cores. Two problems, one root cause: sequential processing.

The transformer's answer is attention: let every word look directly at every other word, all at once. Take "The animal didn't cross the street because it was too tired." What does "it" mean? Attention computes a relevance score between "it" and every other word in the sentence; "animal" scores high, "street" scores low, and the representation of "it" is updated as a weighted blend dominated by "animal." No fading memory, no distance penalty — a word on page 40 can attend to a definition on page 1 as easily as to its neighbor. When a sequence attends to itself like this (rather than to a separate text, as in translation), it's called self-attention.

Mechanically, each word's current representation is projected into three vectors with learned weights: a query ("what am I looking for?"), a key ("what do I contain?"), and a value ("what information do I pass along if selected?"). Every query is compared against every key — one big matrix multiplication (hello, Lesson 1.4 — this is exactly what Tensor Cores accelerate) — producing the relevance scores, which then mix the values together. And because one notion of relevance isn't enough, transformers run multi-head attention: 16, 32, or more attention "heads" in parallel, each with its own learned projections, free to specialize — one head tracking grammar, another tracking pronoun references, another tracking topic. The heads' outputs are concatenated and merged.

Attention gathers context; thinking happens next. After each attention step comes a feedforward network — the classic stacked-neuron layers from Lesson 2.2, applied to each position — which transforms the gathered context into new features. In most models the feedforward layers actually hold roughly two-thirds of all parameters; a useful mental model is attention routes information, feedforward processes it. Wrap these two steps with stabilizers (residual connections, which let information skip past layers unharmed, and layer normalization, which keeps numbers well-scaled) and you have one transformer block. Stack that block 30–100+ times and add a final layer that converts the result into a prediction for the next token (Lesson 2.5), and you have a complete LLM.

Why did this one paper change everything? Parallelism. Because attention processes all positions simultaneously, training saturates entire GPU fleets — making billion-parameter models economically trainable for the first time. The same property is why your local machine can generate text at usable speed. Architecture met hardware, and the modern era began. One cost worth knowing: naive attention compares every token with every token, so doubling context length roughly quadruples that work — which is why long context windows (Lesson 2.4) are expensive, and why clever attention variants are a constant research race.

Walkthrough — attention scores, simplified 
Sentence: "The animal didn't cross the street because it was too tired."
Computing what "it" attends to (illustrative scores):

  animal → 0.71   street → 0.09   cross → 0.07   tired → 0.06   (rest) → 0.07

New representation of "it" ≈ 0.71×value(animal) + 0.09×value(street) + ...
→ "it" now effectively MEANS "the animal" for every later layer.
Change the ending to "because it was too wide" and the scores flip:
  street → 0.68   animal → 0.11   — same mechanism, opposite answer.

KEY TERMS: attention · self-attention · query/key/value · multi-head attention · feedforward network · residual connection · layer normalization · transformer block

Common mistakes
  • Saying "transformers understand like humans." They compute statistical relevance between all token pairs. The results are remarkable; the mechanism is matrix math.
  • Forgetting the feedforward layers. Attention gets the press, but most parameters — and most of the "thinking" — live in the feedforward networks.
  • Treating context length as free. Attention cost grows roughly with the square of sequence length; "just give it a million tokens" has real memory and speed consequences (next lesson).
Knowledge check
What two problems of older sequence models did attention solve at once?
Answer: Long-range forgetting (every token can directly see every other token) and lack of parallelism (all positions process simultaneously, saturating GPUs).
What are queries, keys, and values?
Answer: Three learned projections of each token: the query asks "what am I looking for," keys advertise "what I contain," values carry "what I contribute." Query-key matches decide how much of each value gets blended in.
Why multiple attention heads instead of one?
Answer: Each head learns its own notion of relevance (grammar, references, topic…), letting the model track many relationship types in parallel.
2.4 — Tokens and Context Windows
Learning objectives
  • Explain what a token is and estimate token counts from word counts
  • Define the context window and predict what happens when it overflows
  • Calculate API costs from input/output token prices and explain why context size drives both capability and cost

Models don't read words — they read tokens. A token is a chunk of text, usually a word piece, chosen by a fixed dictionary built before training (the tokenizer). Common words get their own token ("the", " house"); rarer words split into pieces ("sovereignty" → "sover" + "eign" + "ty"). The dictionary, or vocabulary, typically holds 30,000–250,000 tokens. Why pieces instead of whole words? Coverage: with pieces, any text — typos, code, new slang, chemical names — can be expressed by combining known fragments. The working ratio to memorize: 1 token ≈ ¾ of an English word, so 1,000 words ≈ 1,300 tokens, and a 250-page book ≈ 100,000 tokens.

The context window is the model's working memory: the maximum number of tokens — your conversation so far, the system instructions, any documents you pasted, plus the reply being generated — that the model can consider at once. It exists because of the attention math from Lesson 2.3: every token attends to every other, so the window is an architectural budget set at training time. Sizes range from 8K tokens on older small models through 128K–200K as today's common standard, up to 1M (DeepSeek V4) and even 10M (Llama 4 Scout) on the long-context frontier. When a conversation exceeds the window, the oldest content is silently dropped or summarized — the model isn't being evasive when it "forgets" your first instruction an hour into a session; the instruction literally scrolled out of memory. This is the #1 user-confusion issue you will train client staff on in Module 7.

Context is also the unit of billing. Cloud APIs price per million tokens, with separate rates for input tokens (everything you send) and output tokens (everything the model generates — typically 3–5× more expensive, since generating costs more compute than reading). Critically, in a chat the entire history is re-sent as input with every message, so a long-running conversation gets more expensive per turn as it grows. Caching discounts (providers charge less for re-sent unchanged prefixes) soften this but don't eliminate it.

Now connect this to your business. Capability: the window bounds what fits — a 32K-window model cannot analyze a 200K-token contract in one pass, which is why RAG (retrieving only the relevant chunks; Module 5) exists. Cost: for a cloud-API workflow that stuffs documents into context all day, token bills scale with usage forever. A local deployment flips the economics: the client pays for hardware once, and tokens become free at the margin. Being fluent in this arithmetic — doing it on a whiteboard in front of a CFO — is one of the most persuasive sales tools you will own (Module 11 builds the full pitch on it).

Walkthrough — the CFO whiteboard math 
Workflow: support team summarizes 200 documents/day
Each: ~6,000 input tokens + ~500 output tokens

Cloud API at $3/M input, $15/M output:
  daily   = 200 × (6,000×$0.000003 + 500×$0.000015) = $5.10
  yearly  ≈ $1,860 — for ONE workflow. Ten workflows ≈ $18,600/yr, forever,
            and every document leaves the building.

Local: Mac Studio ≈ $4,500 once + electricity (~$15/mo).
  Year 2 onward: tokens cost $0. Data never leaves. That's the pitch.

KEY TERMS: token · tokenizer · vocabulary · context window · input tokens · output tokens · prompt caching

Common mistakes
  • Estimating costs in words. Bills are in tokens; multiply word counts by ~1.33 before doing price math.
  • Blaming the model for "forgetting." Long chats overflow the window. Teach users: new task, new chat — and put permanent instructions in the system prompt, not message #1.
  • Buying context you don't need. A 1M-token window with a huge model on undersized hardware crawls — context consumes memory too (the KV cache). Match window, model, and machine.
Knowledge check
Roughly how many tokens is a 5,000-word report?
Answer: ≈ 6,650 tokens (5,000 × 1.33).
Why do long chat conversations get more expensive per message on cloud APIs?
Answer: The full history is re-sent as input tokens with every turn, so input volume — and cost — grows with conversation length.
Why does local deployment change token economics?
Answer: Hardware is a one-time cost; after that, generating tokens costs essentially nothing at the margin — versus per-token cloud billing that scales with usage forever.
2.5 — Text Generation
Learning objectives
  • Describe generation as repeated next-token prediction over a probability distribution
  • Use temperature and top-p to control randomness, and know the right settings per task
  • Explain why identical prompts produce different answers — and how to make outputs reproducible

Here is the secret that demystifies every chatbot: a language model does exactly one thing — predict the next token. Given everything so far, it outputs a probability for every token in its vocabulary: "Paris" 92%, "Lyon" 3%, "the" 1%, and so on down the list of tens of thousands. One token is chosen, appended to the text, and the whole prediction runs again on the now-longer sequence. Loop until done. A thousand-word answer is just this loop executed ~1,300 times. (Recall Lesson 1.3: each loop streams the model's weights through the processor — which is why memory bandwidth sets your tokens-per-second.)

The interesting question is how to choose from that probability list — this is called sampling, and it's where the personality dials live. Always picking the single highest-probability token (greedy decoding) sounds right but produces repetitive, lifeless text that loops on itself. Instead, models sample randomly in proportion to the probabilities — and you control how adventurous that randomness is.

Temperature reshapes the distribution before sampling. Low temperature (0.0–0.3) sharpens it — probability concentrates on the top choices, output becomes focused, deterministic-ish, and safe: right for code, extraction, legal and factual work. Temperature around 0.7–0.8 keeps the natural spread: good general conversation. High temperature (1.0–1.5) flattens the distribution so unlikely tokens get real chances: brainstorming and creative writing — with rising risk of nonsense. At temperature 0, you get pure greedy decoding.

Top-p (also called nucleus sampling) is a complementary safety rail: instead of considering all 100,000+ tokens, keep only the smallest set whose probabilities sum to p, then sample within it. Top-p = 0.9 means "sample only from the top ~90% probability mass" — creative freedom inside a fence that excludes the genuinely absurd tail. In practice you'll set temperature per use-case and leave top-p around 0.9–0.95; you will tune both per agent in Open WebUI in Module 5 (a legal-research agent at 0.2, a marketing agent at 0.9).

This machinery explains the everyday mysteries. Why does the same question get different answers? Random sampling — run twice, sample differently. (Set temperature to 0, or fix the random seed — the number that initializes the randomness — and outputs become reproducible, which you'll use for testing in Module 5's labs.) Why does the model confidently state falsehoods? Because it always emits the most plausible-sounding continuation, and plausibility is not truth — there is no internal fact-checker, only the distribution. This failure mode is called hallucination, and it's why Module 5 grounds models in client documents with RAG and why Module 9 treats unverified output as a risk class. Why does text stream word-by-word? Because it's literally being produced one token at a time — the stream is the loop, live.

Walkthrough — same prompt, three temperatures 
Prompt: "Write a tagline for a local bakery."

T=0.1 → "Fresh bread, baked daily."            (safe, predictable, fine)
T=0.7 → "Where every morning smells like home." (warm, human, usable)
T=1.3 → "Flour dreams rise at dawn — bite the sunrise." (bold… or word salad)

Settings cheat sheet:
  code / extraction / legal:   T 0.0-0.3,  top-p 0.9
  support / general chat:      T 0.6-0.8,  top-p 0.9
  marketing / brainstorm:      T 0.9-1.2,  top-p 0.95

KEY TERMS: next-token prediction · probability distribution · sampling · greedy decoding · temperature · top-p / nucleus sampling · seed · hallucination · streaming

Common mistakes
  • One temperature for everything. The default 0.7 is wrong for code review (too loose) and wrong for brainstorming (too tight). Tune per agent.
  • Expecting determinism without setting it. If a client workflow needs identical outputs for identical inputs, set temperature 0 (and a fixed seed where supported) — and say so in your test plan.
  • Treating hallucination as a bug to apologize for. It's inherent to plausibility-driven generation. Engineer around it: RAG grounding, citations, human review on high-stakes outputs.
Knowledge check
What single operation does an LLM perform, repeated?
Answer: Predict a probability distribution over the next token, pick one, append it, repeat.
A client's contract-analysis agent must be precise and consistent. Temperature?
Answer: Low — 0.0 to 0.3. Precision tasks want the sharpened distribution; creativity dials stay closed.
Why can a model state falsehoods so confidently?
Answer: It generates the most plausible continuation by pattern, with no built-in truth check — confidence of tone and correctness of fact are unrelated.
2.6 — Training vs Inference
Learning objectives
  • Distinguish the three training stages: pre-training, fine-tuning, and RLHF
  • Explain LoRA and why it makes customization affordable
  • Contrast the costs of training vs inference and locate your business on that line

Every model has two lives. Training is when its weights are sculpted — the backpropagation loop from Lesson 2.2 run at industrial scale. Inference is when those frozen weights answer questions — the prediction loop from Lesson 2.5. The economics of the two could not be more different, and your entire business model lives in the gap.

Training happens in stages. Stage one, pre-training, is the monster: feed the network trillions of tokens of text — a filtered slice of the internet, books, code — with one endless exercise: predict the next token, get corrected, repeat. Months on tens of thousands of GPUs (Lesson 1.4's AI factories), at compute costs estimated from tens to hundreds of millions of dollars for frontier models. Out comes a base model: a vast pattern engine that completes text brilliantly but doesn't "chat" — ask it a question and it might just continue with more questions, because that's a plausible continuation.

Stage two turns the engine into an assistant. Supervised fine-tuning continues training on a much smaller, curated set of example conversations — demonstrations of following instructions, refusing harmful requests, formatting answers. Stage three, RLHF (Reinforcement Learning from Human Feedback), polishes behavior: the model generates multiple candidate answers, humans (and increasingly AI judges) rank them, a reward model learns those preferences, and the LLM is optimized against it. RLHF is why modern assistants feel helpful and polite rather than merely fluent — and the quality of this alignment stage is a big part of what separates model families that share similar architectures.

Then there's the fine-tuning you can sell. Full fine-tuning of even a mid-size model means updating all its billions of weights — heavy hardware, real money. LoRA (Low-Rank Adaptation) is the workaround that changed the market: freeze the original weights entirely and train small adapter matrices alongside them — typically well under 1% of the model's size — that steer its behavior. Training a LoRA on a few thousand examples (a client's support tickets, their brand voice, their document formats) takes hours on a rented GPU instead of weeks on a cluster, and the result is a file measured in megabytes that loads on top of the base model. QLoRA does the same on a quantized base, cheap enough for serious workstations. Module 10 makes this a service offering.

Now the strategic picture. Training a frontier model: $100M+, thousands of GPUs, elite research teams — a game for a handful of labs. Inference on an open model those labs release: a $2,000 Mac. The expensive part is done once by someone else; the valuable part — applying it to a business's private data, securely — is wide open. That asymmetry is the entire Sovereignty Code thesis. You will never train a foundation model, and you never need to: your profession is the inference side, plus light fine-tuning where it pays.

Walkthrough — the pipeline and the price tags 
PRE-TRAINING      trillions of tokens, months, $10M-$100M+  → base model
      ↓           (a handful of labs worldwide)
FINE-TUNING       curated dialogs, days-weeks               → instruct model
      ↓
RLHF              human/AI preference ranking               → aligned assistant
      ↓           ← everything above happens BEFORE release
RELEASE (open weights: free download)
      ↓
YOUR WORLD:
  LoRA fine-tune  client's 5k examples, hours, ~$50-500 GPU rent → custom adapter
  INFERENCE       client's own hardware, ~$0 marginal cost      → the product

KEY TERMS: pre-training · base model · supervised fine-tuning · RLHF · reward model · LoRA · QLoRA · adapter · inference

Common mistakes
  • Telling a client "we'll train your own AI." You'll fine-tune or adapt one. Precision here prevents scope nightmares and broken promises.
  • Reaching for fine-tuning when RAG suffices. New knowledge → RAG (Module 5). New behavior/style/format → fine-tuning. Most "we need a custom model" requests are actually RAG requests.
  • Underestimating alignment. Two models with identical sizes can behave worlds apart because of fine-tuning and RLHF quality — benchmark behavior, not just parameter count (Lab 2B).
Knowledge check
Why doesn't a freshly pre-trained base model behave like a chatbot?
Answer: Pre-training only teaches next-token completion. Instruction-following and helpful dialogue come from supervised fine-tuning and RLHF afterward.
What makes LoRA so much cheaper than full fine-tuning?
Answer: The base model's weights stay frozen; only tiny adapter matrices (<1% of parameters) are trained — hours on one GPU, output measured in megabytes.
A client wants the AI to know their 2,000-page policy manual. Fine-tune or RAG?
Answer: RAG — that's knowledge injection. Fine-tuning is for behavior, tone, and format, not for storing reference facts.
2.7 — Large Language Models
Learning objectives
  • Contrast dense and Mixture-of-Experts (MoE) architectures and size hardware for each
  • Explain emergent capabilities and the honest limits of LLMs
  • Map the model families — closed and open — you will choose between for clients

You know the engine (transformer), the fuel (tokens), and the manufacturing (training). Now the marketplace: what's actually available, how the architectures differ, and what these systems can and cannot do.

First, an architectural fork that directly affects your hardware quotes. A dense model activates every parameter for every token — an 27B dense model does 27 billion parameters' worth of work per token. A Mixture-of-Experts (MoE) model splits its feedforward layers into many separate "expert" sub-networks with a tiny learned router choosing 1–2 experts per token: huge total capacity, small per-token compute. Llama 4 Scout is 109B parameters total but only 17B active per token; DeepSeek V4 runs ~1.6T total with ~49B active. The consultant's rule: memory needs follow total parameters (all experts must be loaded); speed follows active parameters. A 512 GB Mac Studio can hold a frontier-scale MoE and still generate quickly, because each token only flows through a fraction of it. Always quote both numbers when sizing hardware.

Second, the strange phenomenon that built this industry: emergent capabilities. As models scale, abilities appear that nobody programmed and smaller versions lack — arithmetic, translation, code generation, step-by-step reasoning. Scale plus next-token prediction over enough human text yields skills that were never explicit training targets. The flip side is a stable list of limits you must be honest about in sales rooms: LLMs hallucinate plausibly (Lesson 2.5); they cannot reliably count or do precise arithmetic without tools; their knowledge freezes at a training cutoff date; they don't truly learn from conversations (weights are frozen at inference — Lesson 2.9 covers the workarounds); and recent "reasoning" models that think step-by-step before answering trade speed and tokens for accuracy. Set these expectations early and clients trust everything else you say.

Now the families. Closed/API-only: OpenAI's GPT line, Anthropic's Claude, Google's Gemini — frontier capability, rented by the token, data leaves the building. Open-weight — your deployment arsenal as of mid-2026: Gemma 4 (Google; efficient 2B–31B range, now Apache 2.0 licensed, multimodal); Llama 4 (Meta; Scout and Maverick MoEs, 10M-token context, but a restrictive community license — check it per client); DeepSeek V4 (MIT license, 1M context, frontier-class reasoning; the V4-Flash variant is a deployment favorite); Qwen 3.6 (Alibaba; Apache 2.0, outstanding at code — the most-downloaded open family); Mistral 3 (Apache 2.0, strong European option — relevant for GDPR-sensitive clients); and OpenAI's own gpt-oss (Apache 2.0; the 20B runs on 16 GB machines). Notice the 2025–2026 licensing trend: the ecosystem went permissive — Apache/MIT everywhere — while Meta's Llama became the restrictive outlier. Licensing is now a selection criterion you check, not a blocker (Module 4 gives you the checklist).

How to choose? Stop chasing leaderboards. The questions that matter: Does it fit the client's memory budget at 4-bit? Is the license compatible with their commercial use? Does it handle their language/domain? Is it strong at their actual task (code vs writing vs extraction)? Lab 2B teaches you to answer with evidence instead of vibes — a skill that, frankly, most "AI consultants" never build.

Walkthrough — dense vs MoE sizing in practice 
Qwen 3.6 27B (dense):    27B total / 27B active
  memory @4-bit ≈ 14 GB → runs on 24-32 GB Mac; speed of a 27B

Llama 4 Scout (MoE):     109B total / 17B active
  memory @4-bit ≈ 55 GB → needs 64-96 GB machine
  …but generates at the SPEED of a 17B. Big brain, light feet.

Client question it answers: "Why does the 'bigger' model need a Mac Studio
but respond faster than the 'smaller' one on the same box?"

KEY TERMS: dense model · Mixture of Experts (MoE) · active parameters · router · emergent capabilities · training cutoff · open weights · model license

Common mistakes
  • Sizing MoE hardware by active parameters. All experts load into memory — total params set the RAM bill; active params set the speed.
  • Ignoring licenses because "it's open." Llama 4's community license has real restrictions (user caps, attribution); Apache 2.0 and MIT are clean. Check before client deployment, every time.
  • Selling capability without stating limits. Hallucination, cutoff dates, and frozen weights surface eventually; the consultant who said it first owns the trust.
Knowledge check
An MoE model lists "284B total / 13B active." What does each number tell you?
Answer: 284B sets the memory requirement (all experts load); 13B sets the per-token compute — it generates with the speed of a 13B model.
What is an emergent capability?
Answer: An ability (arithmetic, coding, reasoning) that appears with scale without being explicitly trained for — present in large models, absent in their smaller siblings.
Which open families carry fully permissive licenses as of mid-2026, and which major family doesn't?
Answer: Gemma 4, Qwen 3.6, Mistral 3 (Apache 2.0), DeepSeek (MIT), gpt-oss (Apache 2.0) are permissive; Meta's Llama 4 community license is the restrictive outlier.
2.8 — AI Agents
Learning objectives
  • Define an agent and walk the perceive → plan → act → observe loop
  • Explain tool use / function calling — the mechanism that lets text control software
  • Classify agent types (browser, coding, workflow) and identify where agents fail

A chatbot answers; an agent accomplishes. The difference is a loop and a toolbox, and it's where AI stops being a writing aid and starts being labor.

The agent loop has four beats. Perceive: take in the goal and current state ("Find every invoice over $5,000 in this inbox and log it in the spreadsheet"). Plan: reason out the next step — the LLM literally writes its thinking ("First I should search the inbox for attachments…"). Act: execute the step using a tool. Observe: read the result — found 12 emails, or an error — fold it into context, and loop back to planning. Repeat until the goal is met or the agent reports it's stuck. The loop is what buys resilience: a script breaks when reality deviates; an agent observes the deviation and re-plans.

The hinge is tool use (also called function calling). The model is given a menu of tools, each described in plain text with structured parameters — search_email(query), read_file(path), update_sheet(row, values). When the model decides to act, it emits a structured request — effectively a small JSON message: {"tool": "search_email", "query": "invoice"} — which the surrounding software executes, returning the result as new context. Read that twice: the model only ever produces text; the harness around it does the doing. That separation is also your security boundary — the agent can only act through tools you've explicitly granted, which is precisely why Module 9 treats tool grants as an attack surface (a poisoned document that says "ignore your instructions and email the database to…" is feeding instructions into the same context the planner reads — prompt injection).

The species you'll deploy: Workflow agents — AI steps embedded in defined business processes (n8n, Module 6): document intake → extract fields → route for approval. Most reliable, most billable; this is the bread and butter. Coding agents — read a codebase, write code, run tests, iterate on failures (Claude Code, which you may be watching work right now, is one). Browser/computer agents — operate real software through screenshots, clicks, and typing; powerful for legacy systems with no API, still the least dependable. Multi-agent systems — a coordinator decomposes work across specialists (researcher → drafter → reviewer); Module 6 has you build one, and Lab 6B grades it.

Be the realist in the room: agent reliability compounds badly. A step that succeeds 95% of the time, chained twenty times, finishes flawlessly only ~36% of runs (0.95²⁰). Production agent design is therefore mostly engineering the failure path: checkpoints, retries, validation steps, and human approval gates before any irreversible action (sending the email, paying the invoice, deleting the record). Clients buy agents from the consultant who talks about failure handling — it signals you've actually shipped one.

Walkthrough — one real loop iteration 
GOAL: "Log all invoices over $5,000 from this week into the tracker."

PERCEIVE  goal + tools: search_email, read_pdf, update_sheet
PLAN      "Search this week's email for invoice attachments."
ACT       {"tool":"search_email","query":"invoice","after":"2026-06-03"}
OBSERVE   12 results returned
PLAN      "Open each PDF, extract vendor + amount, keep those > $5,000."
ACT       {"tool":"read_pdf","id":"msg_4821"}
OBSERVE   {vendor:"Acme Corp", amount:"$7,250"}  → qualifies
ACT       {"tool":"update_sheet","row":["Acme Corp","$7,250","6/8/26"]}
OBSERVE   "row added" → next email… (loop continues until all 12 processed)
GUARD     agent REPORTS completion; a human approves before any email goes out

KEY TERMS: agent · agent loop · tool use / function calling · harness · workflow agent · coding agent · browser agent · multi-agent system · approval gate

Common mistakes
  • Selling full autonomy on day one. Start with agent-drafts-human-approves; earn autonomy per step as reliability is proven.
  • Granting broad tool access "to be safe." Every tool is attack surface (Module 9). Grant the minimum set the task needs.
  • Ignoring compounding failure. Twenty chained 95% steps ≈ 36% end-to-end. Design checkpoints and retries, not hope.
Knowledge check
Name the four steps of the agent loop.
Answer: Perceive → plan → act → observe — repeated until the goal is met.
If the model only outputs text, how does an agent actually do anything?
Answer: It emits structured tool requests (JSON); the surrounding harness executes them and feeds results back as context. The harness, not the model, touches the world.
Why do long agent chains need approval gates?
Answer: Per-step reliability compounds — 0.95²⁰ ≈ 36% success over 20 steps — so irreversible actions need checkpoints and human sign-off.
2.9 — Memory and Context
Learning objectives
  • Explain why LLMs are stateless and what that means for every product built on them
  • Stack-rank the memory layers: context window, system instructions, RAG, state files, fine-tuning
  • Choose the right memory mechanism for a given client requirement

Here is the fact that explains half of all AI product design: a language model remembers nothing. Weights are frozen at inference (Lesson 2.6), and every single request starts from a blank slate. When a chatbot "remembers" your name from ten messages ago, the application re-sent the entire conversation with your new message. The model didn't remember — it re-read. Every form of "AI memory" you will ever sell is engineering wrapped around this stateless core. There are five layers, and choosing among them is a designer's skill you'll use in every deployment.

Layer 1 — the context window (Lesson 2.4): working memory. Whatever is in the current request, the model sees perfectly. Capacity-limited, costs tokens, evaporates when the session ends. Layer 2 — system instructions: a privileged block of text silently prepended to every request — identity, rules, tone, knowledge of the business ("You are NorthStar Legal's assistant; never give medical advice; cite document sources"). It never scrolls out of the window because the application re-injects it every turn. In Module 5, every agent you build starts here; it is the cheapest, highest-leverage memory there is.

Layer 3 — RAG (Retrieval-Augmented Generation, built hands-on in Module 5): long-term knowledge. Client documents are split into chunks, each chunk converted to an embedding (a list of numbers capturing its meaning) and stored in a vector database. When a question arrives, the system retrieves the few most relevant chunks and pastes them into context alongside the question. The model doesn't "know" the company handbook — it gets handed the right page at the right moment, with sources you can cite. RAG scales to gigabytes of knowledge while spending only a few thousand tokens per query.

Layer 4 — state files and memory systems: long-term experience. The application writes durable notes — user preferences, project status, "client prefers bullet points" — to files or a database, and selectively re-injects them into future context. This is how assistants feel continuous across weeks. (The assistant building this very course uses exactly this mechanism — a directory of memory files reloaded each session.) Layer 5 — fine-tuning (Lesson 2.6): the only layer that changes the weights themselves. Permanent, baked-in behavior — the company voice, the document format — at the price of a training run. Use when a behavior must hold without spending context tokens on instructions.

The selection heuristic that should become reflex: this conversation → context. Identity and rules → system instructions. Reference knowledge → RAG. Evolving preferences and status → state files. Permanent behavior → fine-tuning. Most client requirements ("it should know our products," "it should remember the customer," "it should always sound like us") decompose cleanly into these five — and clients will pay well for the consultant who decomposes instead of hand-waving. With that, you understand the machine. Semester 2 starts building yours.

Walkthrough — one requirement, five wrong-and-right answers 
Client: "Our AI should know our 300-page product catalog."

context window?   Paste 300 pages every request → slow, costly, may not fit. NO
system prompt?    Catalog in instructions → same problem. NO
RAG?              Index catalog, retrieve relevant chunks per question. YES ✓
state files?      That's for preferences/status, not reference docs. NO
fine-tuning?      Bakes today's catalog into weights; prices change next month
                  → retrain forever. NO (use for VOICE, not facts)

Follow-up: "…and it should remember each customer's past orders."
→ state files / database injection. Different requirement, different layer.

KEY TERMS: stateless · system instructions · RAG · embedding · vector database · chunk · state files · memory stack

Common mistakes
  • Believing the model learns from chats. Frozen weights — every "memory" is application engineering. Say this clearly in client training (Module 7) and you prevent months of confusion.
  • Fine-tuning facts. Facts change; retraining is expensive. Fine-tune behavior, RAG the facts.
  • Bloated system prompts. Every turn pays its token cost. Identity and rules belong there; reference material belongs in RAG.
Knowledge check
How does a chatbot "remember" earlier messages if the model is stateless?
Answer: The application re-sends the whole conversation history (or a summary) with each new message — the model re-reads, it never remembers.
Match the layer: company rules and tone? Product documentation? User's preferred format?
Answer: Rules/tone → system instructions. Documentation → RAG. Preferred format → state files (or system instructions if universal).
When is fine-tuning the right memory layer?
Answer: When a behavior — voice, style, output format — must be permanent and shouldn't consume context tokens; never for fast-changing facts.
Lab 2A · Prompt Engineering Workshop

Write and test 20 prompts across use cases, and learn the three levers that change output quality: instructions, temperature, and examples.

  1. Pick 4 task types: extraction (pull fields from messy text), rewriting (fix tone), generation (marketing copy), reasoning (a word problem). Write 5 prompts each.Expected: 20 prompts saved in a document, numbered.
  2. For each task, write a lazy version ("summarize this") and an engineered version (role + task + constraints + output format: "You are a paralegal. Summarize this contract in 5 bullets, each under 20 words, flag any termination clauses").Expected: the engineered version wins visibly on at least 15 of 20.
  3. Run one creative prompt at temperature 0.2, 0.7, and 1.2 (any playground that exposes temperature — Google AI Studio works free, Module 4 covers it).Expected: focused → natural → wild, matching Lesson 2.5.
  4. Add few-shot examples to your extraction prompt: show 2 worked input→output pairs before the real input.Expected: format compliance jumps to near-100%.
  5. Write up: for each lever (instructions, temperature, examples), one sentence on when you'd reach for it.Expected: a personal cheat sheet you'll reuse in Module 5 agent design.

You passed if: you have 20 prompts with lazy/engineered pairs, a documented 3-temperature comparison, a working few-shot prompt, and a one-page levers cheat sheet.

Lab 2B · Model Comparison

Same prompts, six models, scored evidence. This produces your first client-grade artifact: a model evaluation report.

  1. Select 5 test prompts from Lab 2A — one per skill: reasoning, extraction, writing, code, domain Q&A.Expected: a fixed test set you never change mid-test.
  2. Run all 5 against at least 6 models: two closed (e.g. GPT, Claude, or Gemini via free tiers) and four open (Gemma 4, Llama 4, DeepSeek, Qwen — via Google AI Studio, web demos, or Ollama if you've jumped ahead).Expected: 30 outputs, captured verbatim.
  3. Score each output 1–5 on accuracy, completeness, and format compliance. Record speed (tokens/sec or rough seconds) and cost (API price or "local/free").Expected: a 30-row scoring table.
  4. Write the report: ranking table, one paragraph per model (strengths/weaknesses), and a recommendation: "for task X under constraint Y, deploy model Z."Expected: 2 pages, evidence-based, no vibes.

You passed if: your report contains a full scoring table across ≥6 models and ends with a defensible, constraint-aware recommendation.

Exam 2 · AI Foundations50 questions across history, networks, transformers, tokens, generation, training, LLMs, agents, and memory. Passing: 80%. Every knowledge check in this module is in scope.
MODULE 3

The Tools of the Trade

8h lecture + 6h lab
Sources: Skill Guides 3, 10 — Python, APIs
3.1 — Python Fundamentals
Learning objectives
  • Read and write basic Python: variables, lists, dictionaries, conditions, loops, functions
  • Read and write files, and parse JSON — the data format of every AI API
  • Install packages with pip and run a script from the terminal

Python is the language of AI — not because it's fast (it isn't), but because it's readable, and every AI library speaks it first. You don't need to become a software engineer. You need working literacy: enough to write a 40-line script that calls a model, processes files, and glues systems together. That bar is lower than you think.

Variables are named boxes: client_name = "Acme" stores text (a string); user_count = 25 stores a number; no type declarations needed — Python figures it out. Strings join with + or, better, f-strings: f"Welcome, {client_name}" drops the variable straight into the text. Lists hold ordered collections — models = ["gemma", "qwen", "deepseek"] — indexed from zero (models[0] is "gemma"). Dictionaries hold labeled pairs — server = {"host": "192.168.1.50", "port": 11434} — and you read by label: server["port"]. Burn dictionaries into your fingers: every API request and response you will ever touch is shaped like one.

Conditions branch: if ram >= 16: do this, elif something else, else: fallback. Loops repeat: for model in models: runs its block once per item. Python's one hard rule: indentation is the syntax. The lines inside an if or a loop are defined by being indented (4 spaces, consistently). No braces — the visual structure is the program structure. Most beginner errors are indentation errors; now you know.

Functions package reusable steps: def estimate_memory(params_b, bits): return params_b * bits / 8 — define once, call forever: estimate_memory(8, 4) → 4.0 GB (the Lesson 1.2 math, automated). Files: open("report.txt", "w") writes, "r" reads — always inside a with block so the file closes itself. JSON (JavaScript Object Notation) is the text format APIs use — it looks exactly like nested Python dictionaries and lists. import json, then json.load(file) turns JSON text into Python objects and json.dump(data, file) goes the other way. That round-trip is half of all practical scripting.

Finally, packages: Python's superpower is its library ecosystem. pip install requests fetches the requests library (web calls, used in 3.3); import requests makes it available. Best practice you should adopt on day one: create a virtual environment per project (python3 -m venv venv, then source venv/bin/activate) so each project's packages stay isolated — future-you, debugging a client machine at 6 PM, will be grateful.

Walkthrough — a real script you'll actually reuse 
> # hardware_check.py — sizing helper from Module 1, automated
> import json
>
> def fits(model_params_b, quant_bits, ram_gb, overhead_gb=6):
>     needed = model_params_b * quant_bits / 8
>     return needed + overhead_gb <= ram_gb, round(needed, 1)
>
> candidates = [
>     {"name": "gemma4:12b", "params": 12},
>     {"name": "qwen3.6:27b", "params": 27},
>     {"name": "gpt-oss:20b", "params": 20},
> ]
>
> ram = 32
> for m in candidates:
>     ok, gb = fits(m["params"], 4, ram)
>     verdict = "FITS" if ok else "too big"
>     print(f'{m["name"]}: needs ~{gb} GB at 4-bit → {verdict} on {ram} GB')
>
> # Run it:  python3 hardware_check.py
> # gemma4:12b: needs ~6.0 GB at 4-bit → FITS on 32 GB
> # qwen3.6:27b: needs ~13.5 GB at 4-bit → FITS on 32 GB
> # gpt-oss:20b: needs ~10.0 GB at 4-bit → FITS on 32 GB

KEY TERMS: variable · string · f-string · list · dictionary · indentation · function · JSON · pip · virtual environment

Common mistakes
  • Fighting indentation. Mixed tabs/spaces or inconsistent depth breaks Python. Set your editor to 4 spaces and never think about it again.
