  • Skipping virtual environments. Installing everything globally works until two projects need different versions of the same package — then nothing works.
  • Memorizing instead of reading. Working consultants look syntax up constantly (and AI writes most boilerplate now). Your job is to read code confidently and know what to ask for.
Knowledge check
What Python structure mirrors a JSON API response?
Answer: The dictionary (often nested with lists) — JSON objects map directly to dicts, JSON arrays to lists.
What defines a code block in Python?
Answer: Indentation — consistently indented lines under an if/for/def form the block. There are no braces.
Why use a virtual environment per project?
Answer: It isolates each project's installed packages so version conflicts between projects can't break your tooling.
3.2 — The Command Line
Learning objectives
  • Navigate and manipulate files from the terminal with the core commands
  • Set and use environment variables — where API keys live
  • Connect to a remote machine with SSH and run a basic shell script

The terminal is where deployments actually happen. Every server you'll ever configure — the client's Mac Mini in a closet, a Linux box in a rack — is administered by typed commands, often remotely with no screen attached. Fluency here is what separates "AI enthusiast" from "person you pay to install infrastructure." The good news: 15 commands cover 95% of the job.

Orientation and movement. The filesystem is a tree of folders ("directories"). pwd prints where you are; ls lists what's here (ls -la shows everything, with sizes and permissions); cd projects enters a folder, cd .. goes up one, cd ~ jumps home. Paths starting with / are absolute (from the root); others are relative (from here). Manipulation. mkdir deploy makes a folder; cp source dest copies; mv moves or renames; cat file prints a file; rm file deletes — permanently, no trash can, so cultivate a half-second pause before every rm. grep "error" logfile searches text — your best friend when a client says "it stopped working."

Environment variables are named values the shell holds for programs to read — and they are the professional answer to "where do I put API keys." export GHL_API_KEY="pit-abc123" sets one for this session; programs read it (Python: os.environ["GHL_API_KEY"]) without the key ever appearing in code. Add the export line to your shell profile (~/.zshrc on Mac) to make it permanent. The rule, which Module 9 elevates to law: secrets live in environment variables or secret managers — never in code, never in Git.

SSH (Secure Shell) is remote control: ssh admin@192.168.1.50 opens an encrypted terminal session on another machine — suddenly "the server in the client's office" is administrable from your desk. First-class habit: use SSH keys instead of passwords (ssh-keygen creates a key pair; ssh-copy-id installs your public key on the server) — stronger and script-friendly. Its sibling scp file admin@server:/path copies files across. Every Phase 2+ deployment in this course assumes SSH.

Shell scripts bundle commands into a file (backup.sh) that runs as one unit — the backbone of the backup automation you'll build in Module 8. Two power moves complete your kit: the pipe | chains commands (cat access.log | grep "error" | wc -l — count error lines), and history recalls what you typed. Speed comes from Tab (autocomplete) and ↑ (last command); use both relentlessly.

Walkthrough — your first ten minutes on a client server 
ssh admin@192.168.1.50          # connect (key-based, no password prompt)
pwd && ls -la                   # where am I, what's here
df -h                           # disk space — is the SSD filling up?
free -h                         # RAM in use (Linux; macOS: vm_stat)
ps aux | grep ollama            # is the model engine running?
cat ~/.zshrc | grep export      # what environment variables are configured?
grep -i "error" ~/server.log | tail -20   # last 20 errors
exit                            # done — session closes

Ten commands, no GUI, and you know the machine's health. This exact sequence opens Module 8's monitoring lab.

KEY TERMS: terminal / shell · directory · absolute vs relative path · environment variable · shell profile · SSH · SSH key · scp · pipe · shell script

Common mistakes
  • Running half-understood commands as root. sudo means "do this with absolute power." Read before you paste — especially anything containing rm -rf.
  • Putting API keys in code anyway. The leak always happens at the worst time (usually via Git, Lesson 3.6). Environment variables, always.
  • Password-based SSH on client servers. Keys are both more secure and automatable; passwords get shared in Slack and never rotated.
Knowledge check
Where should an API key live, and name one place it must never appear?
Answer: In an environment variable (or secret manager). Never in source code or a Git repository.
What does ssh admin@192.168.1.50 give you?
Answer: An encrypted remote terminal on that machine — full command-line control as if you were sitting at it.
What does cat app.log | grep "timeout" | wc -l do?
Answer: Prints the log, filters to lines containing "timeout," and counts them — i.e., "how many timeouts have we had?"
3.3 — APIs and API Keys
Learning objectives
  • Explain REST: resources, HTTP methods, status codes, and JSON bodies
  • Authenticate a request with an API key in a header
  • Make a real AI API call with curl and with Python, and handle rate limits

An API (Application Programming Interface) is a service's front door for software: a published set of URLs that accept structured requests and return structured answers. Everything you integrate in this course — models, CRMs, payment systems — happens through APIs. Master the anatomy of a single request and you've mastered them all, because nearly every modern API follows the same style: REST.

REST organizes a service into resources reachable by URL (called endpoints): /v1/contacts, /v1/chat/completions. What you do to a resource is expressed by the HTTP method: GET reads, POST creates (and, by convention, runs actions like "generate a chat reply"), PUT/PATCH update, DELETE removes. Data travels as JSON (Lesson 3.1) in the request body; metadata travels in headers — including the one that matters most: authentication.

An API key is a long secret string identifying and authorizing you. It typically rides in a header — commonly Authorization: Bearer sk-abc123…, though providers vary (Anthropic uses x-api-key; you check each provider's docs once and move on). Treat keys like signed blank checks: anyone holding the key is you, spends as you, accesses data as you. Storage discipline came in 3.2 (environment variables); add rotation (regenerate periodically and on any suspicion) and scoping (use keys with the narrowest permissions offered).

Every response carries a status code — learn the handful that matter: 200 success · 400 your request is malformed · 401 bad/missing key · 403 key valid but not allowed · 404 no such endpoint · 429 slow down (rate limited) · 500-series the server itself broke. That last pair deserves strategy, not anger: APIs enforce rate limits (N requests or tokens per minute), and production code handles 429/500 with exponential backoff — wait 1s, retry; wait 2s, then 4s, then 8s. You will write exactly this in Lab 3A, and n8n gives it to you as a checkbox in Module 6.

Why this lesson sits at the center of the course: the AI industry standardized — informally but near-universally — on one request shape, the OpenAI-compatible chat completions format: POST to /v1/chat/completions with a JSON body containing a model name and a list of role-tagged messages (system / user / assistant — recognize Layer 2 of Lesson 2.9 in that system role). OpenAI serves it, DeepSeek serves it, and — decisively for you — Ollama and vLLM serve it locally. Learn the shape once and you can swap a $20-per-million-token cloud model for a $0 local one by changing a URL. That URL swap is, in one line, the technical core of the sovereignty pitch.

Walkthrough — the same call, cloud and local 
# Cloud (DeepSeek) — key from environment, never typed inline
curl https://api.deepseek.com/v1/chat/completions \
  -H "Content-Type: application/json" \
  -H "Authorization: Bearer $DEEPSEEK_API_KEY" \
  -d '{
    "model": "deepseek-chat",
    "messages": [
      {"role": "system", "content": "You are a concise assistant."},
      {"role": "user", "content": "Explain RAG in one sentence."}
    ]
  }'

# Local (Ollama, Module 4) — same shape, new URL, no key, no bill
curl http://localhost:11434/v1/chat/completions \
  -H "Content-Type: application/json" \
  -d '{"model": "gemma4:12b", "messages": [
        {"role": "user", "content": "Explain RAG in one sentence."}]}'

# Response (both): {"choices":[{"message":{"role":"assistant",
#   "content":"RAG retrieves relevant documents and..."}}], "usage":{...}}

KEY TERMS: API · REST · endpoint · HTTP method (GET/POST/PUT/DELETE) · header · API key · status code · rate limit · exponential backoff · OpenAI-compatible API

Common mistakes
  • Pasting keys into code, chats, or screenshots. One leaked key = someone else's usage on your bill. Environment variables + rotation.
  • Retrying 429s instantly in a tight loop. You'll dig the hole deeper and can get banned. Exponential backoff is three lines of code; write them.
  • Reading error text but not status codes. The code tells you whose fault it is: 4xx is yours, 5xx is theirs. Debug accordingly.
Knowledge check
You get a 401, then after fixing it a 429. What does each mean and what do you do?
Answer: 401 = authentication failed (check the key/header). 429 = rate limited (back off exponentially and retry, then request a higher limit if chronic).
Why is the OpenAI-compatible format strategically important to local AI?
Answer: Ollama and vLLM serve the same request shape, so any tool built for cloud APIs can be pointed at local hardware by changing the base URL — instant sovereignty migration path.
What are the three roles in a chat completions message list?
Answer: system (standing instructions), user (the human's input), assistant (the model's replies — included as history in later turns).
3.4 — Webhooks
Learning objectives
  • Contrast polling (pull) with webhooks (push) and pick the right one per integration
  • Describe a webhook endpoint and what arrives in its payload
  • Test and secure webhooks: capture tools, signatures, retries, and idempotency

APIs as you just learned them are pull: you ask, they answer. But how do you find out the moment a customer submits a form? Asking every ten seconds (polling) is wasteful — thousands of "anything new?" calls returning "no" — and still up to ten seconds late. The inversion is the webhook: you give the service a URL, and when the event happens, the service immediately POSTs the details to you. Pull becomes push. You stop asking; you get told.

Mechanically, a webhook is just an HTTP request flowing the other direction — everything from 3.3 still applies, with the roles reversed: now you host the endpoint. A webhook endpoint is a URL on your system that accepts POSTs, reads the JSON payload (event type, timestamp, and the data — the new contact, the paid invoice), responds 200 quickly, and then does its work. "Your system" sounds heavy, but in this course it's nearly free: n8n's first-class trigger is a webhook URL (Module 6) — you paste that URL into your CRM or form builder, and events start flowing into your automations. This single pattern — event → webhook → n8n → AI processing → action — is the skeleton of most workflows you will ever sell.

Testing has a standard toolkit. Capture services (webhook.site and similar) hand you a temporary URL that displays every request it receives — perfect for seeing exactly what a CRM actually sends before you build against it. For local development there's a wrinkle: your laptop isn't reachable from the internet, so tunnel tools (ngrok and similar) create a temporary public URL that forwards to your machine. And every sane provider includes a "send test event" button — use it before wiring anything real.

Securing and hardening separates hobbyists from professionals, because an open endpoint is an open door: anyone who finds the URL can POST fake events. Defense one: signature verification — providers sign each delivery with a shared secret (an HMAC header); your endpoint recomputes the signature and rejects mismatches, proving the event is genuine. Defense two: expect retries — if your endpoint is down, providers re-deliver, sometimes repeatedly — so process idempotently: track event IDs you've handled and skip duplicates, or the client gets three welcome emails. Defense three: respond fast — acknowledge with 200 immediately and do slow work (AI calls!) afterward, because providers time out slow endpoints and mark them failed. n8n's "respond immediately" setting exists for exactly this.

Walkthrough — what actually arrives 
# GoHighLevel fires on form submission → POST to your n8n URL:
POST https://n8n.client.com/webhook/lead-intake
Content-Type: application/json
X-Signature: sha256=9f2b4c...        ← verify this before trusting anything

{
  "event": "contact.created",
  "id": "evt_8d3kq",                  ← store: skip if seen before (idempotency)
  "timestamp": "2026-06-10T14:32:08Z",
  "contact": {
    "name": "Maria Lopez",
    "email": "maria@example.com",
    "message": "Need a quote for 20-user AI setup"
  }
}

# Your flow: verify signature → 200 OK immediately →
#   AI qualifies the lead (Module 6) → CRM updated → sales rep notified.
# Elapsed: ~2 seconds from form click. Polling would average 5+ min delays.

KEY TERMS: polling · webhook · push vs pull · endpoint · payload · signature verification (HMAC) · idempotency · tunnel (ngrok) · event-driven architecture

Common mistakes
  • Trusting unsigned webhooks. An unverified endpoint will eventually process forged events. Verify signatures (or at minimum use an unguessable URL + IP allowlist).
  • Doing heavy work before responding. AI calls take seconds; webhook timeouts are short. Acknowledge first, process after.
  • Ignoring duplicate deliveries. Retries are a feature, not a bug — design for "this event may arrive twice" from day one.
Knowledge check
Polling vs webhook — what's the core difference?
Answer: Polling: you repeatedly ask the service for changes (pull). Webhook: the service immediately POSTs events to your URL when they happen (push) — faster and far cheaper.
Why must webhook processing be idempotent?
Answer: Providers retry failed/slow deliveries, so the same event can arrive more than once; tracking event IDs prevents duplicate side effects.
What does signature verification prove?
Answer: That the payload genuinely came from the provider (it was signed with the shared secret) and wasn't forged or tampered with.
3.5 — OAuth and MCP
Learning objectives
  • Walk through the OAuth authorization flow and contrast it with API keys
  • Explain tokens: access tokens, refresh tokens, expiry, and scopes
  • Describe MCP (Model Context Protocol) and why it standardizes AI-to-tool connections

API keys (3.3) authenticate you to a service. But integration constantly needs something subtler: your application acting on someone else's account — reading a client's calendar, posting to their CRM — without ever learning their password. That's OAuth, the authorization standard behind every "Sign in with Google" and "Connect your account" button you've ever clicked.

The flow has four actors: the user (your client), the provider (Google, GoHighLevel), your application, and the prize — a token. The dance: (1) your app sends the user to the provider's consent screen, declaring which permissions — scopes — it wants ("read calendar," "manage contacts"); (2) the user logs in at the provider (your app never sees the password) and approves; (3) the provider redirects back with a short-lived authorization code; (4) your app exchanges that code (plus its own credentials) for an access token. The token is the key it actually uses — sent in the same Authorization: Bearer header you already know. Access tokens expire quickly (often an hour), so the provider also issues a refresh token that quietly obtains new access tokens without re-asking the user. Compare the trust model with API keys: scoped instead of all-powerful, expiring instead of eternal, revocable per-app instead of all-or-nothing. That's why serious platforms push integrations toward OAuth — and why GoHighLevel's "Private Integration Tokens," which you'll use in Module 6, are a deliberate middle ground: key-simple, but scoped like OAuth.

Now the second half: MCP — Model Context Protocol. The problem it solves is combinatorial: every AI app used to need custom glue for every tool (N apps × M tools = N×M integrations, each bespoke). MCP, introduced by Anthropic in 2024 and since adopted across the industry, standardizes the connection. An MCP server is a small program that fronts some capability — a database, a CRM, a filesystem — and describes its tools in a standard, machine-readable form. An MCP client (Claude, an IDE, your Open WebUI deployment) connects, asks "what tools do you offer?", and the model can then invoke them through the standard tool-use mechanism you learned in Lesson 2.8. Write one MCP server for a system, and every MCP-speaking AI can use it: N+M instead of N×M. Authentication for remote MCP servers? OAuth — the two halves of this lesson are one design.

For your practice, MCP is leverage: instead of hand-writing API glue per client per tool, you increasingly install or configure existing MCP servers (there are registries full of them) and grant the client's AI exactly the tool set their workflows need. The security instincts transfer wholesale: scope minimally, treat every granted tool as attack surface (Lesson 2.8's prompt-injection warning, formalized in Module 9), and audit what's connected.

Walkthrough — the OAuth dance, annotated 
1. App → user's browser → provider:
   https://provider.com/oauth/authorize?client_id=APP123
     &scope=contacts.read+contacts.write&redirect_uri=https://app.com/callback
2. User logs in AT THE PROVIDER, sees: "App requests: read & write contacts
   [Approve] [Deny]" → approves
3. Provider → https://app.com/callback?code=AUTH_xyz   (short-lived code)
4. App (server-side) POSTs code + its client_secret → receives:
   { "access_token": "ya29.a0...",  ← use in Bearer header, expires in 3600s
     "refresh_token": "1//0e...",   ← store securely, mint new access tokens
     "scope": "contacts.read contacts.write" }

Password seen by app: never. Permissions: only the two scopes. Revocable: yes.

KEY TERMS: OAuth · scope · consent screen · authorization code · access token · refresh token · Bearer header · MCP · MCP server / client

Common mistakes
  • Storing refresh tokens casually. A refresh token is long-lived account access — encrypt it, secret-manage it, treat it like a password.
  • Requesting every scope "while we're at it." Broad scopes scare users at the consent screen and widen breach blast radius. Ask for what the workflow needs.
  • Hand-rolling integrations MCP already solves. Check the MCP registry before writing custom glue; an afternoon of configuration beats a week of API code.
Knowledge check
In OAuth, why does your app never see the user's password?
Answer: Login happens on the provider's own page; your app only receives an authorization code, exchanged for scoped, expiring tokens.
Access token vs refresh token?
Answer: The access token is the short-lived credential used on every API call; the refresh token is the long-lived credential used only to obtain new access tokens.
What inefficiency does MCP eliminate?
Answer: Bespoke glue for every app×tool pair. One standard server per tool, usable by every MCP-speaking AI client — N+M instead of N×M.
3.6 — Git and Version Control
Learning objectives
  • Explain repositories, commits, and why version control is non-negotiable for client work
  • Run the daily workflow: status → add → commit → push / pull
  • Use branches and merges, and keep secrets out of repositories with .gitignore

Git is a time machine for folders. It records snapshots of your project so you can see every change ever made, recover any earlier state, and collaborate without overwriting each other. For you it's also something more commercial: the professional packaging of your work. A client deployment delivered as a clean Git repository — configs, scripts, documentation, history — looks and is maintainable; a folder of files named final_v3_REAL.txt is not.

The mental model: a repository ("repo") is a project folder Git watches (created with git init, or copied from elsewhere with git clone <url>). A commit is a snapshot of the project at a moment, with an author, timestamp, and message — the unit of history. Staging (git add) is how you choose which changes go into the next snapshot. GitHub is a hosting service for repos (Git is the tool; GitHub the place) — your offsite copy, collaboration hub, and, per Lesson 11.8, where client project repos live.

The daily loop is four commands and becomes muscle memory within a week: git status (what changed?) → git add -A (stage it) → git commit -m "Add backup script for Phase 2 server" (snapshot it) → git push (sync to GitHub). Coming back to a machine: git pull first, to fetch what's newer. Commit messages deserve one sentence of care — "fix stuff" tells future-you nothing; "Raise Ollama context to 8k for legal agent" is documentation for free. Commit small and often: each commit is a restore point, and restore points are what you'll want at 11 PM when a config change breaks a client server.

Branches let you work on a copy of history without touching the stable line. git checkout -b new-rag-config forks a parallel track; you commit experiments there while main stays deployable; git merge folds the branch back when it's proven. If both lines touched the same code, Git declares a merge conflict and shows you both versions between <<<<<<< markers — you pick the right text, commit, done. Conflicts feel scary once, then never again. Solo consultants need branches less than teams do, but use them for anything risky on a live client repo.

Now the warning this lesson exists for: Git never forgets — so a secret committed once is compromised forever. Deleting the key in the next commit does nothing; it lives in history, and bots scrape public GitHub for exactly this within minutes. The guard rail is .gitignore: a file listing what Git must never track — .env (your environment-variable files), key files, logs, the venv/ folder. Write it before the first commit of every project, and if a key does slip through: rotate the key immediately (Lesson 3.3); scrubbing history is secondary cleanup, not the fix.

Walkthrough — a client project, from zero 
mkdir acme-deployment && cd acme-deployment
git init
printf ".env\nvenv/\n*.log\nmodels/\n" > .gitignore   # BEFORE anything else
echo "OLLAMA_HOST=0.0.0.0" > .env                     # secrets — ignored ✓

git add -A && git status        # confirms .env is NOT staged
git commit -m "Initial deployment scaffold for Acme"
git remote add origin git@github.com:you/acme-deployment.git
git push -u origin main

# risky change? branch it:
git checkout -b experiment-vllm
# ...edit, test, commit...
git checkout main && git merge experiment-vllm   # proven → merged

KEY TERMS: repository · commit · staging · push / pull · clone · branch · merge · merge conflict · .gitignore · GitHub

Common mistakes
  • Writing .gitignore after the first commit. By then .env may already be in history. Ignore file first, always.
  • Giant monthly commits. One commit per logical change — your history becomes a debugging timeline instead of a blob.
  • Treating a pushed secret as "deleted" after removing it. History keeps it. Rotate the credential first, then clean up.
Knowledge check
Order the daily Git loop.
Answer: git status → git add → git commit -m "…" → git push (and git pull when starting on a machine).
You committed and pushed an API key. First action?
Answer: Rotate (revoke and regenerate) the key immediately — history preserves the old one, so cleanup alone fixes nothing.
What problem do branches solve?
Answer: Experimenting safely: changes accumulate on a parallel line while main stays stable, merged back only when proven.
3.7 — Docker Basics
Learning objectives
  • Explain images vs containers and why "works on my machine" disappears with Docker
  • Run, inspect, stop, and update containers; map ports and persist data with volumes
  • Read a docker-compose file — the format your whole AI stack ships in

Docker packages an application together with everything it needs — runtime, libraries, settings — into a sealed, portable unit that runs identically on any machine. Why is this the last foundation lesson before you start building? Because the stack you deploy for clients — Open WebUI, n8n, monitoring, databases — ships as Docker containers. One consistent skill installs all of it, on a Mac Mini or a Linux rack server, identically every time. "Works on my machine" stops being a punchline and becomes a guarantee.

Two words carry the whole concept. An image is the frozen blueprint — the packaged application, versioned and downloadable from a registry (Docker Hub and friends), like ghcr.io/open-webui/open-webui:main. A container is a running instance of an image — start three containers from one image, get three isolated copies. Unlike a virtual machine, a container doesn't bundle an entire operating system; it shares the host's kernel and isolates just the process — so it starts in about a second and adds almost no overhead. Delete a container and the image remains; and here is the single most important Docker fact: anything written inside a container dies with it. Containers are designed to be disposable. Data that must survive — user accounts, chat history, workflow definitions — lives in a volume: a named storage area on the host that Docker mounts into the container. Update ritual everywhere in this course: pull the new image, remove the old container, start a fresh one with the same volume — data intact, app upgraded.

One more bridge between sealed container and outside world: port mapping. The flag -p 3000:8080 means "the host's port 3000 forwards into the container's port 8080" — host side first. That's why Open WebUI's docs say the interface appears at localhost:3000 even though the app inside listens on 8080. When a deployment "doesn't load," wrong port mapping is the first suspect; docker ps shows the live mappings.

Running one container is a long docker run command; running a stack is docker compose: a YAML file (docker-compose.yml) declaring every service, port, volume, and environment variable in one place, brought up with docker compose up -d (-d = detached, in the background). The compose file is more than convenience — it is your deployment as a document: reviewable, version-controlled in the client's Git repo (3.6), reproducible on replacement hardware in minutes. Modules 5 through 8 hand you compose files; after this lesson you can read every line.

Walkthrough — your essential Docker session 
docker run -d -p 3000:8080 -v open-webui:/app/backend/data \
  --name open-webui ghcr.io/open-webui/open-webui:main
#      -d background · -p host:container · -v volume:path-inside · --name handle

docker ps                      # what's running, with port mappings
docker logs -f open-webui      # live logs (Ctrl-C to stop watching)
docker stop open-webui         # graceful stop … docker start open-webui resumes

# UPDATE — the ritual (data survives in the volume):
docker pull ghcr.io/open-webui/open-webui:main
docker rm -f open-webui
docker run -d -p 3000:8080 -v open-webui:/app/backend/data \
  --name open-webui ghcr.io/open-webui/open-webui:main

# Same stack as compose (docker-compose.yml):
# services:
#   open-webui:
#     image: ghcr.io/open-webui/open-webui:main
#     ports: ["3000:8080"]
#     volumes: ["open-webui:/app/backend/data"]
# volumes: { open-webui: }
docker compose up -d           # the whole stack, one command

KEY TERMS: Docker · image · container · registry · volume · port mapping · docker compose · detached mode · kernel

Common mistakes
  • Running stateful apps without volumes. First update wipes the client's users and chat history. Volume on day one, verified in your deployment checklist.
  • Reversing port order. It's -p host:container — "can't reach the app" is usually this, checked in five seconds with docker ps.
  • "Fixing" things inside running containers (installing packages, editing files via shell). Disposable means your fix evaporates on update. Fix the compose file or image instead.
Knowledge check
Image vs container vs volume — one phrase each.
Answer: Image: the frozen blueprint. Container: a running, disposable instance of it. Volume: host-side storage that survives the container.
What does -p 3000:8080 mean exactly?
Answer: Requests to the host machine's port 3000 are forwarded to port 8080 inside the container.
Why is a compose file part of professional client delivery?
Answer: It documents the entire stack declaratively — reviewable, version-controlled, and able to rebuild the deployment on new hardware in minutes.
Lab 3A · API Workshop

Call real AI APIs with curl and Python, parse responses, and produce a working multi-model comparison script.

  1. Get two free-tier API keys (Google AI Studio is free; DeepSeek or another provider for the second). Store both as environment variables in your shell profile.Expected: echo $GOOGLE_API_KEY prints your key; it appears in no file under Git.
  2. Make your first curl call to each provider's chat endpoint (Lesson 3.3 shape). Pretty-print the JSON response by piping through python3 -m json.tool.Expected: a 200 response; you can point at the reply text and the usage/token counts in the JSON.
  3. Recreate the call in Python with the requests library: read the key from os.environ, POST the JSON, print response.json()["choices"][0]["message"]["content"] (adjust path per provider).Expected: same answer as curl, now programmable.
  4. Add error handling: check response.status_code; on 429/500, retry with exponential backoff (1s, 2s, 4s, give up after 3).Expected: a deliberate bad-key test prints a clean "auth failed" message instead of a crash.
  5. Build compare.py: loop one prompt across both providers, record reply, latency (time.time() around the call), and token usage; compute cost from each provider's prices; print a comparison table.Expected: a table with model, seconds, tokens, and cost per call.

You passed if: compare.py runs end-to-end with keys from environment variables, survives a forced error gracefully, and prints a real cost/speed comparison.

Lab 3B · Docker Deployment

Install Docker and prove the full container lifecycle, including the data-survival test most beginners fail.

  1. Install Docker Desktop (Mac/Windows) or Docker Engine (Linux). Verify with docker run hello-world.Expected: the hello message prints — engine works.
  2. Deploy a real web app: run the Open WebUI container from Lesson 3.7's walkthrough. Open http://localhost:3000 and create the admin account.Expected: the interface loads; you're logged in.
  3. Inspect: docker ps, docker logs open-webui, docker stats (note RAM use).Expected: you can state the container's port mapping and memory footprint.
  4. The survival test: stop and remove the container, start a new one with the same volume. Log in again.Expected: your admin account still exists — the volume preserved it.
  5. Repeat without the volume flag once (different name/port) and confirm data does NOT survive removal. Delete the test container.Expected: you have personally witnessed why volumes are non-negotiable.

You passed if: you can run, inspect, remove, and re-create a container and prove which data survived and why.

Lab 3C · Git Portfolio

Create the GitHub repository that becomes your course portfolio — every lab artifact from here forward gets committed to it.

  1. Create a GitHub account (if needed), generate an SSH key (ssh-keygen -t ed25519), add the public key to GitHub.Expected: ssh -T git@github.com greets you by username.
  2. Create a repo sovereignty-portfolio, clone it, write a README.md describing the course and yourself, and a .gitignore covering .env, venv/, *.log.Expected: first commit pushed; README renders on GitHub.
  3. Add your Module 1 hardware report, Lab 2 prompt sheets and model report, and Lab 3A scripts in organized folders. Commit each batch separately with descriptive messages.Expected: ≥3 commits with messages a stranger could follow.
  4. Branch experiments, change the README there, merge it back to main, push.Expected: merge completed; history shows the branch.

You passed if: the repo exists with organized lab artifacts, a clean history of meaningful commits, one merged branch — and zero secrets tracked.

Exam 3 · Developer Tools40 questions + practical: write a Python script that calls an API with a key from an environment variable, commit it to Git correctly, and deploy a container with persistent data. Passing: 80%.

MODULE 4

The Local AI Stack

6h lecture + 8h lab
Sources: Skill Guides 4, 5, 6 — DeepSeek, Google AI Studio, Open WebUI + Ollama
4.1 — Ollama: The Model Engine
Learning objectives
  • Install Ollama and manage models: pull, run, list, delete
  • Read model tags (size, quantization) and predict memory needs before pulling
  • Use the Ollama API — both its native endpoint and its OpenAI-compatible one

Everything in Semester 1 was preparation for this command: ollama run gemma4:12b. Ollama is the engine room of the local stack — a free, open-source program that downloads open-weight models, loads them into memory, runs inference on your hardware (Apple Silicon GPU, NVIDIA GPU, or CPU), and exposes the whole thing as a local API. Every layer you build later — Open WebUI, n8n workflows, custom agents — sits on top of this.

Installation is one line. macOS: brew install ollama (or the .dmg from ollama.com). Linux: curl -fsSL https://ollama.com/install.sh | sh. Windows: the installer from the site. Once running, Ollama listens at http://localhost:11434 — memorize that port; you'll type it hundreds of times in your career. ("localhost" means "this machine"; in Module 8 you'll open it to the office network.)

Model management is four verbs. ollama pull gemma4:12b downloads a model from Ollama's registry. ollama list shows what's installed and how big it is. ollama run gemma4:12b loads it and opens a chat right in the terminal (first load takes seconds to a minute as weights stream from SSD to memory — Lesson 1.1's data flow, live). ollama rm deletes. The text after the colon is the tag, selecting a variant: gemma4:12b is the 12-billion-parameter version. Tags can also pin quantization — qwen3.6:27b-q4_K_M means 4-bit quantized (the q4 family is the sweet spot you learned in Lesson 1.2; q8 is higher fidelity and double the memory). Pulling without a tag gets the default, which is almost always a q4 variant. Before any pull, run the Lesson 1.2 math: parameters × bits ÷ 8 + overhead. Ollama keeps a model loaded for a few minutes after use (then unloads to free memory) and can hold several loaded at once if RAM allows — relevant when you serve multiple agents in Module 5.

The API is what makes Ollama infrastructure rather than a toy. Two flavors on the same port: the native endpoint (POST /api/generate, POST /api/chat) and — the one you'll use most — the OpenAI-compatible endpoint at /v1/chat/completions, exactly the shape you mastered in Lesson 3.3. Any tool that can talk to OpenAI can talk to your Mac by changing one URL. This is the hinge of your business, so test it yourself in Lab 4A: same curl, zero API key, zero per-token cost, zero data leaving the room.

Two configuration knobs matter early. OLLAMA_HOST=0.0.0.0 (an environment variable — Lesson 3.2) makes Ollama accept connections from other machines, not just localhost — required the moment a second computer or a Docker container needs to reach it. And each model's context length defaults conservatively (often 4K–8K tokens); for RAG workloads in Module 5 you'll raise it (e.g. OLLAMA_CONTEXT_LENGTH or per-request num_ctx), paying the memory cost knowingly — context eats RAM beyond the weights themselves (the KV cache from Lesson 2.4).

Walkthrough — zero to local AI in five minutes 
brew install ollama                  # macOS (Linux: curl install script)
ollama pull gemma4:12b               # ~7 GB download, q4 default
ollama list                          # NAME          SIZE   MODIFIED
                                     # gemma4:12b    7.1 GB now
ollama run gemma4:12b                # chat in terminal; /bye to exit
ollama ps                            # what's loaded in RAM right now

# The money shot — your machine, speaking the industry-standard API:
curl http://localhost:11434/v1/chat/completions \
  -H "Content-Type: application/json" \
  -d '{"model":"gemma4:12b","messages":[
        {"role":"user","content":"One sentence: why local AI?"}]}'
# → {"choices":[{"message":{"content":"Because your data, your costs,
#    and your uptime belong to you."}}],...}   No key. No bill. No exfiltration.

KEY TERMS: Ollama · model registry · tag · pull / run / list / rm · port 11434 · OLLAMA_HOST · num_ctx / context length · KV cache · OpenAI-compatible endpoint

Common mistakes
  • Pulling models bigger than RAM. Ollama will try — and swap to disk at unusable speed. Do the memory math first; ollama ps confirms what actually fits.
  • Forgetting that context costs memory. A 32K-context session on a big model can add gigabytes (KV cache) beyond the weights. Size for weights + context, not weights alone.
  • Exposing 0.0.0.0 with no firewall. Open-to-the-network means everyone on the network. Module 8 pairs this setting with firewall rules — don't ship it bare.
Knowledge check
What lives at localhost:11434, and why does the /v1 path matter commercially?
Answer: Ollama's API. The /v1/chat/completions path is OpenAI-compatible, so any cloud-API tool can switch to local hardware by changing its base URL.
Decode qwen3.6:27b-q4_K_M and estimate its memory need.
Answer: Qwen 3.6, 27B parameters, 4-bit quantized → roughly 13–14 GB for weights plus overhead; comfortable on 24–32 GB machines.
When do you need OLLAMA_HOST=0.0.0.0?
Answer: When anything other than the local machine — another computer, or a Docker container like Open WebUI — must reach the API. Always paired with firewall rules.
4.2 — The Open Model Ecosystem
Learning objectives
  • Profile the major open families — Gemma 4, Llama 4, DeepSeek V4, Qwen 3.6, Mistral 3, gpt-oss — and their licenses
  • Apply a five-question selection checklist to pick a model for a client
  • Explain why licensing went permissive in 2025–26 and what that means for deployments

Your deployment arsenal is the open-weight ecosystem, and as of mid-2026 it is the strongest it has ever been. Walk the shelves like a professional buyer — capabilities first, then licenses, then the checklist you'll apply on every engagement.

Gemma 4 (Google) is the efficiency family: from tiny multimodal E2B/E4B models up through a 12B workhorse, a 26B MoE, and a 31B dense flagship — and, decisively, now licensed Apache 2.0 (Google dropped its custom license in 2026). For typical 16–64 GB client machines, Gemma 4's 12B is often your default first install. Qwen 3.6 (Alibaba) is the coder and the volume champion — the most-downloaded open family; the 27B dense is arguably the best programming model that runs on consumer hardware, Apache 2.0. DeepSeek V4 is the reasoning frontier in the open: MIT license (the most permissive there is), a 1-million-token context window, and an MoE design — V4-Flash (284B total / 13B active — recall the Lesson 2.7 sizing rule) is the deployment favorite on big-memory Mac Studios. Llama 4 (Meta) — Scout (109B/17B active, 10M context) and Maverick (400B/17B) — remains capable, but note both the hardware appetite and the Llama Community License: usage caps, attribution requirements, EU restrictions. It's the one major family whose license you must actually read against the client's situation. Mistral 3 (French; Apache 2.0 across Large 3 and the small dense Ministral line) carries extra weight with European and GDPR-sensitive clients. And gpt-oss (yes, OpenAI — their 2025 return to open weights, Apache 2.0): the 20B variant runs in 16 GB and punches at small-frontier reasoning level.

The story behind those license labels matters to your positioning. In 2025–26 the ecosystem flipped permissive: DeepSeek shipping MIT, Gemma moving to Apache 2.0, Qwen and Mistral staying Apache, OpenAI re-entering openly — while Meta, the family that started the open wave, became the restrictive outlier and shifted focus to closed flagships. Two consequences for you. First, the old objection "open models are legally murky" is mostly dead — Apache/MIT are the same licenses enterprise software has trusted for decades. Second, the frontier now splits: labs keep their very best models closed and API-only, releasing excellent mid-tier weights openly. Your honest pitch acknowledges this: clients aren't getting the #1 model on Earth — they're getting 90-something percent of it, owned outright, on their hardware, with their data. For the overwhelming majority of business tasks, that trade isn't close.

The selection checklist — five questions, in order: (1) Fit: does it run in the client's memory at q4, with context headroom? (2) License: Apache/MIT → proceed; community licenses → read against client specifics. (3) Task strength: code → Qwen; reasoning/analysis → DeepSeek; general chat/multilingual → Gemma or Mistral; long documents → context monsters. (4) Speed: active parameters vs the machine's bandwidth (Lesson 1.3 math) — will it feel instant to staff? (5) Evidence: your own Lab 2B-style scores on the client's real tasks, because leaderboards measure benchmarks, not their invoices. Models are also not mutually exclusive — Ollama hosts several side by side, and Module 5 assigns different models to different agents. The selection skill compounds: every deployment teaches you another data point no leaderboard has.

Walkthrough — three clients, three right answers 
Dental office, Mac Mini 32 GB, summaries + drafting:
  → gemma4:12b (Apache 2.0, ~7 GB, fast, friendly tone)
  + backup: gpt-oss:20b for harder reasoning days

Software agency, Mac Studio 64 GB, code assistant:
  → qwen3.6:27b (Apache 2.0, ~14 GB, best-in-class local code)

Law firm, Mac Studio 256 GB, long-contract analysis, EU clients:
  → deepseek V4-Flash (MIT, 1M context, MoE speed)
  + mistral 3 for the GDPR-comfort conversation
  ✗ llama4 scout — capable, but why carry license caveats
    when Apache/MIT peers exist at this tier?

KEY TERMS: open weights · Apache 2.0 · MIT license · community license · Gemma 4 · Llama 4 · DeepSeek V4 · Qwen 3.6 · Mistral 3 · gpt-oss · selection checklist

Common mistakes
  • Chasing the leaderboard #1. The right question is "best for THIS task on THIS hardware under THIS license," and the answer is frequently a 12–27B model, not a giant.
  • Treating all "open" licenses as identical. Apache 2.0 and MIT are clean; Llama's community license has real conditions. Thirty seconds of reading prevents real liability.
  • One-model deployments. Different agents deserve different engines — a code model for devs, a writer for marketing. Ollama makes multi-model trivial; use it.
Knowledge check
Which family would you shortlist first for: local coding? frontier reasoning with 1M context? a GDPR-anxious EU client?
Answer: Qwen 3.6 (code) · DeepSeek V4 (reasoning/long context, MIT) · Mistral 3 (European, Apache 2.0).
What changed in open-model licensing in 2025–26?
Answer: The ecosystem went permissive — Gemma to Apache 2.0, DeepSeek MIT, Qwen/Mistral Apache, OpenAI's gpt-oss Apache — while Meta's Llama became the restrictive outlier.
List the five selection-checklist questions in order.
Answer: Memory fit → license → task strength → speed (active params vs bandwidth) → your own task-specific evidence.
4.3 — Running Models Locally
Learning objectives
  • Deploy and benchmark models on Mac and Linux, step by step
  • Manage memory across multiple loaded models and tune for performance
  • Produce a benchmark report that justifies hardware and model choices to a client

Installing Ollama is five minutes; running models well is a craft. This lesson is the operations manual: how to deploy on the two platforms you'll meet, how to measure honestly, and which knobs actually matter.

On macOS, Ollama uses Apple's Metal framework to run inference on the GPU cores against unified memory — it simply works, no drivers, no configuration. One important default: macOS reserves a portion of unified memory for the system, so usable model memory is roughly 65–75% of total on default settings (a 64 GB Mac comfortably serves ~45 GB of model+context). On Linux, Ollama with an NVIDIA GPU requires the NVIDIA driver and container toolkit if Dockerized — afterwards, models run in VRAM at full speed, spilling to system RAM (with a sharp slowdown) only if too big. Linux is also where headless server discipline applies: run Ollama as a systemd service so it starts on boot and restarts on failure — systemctl status ollama becomes part of your health checks (Lesson 3.2's ten-minute inspection, extended).

Benchmarking turns feelings into numbers. The metrics that matter: load time (SSD → RAM, felt on first request after idle), time to first token (how long before text starts appearing — dominated by reading the prompt, so it grows with input length), and generation speed in tokens/second (the bandwidth math of Lesson 1.3, now measured). Get all three from ollama run <model> --verbose, which prints statistics after each reply — prompt eval rate, eval rate, durations. Benchmark with consistent prompts (short Q&A, a 2-page summary, a code task), three runs each, recording the spread. The deliverable habit: every deployment ends with a one-page benchmark table in the client repo — it sets expectations, catches regressions after updates, and quietly demonstrates rigor no competitor shows.

Multi-model serving is mostly automatic: Ollama loads models on demand and keeps them resident while memory allows (controlled by OLLAMA_MAX_LOADED_MODELS and OLLAMA_KEEP_ALIVE, which sets how long an idle model stays in RAM). The planning rule: sum the loaded set, not the installed set — twelve models on the SSD is fine; the three that serve simultaneously must sum (with contexts) under usable memory. If a requested model doesn't fit alongside the residents, Ollama evicts — which a user experiences as a mysterious slow first reply. On busy multi-agent boxes, pin your KEEP_ALIVE deliberately: long (or -1, forever) for the daily-driver model, short for occasional specialists. Parallelism: OLLAMA_NUM_PARALLEL lets one loaded model answer several requests concurrently (sharing the context budget); it's how a single Mac serves a 5-person office without queuing — and its limits are exactly where vLLM (Lesson 4.5) picks up.

Performance tuning, in honest order of impact: (1) quantization choice — q4 vs q8 is a 2× memory swing; (2) context length — set what the workload needs, not the maximum; (3) keep-alive/eviction tuning as above; (4) everything else is marginal on Apple Silicon. When tuning stops closing the gap, the answer is hardware tier or architecture (vLLM, Module 10) — knowing when to stop tuning is also expertise.

Walkthrough — the benchmark session, verbatim 
ollama run gemma4:12b --verbose
>>> Summarize: [paste 2-page document] ...
# after reply:
# total duration:       9.84s
# prompt eval rate:     412.3 tokens/s   ← reading speed (time to first token)
# eval rate:            31.7 tokens/s    ← generation speed (the headline number)

# Benchmark table for the client repo:
# model          load   1st-token   gen tok/s   RAM
# gemma4:12b     4.1s   0.6s        31.7        7.3 GB
# qwen3.6:27b    8.9s   1.2s        14.2        14.1 GB
# gpt-oss:20b    6.2s   0.9s        19.8        11.8 GB
# Verdict: gemma4 default for chat; qwen for code despite speed cost.

# Service hygiene (Linux):
sudo systemctl enable ollama && systemctl status ollama
# Memory knobs (set as environment variables for the service):
# OLLAMA_MAX_LOADED_MODELS=2  OLLAMA_KEEP_ALIVE=30m  OLLAMA_NUM_PARALLEL=4

KEY TERMS: Metal · VRAM spillover · systemd service · load time · time to first token · eval rate · OLLAMA_KEEP_ALIVE · OLLAMA_NUM_PARALLEL · eviction

Common mistakes
  • Benchmarking once, on one prompt. First runs include load time; short prompts hide prompt-eval costs. Three runs, three workloads, report the spread.
  • Planning memory by installed models. Only the simultaneously-loaded set matters — and each loaded model's context (KV cache) counts too.
  • Chasing exotic flags before fixing quantization and context. The big knobs are q-level and num_ctx; tune those first, measure, then stop.
Knowledge check
Name the three benchmark metrics and what dominates each.
Answer: Load time (SSD→RAM streaming), time to first token (prompt reading — grows with input length), generation tokens/sec (memory bandwidth ÷ model size).
A user reports "the AI is randomly slow on the first question." Likely cause?
Answer: Model eviction/unload — their model was evicted (memory pressure or KEEP_ALIVE expiry) and had to reload from SSD. Tune keep-alive or reduce the resident set.
What does OLLAMA_NUM_PARALLEL buy you, and at what cost?
Answer: Concurrent requests against one loaded model — a small office on one box — at the cost of shared context budget and per-user speed under load.
4.4 — Google AI Studio
Learning objectives
  • Use Google AI Studio as a free playground for prompts, parameters, and model testing
  • Generate an API key and call the Gemini API from code
  • Make the cloud-vs-local tradeoff explicit, and know when hybrid is the honest answer

One cloud tool earns a permanent place in your sovereign toolbox: Google AI Studio (aistudio.google.com). It's Google's free browser playground for the Gemini and Gemma model families — and for you it serves three professional purposes that have nothing to do with abandoning local-first principles.

Purpose one: the laboratory. AI Studio exposes the dials you learned in Lesson 2.5 — temperature, top-p, max output, system instructions, JSON-output toggles — with zero setup and zero invoice. It's the fastest place to prototype a prompt before configuring it into a local agent: test your legal-summarizer instructions against Gemma in the browser, then port the proven prompt into Open WebUI against the same Gemma family running on the client's hardware (Lesson 5.3). Prompts are mostly portable; behavioral quirks transfer within a family far better than across families — which is exactly why prototyping on Gemma-in-the-cloud for Gemma-on-a-Mac is such a clean workflow.

Purpose two: the key vending machine. One click ("Get API key") yields a free-tier Gemini API key — rate-limited but real, and the fastest path to a working cloud endpoint for Lab 3A-style scripting, for n8n experiments in Module 6, and for any workflow where you need a second opinion from a frontier-class model. Note the privacy print: free-tier traffic may be used by Google to improve products — fine for your tests with dummy data, never for client documents. Paid-tier and enterprise arrangements differ; reading those terms aloud to a client is, itself, a sovereignty sales moment.

Purpose three: the honest comparison bench. Cloud versus local is not religion; it's a tradeoff table you should be able to recite cold. Cloud wins on: absolute frontier capability (the very best models remain API-only — Lesson 4.2), zero hardware investment, instant scale, multimodal breadth. Local wins on: data custody (nothing leaves the building — the entire compliance story of Module 9), marginal cost (tokens are free after hardware — the CFO math of Lesson 2.4), latency consistency, offline operation, and immunity to provider price changes, model deprecations, and outages. The professional answer for many clients is hybrid: local by default for anything touching private data; a cloud escape hatch, with explicit data rules, for rare tasks demanding frontier reasoning. Module 10 builds this as architecture; here, plant the principle: sovereignty is about who decides, not about never using clouds.

Practical fluency: in the Studio interface you pick a model (Gemini flagships, Gemma open models), set system instructions, chat, tweak parameters, and — usefully — click "Get code" to export your session as a curl or Python snippet, which drops straight into the Lesson 3.3 request shape you already speak. Spend an hour here and the entire parameter vocabulary of this course becomes tactile.

Walkthrough — prototype in Studio, deploy local 
1. aistudio.google.com → New prompt → model: Gemma (latest)
2. System instructions: "You are a contracts analyst. Summarize in exactly
   5 bullets. Flag termination & liability clauses with ⚠. Cite sections."
3. Temperature 0.2 (precision task — Lesson 2.5). Paste test contract. Iterate
   until output is consistently right. ~20 minutes, $0.
4. "Get code" → confirm it's the familiar chat-completions shape.
5. Port: same instructions + temperature into an Open WebUI agent (Lesson 5.3)
   running gemma4:12b on the client's Mac.
6. Verify with the same test contract locally. Behavior carries.
   The prompt was engineered in the cloud; the client's contracts never were.

KEY TERMS: Google AI Studio · Gemini API · free tier · system instructions panel · "Get code" export · cloud vs local tradeoff · hybrid architecture

Common mistakes
  • Pasting client data into free-tier tools. Free tiers may train on inputs. Dummy data for prototyping; real data stays on metal you control.
  • Prototyping on one family, deploying another. Prompts tuned on a frontier Gemini can overestimate what a 12B local model will do. Prototype on the family you'll ship (Gemma→Gemma).
  • Framing cloud as the enemy. Clients smell zealotry. Frame it as custody and economics; recommend hybrid when hybrid is true.
Knowledge check
What are AI Studio's three legitimate roles in a sovereignty practice?
Answer: Free prompt/parameter laboratory, API-key source for scripting and integration tests, and the comparison bench for honest cloud-vs-local conversations.
Why prototype client prompts on Gemma rather than a Gemini flagship?
Answer: The deployment target is Gemma-family on local hardware — behavior transfers within a family; a frontier model would set expectations a 12B can't meet.
Name three things local wins on, and two things cloud wins on.
Answer: Local: data custody, near-zero marginal cost, latency consistency/offline/immunity to provider changes. Cloud: absolute frontier capability, zero hardware investment and instant scale.
4.5 — vLLM for Production
Learning objectives
  • Identify the concurrency wall where Ollama stops being the right tool
  • Explain continuous batching and tensor parallelism — vLLM's two superpowers
  • Serve a model with vLLM behind the same OpenAI-compatible API

Ollama is the perfect engine for one machine and a handful of users. But push it — 30 staff hammering one box at 9 AM, an automation firing hundreds of requests an hour — and you hit the wall every successful deployment eventually finds. vLLM is what's on the other side: an open-source inference server built for production scale, the same engine class that powers commercial AI services. You'll deploy it for real in Module 10; this lesson is the reconnaissance.

Why does naive serving collapse under concurrency? Two reasons. First, simple servers process requests substantially one-after-another — request #12 waits for #1–11. Second, memory waste: each conversation's KV cache (the attention memory from Lesson 2.4) traditionally reserved space for its maximum possible length, hoarding gigabytes it never used. vLLM attacks both. Continuous batching exploits a beautiful property of transformers: since every request is just next-token prediction in a loop (Lesson 2.5), the server can interleave many conversations through the GPU at once — finishing requests exit the batch mid-flight, arriving ones join immediately, and the GPU never idles. Where a naive server's throughput dies at a dozen concurrent users, vLLM sustains hundreds. Its companion invention, PagedAttention, manages KV-cache memory the way operating systems manage RAM — in small pages allocated on demand — eliminating the hoarding and multiplying how many conversations fit in VRAM.

The second superpower is tensor parallelism: splitting every layer's weight matrices across multiple GPUs, which then compute each token cooperatively. This is how models bigger than any single GPU get served — a 70B+ dense model spread over 4 GPUs, each holding a quarter — and how NVLink (Lesson 1.4) earns its keep, carrying the chatter between GPU shards. (Contrast with the MoE trick of Lesson 2.7: MoE reduces compute per token by architecture; tensor parallelism splits memory and compute across hardware. Big deployments use both at once.)

Operationally, vLLM is comfortable territory: pip install vllm, then vllm serve <model> — it pulls weights from Hugging Face (the model warehouse of the open ecosystem) and exposes — of course — an OpenAI-compatible API on port 8000. The interface layer you'll build in Module 5 cannot tell vLLM from Ollama from OpenAI: same request shape, different muscle behind it. That's the architectural payoff of standardization, and it's why your stacks upgrade without rebuilding.

The decision rule to carry: Ollama — Mac-friendly, multi-model juggling, brilliant simplicity, right for Phases 1–2 (1–25 users). vLLM — Linux + NVIDIA territory, one model served extremely well per instance, continuous batching, multi-GPU, right for Phase 3+ (25 to thousands of users) and for any automation pipeline with real request volume. The honest middle: a Phase 2 office flirting with the wall usually buys more RAM and tighter models first — the jump to vLLM is a hardware-platform jump (NVIDIA, rack, Linux administration), so you make it deliberately, priced into a Phase 3 proposal (Module 10), not as a panic upgrade.

Walkthrough — the wall, and the door through it 
# The wall (symptoms at a 30-user office on one Ollama box):
#   9:02 AM — replies take 40s; ollama ps shows queue depth; staff give up.

# The door (Module 10 preview — Linux box, 2× GPUs):
pip install vllm
vllm serve Qwen/Qwen3.6-27B --tensor-parallel-size 2
# → INFO: Started server on http://0.0.0.0:8000 (OpenAI-compatible)

curl http://localhost:8000/v1/chat/completions \
  -H "Content-Type: application/json" \
  -d '{"model":"Qwen/Qwen3.6-27B","messages":[
        {"role":"user","content":"hello"}]}'
# Same API your whole stack already speaks. Open WebUI: change one base URL.
# Continuous batching: 100+ concurrent chats, GPU saturated, nobody queues.

KEY TERMS: vLLM · inference server · continuous batching · PagedAttention · tensor parallelism · Hugging Face · throughput · concurrency · port 8000

Common mistakes
  • Prescribing vLLM for a 10-user office. It's a Linux/NVIDIA production tool with admin overhead; Ollama covers Phases 1–2 with far less to maintain.
  • Confusing throughput with single-user speed. vLLM's win is many users at once; one lone request isn't dramatically faster than Ollama. Quote the right metric.
  • Forgetting vLLM serves one model per instance (vs Ollama's juggling). Multi-model production means multiple instances behind a router — architecture, not a checkbox.
Knowledge check
What two wastes does vLLM eliminate, and with which mechanisms?
Answer: Sequential GPU idling — via continuous batching (interleaving many generations); and KV-cache hoarding — via PagedAttention (on-demand paged memory).
Tensor parallelism vs MoE — both involve "splitting." Distinguish them.
Answer: Tensor parallelism splits each layer's weights across physical GPUs (hardware sharding). MoE is an architecture that routes each token through a subset of experts (less compute per token). They stack.
Why does switching Ollama → vLLM not disturb the interface layer?
Answer: Both expose the OpenAI-compatible chat completions API — Open WebUI and every workflow just point at a new base URL.
Lab 4A · Local Model Deployment

Install Ollama, deploy three models, benchmark honestly, and write the performance report that becomes your deployment-grade template.

  1. Install Ollama (4.1) and verify: ollama --version, then confirm the API answers: curl http://localhost:11434.Expected: version prints; curl returns "Ollama is running".
  2. Do the memory math from Lesson 1.2 for your machine, then pull three models that fit — e.g. a small (3–4B), a medium (8–12B), and your ceiling model.Expected: ollama list shows three models; none exceeds your computed budget.
  3. Benchmark each with --verbose on three standard tasks (short Q&A, 2-page summary, code function), three runs each. Record load time, first-token time, eval tok/s, RAM (ollama ps).Expected: a 9-row (3 models × 3 tasks) measurement table.
  4. Test the OpenAI-compatible endpoint with curl against each model.Expected: valid chat-completion JSON from all three.
  5. Write the report: machine specs, measurement table, tok/s vs the Lesson 1.3 theoretical ceiling, and a verdict per model (best for what?). Commit to your portfolio repo.Expected: 1–2 pages; measured speeds land 50–80% of theoretical — explain the gap.

You passed if: three models run within your computed memory budget, the API answers via curl, and your committed report contains real numbers with a defensible verdict.

Lab 4B · Multi-Model Server

Turn one machine into a multi-model server and observe scheduling, eviction, and concurrency behavior firsthand.

  1. Set OLLAMA_MAX_LOADED_MODELS=2 and OLLAMA_KEEP_ALIVE=10m (environment variables; restart Ollama).Expected: settings active — behavior in step 3 will prove it.
  2. Load two models by sending one request each; verify both resident with ollama ps.Expected: two models listed with RAM footprints.
  3. Request a third model and watch ollama ps: which was evicted?Expected: the least-recently-used model unloads — you can name it.
  4. Concurrency: send 4 simultaneous requests to one model (4 terminal windows, or a small Python threads script). Time each.Expected: with NUM_PARALLEL default, requests queue or share — record per-request times vs solo baseline.
  5. Set OLLAMA_NUM_PARALLEL=4, restart, repeat step 4.Expected: concurrent latency improves; per-request speed dips slightly — write both numbers down.
  6. Document: a half-page "multi-user behavior" note for the client repo — eviction policy observed, concurrency settings chosen, the load at which this box will need help.Expected: you can answer "how many simultaneous users before it hurts?" with data.

You passed if: you induced and explained one eviction, measured concurrency before/after NUM_PARALLEL, and documented the box's real multi-user ceiling.

Exam 4 · Local AI InfrastructurePractical: deploy a working local AI server from scratch within 60 minutes — Ollama installed, two appropriate models pulled and benchmarked, OpenAI-compatible endpoint verified with curl, settings documented. Graded on correctness, memory-math justification, and documentation.
MODULE 5

The Interface Layer

6h lecture + 8h lab
Sources: Skill Guides 6, 7 — Open WebUI, TypingMind
5.1 — Open WebUI: Installation and Config
Learning objectives
  • Deploy Open WebUI with Docker and connect it to Ollama
  • Configure the admin account, user signup policy, and model visibility
  • Brand the interface so it feels like the client's product, not a tool you installed

Ollama gives you an API; clients need a product. Open WebUI is that product: a polished, self-hosted chat interface — the ChatGPT experience, running entirely on hardware the client owns. It is open source, moves fast (v0.9.x as of mid-2026, with a native desktop app now alongside the web version), and it is the single most important application in your stack because it's the part users actually see.

Deployment is one Docker command — and now every flag reads as plain language to you (Lesson 3.7): docker run -d -p 3000:8080 -v open-webui:/app/backend/data --name open-webui ghcr.io/open-webui/open-webui:main. Detached, host port 3000 into container port 8080, a named volume holding everything that matters (users, chats, knowledge bases, agent configs — your update-survival insurance), pulled from the official registry. Browse to http://localhost:3000 and the first account created becomes the admin — create it immediately yourself; an unclaimed admin on a fresh install is an open door.

Connecting the engine: Open WebUI auto-detects Ollama when both run on the same host — with one classic catch worth understanding rather than cargo-culting: from inside a container, "localhost" means the container itself, not the Mac. The standard fix is the special hostname host.docker.internal:11434 (set in Admin Panel → Settings → Connections), plus OLLAMA_HOST=0.0.0.0 on the Ollama side (Lesson 4.1) so it accepts non-local connections. You can also add additional OpenAI-compatible backends in the same panel — a vLLM server, even a cloud API for an explicit hybrid setup (Lesson 4.4) — and Open WebUI presents them all in one model picker. The interface layer unifies; the engines compete underneath. That's your architecture in one sentence.

Configuration that matters on day one. Admin Panel → Settings: turn off open signup (or set new registrations to "pending" so the admin approves each — right default for offices); set the default model so staff never face an empty dropdown; review which models are visible to which user groups (the 27B coder for the dev team; everyone else sees the friendly default). Then the part consultants skip and shouldn't: branding. Name the deployment after the client ("NorthStar Assistant," not "Open WebUI"), set their logo and colors, write a welcome line in their voice. Psychology, not vanity: staff adopt "our AI" and distrust "some tool IT installed," and adoption is what your invoice ultimately bought. Updates follow the Lesson 3.7 ritual — pull, remove, re-run with the same volume — scheduled monthly in your support agreement (Module 7).

Walkthrough — engine + interface, the canonical pairing 
# 1. Engine accepts network connections (macOS example):
launchctl setenv OLLAMA_HOST "0.0.0.0"   # then restart Ollama

# 2. Interface:
docker run -d -p 3000:8080 \
  -v open-webui:/app/backend/data \
  -e OLLAMA_BASE_URL=http://host.docker.internal:11434 \
  --name open-webui ghcr.io/open-webui/open-webui:main

# 3. http://localhost:3000 → create ADMIN account (you)
# 4. Admin Panel → Settings → Connections → verify Ollama models appear
# 5. Settings → General: disable open signup; default model: gemma4:12b
# 6. Settings → Interface: client name, logo, welcome message
# Smoke test: new chat → "hello" → streamed reply → deployment is alive.

KEY TERMS: Open WebUI · admin account · host.docker.internal · OLLAMA_BASE_URL · signup policy · model visibility · default model · branding

Common mistakes
  • Leaving signup open on a network-reachable install. Anyone on the network can register. Close it or require approval before the box leaves your bench.
  • The localhost trap. Container → Ollama connections fail with "connection refused" until you use host.docker.internal (and OLLAMA_HOST=0.0.0.0). It's the #1 setup ticket.
  • Skipping branding to save an hour. Unbranded tools get ignored; branded ones get adopted. Adoption is the deliverable.
Knowledge check
Why can't the Open WebUI container reach Ollama at "localhost:11434"?
Answer: Inside a container, localhost is the container itself. Use host.docker.internal:11434 and set OLLAMA_HOST=0.0.0.0 so Ollama accepts the connection.
What lives in the open-webui volume, and what does that imply?
Answer: Users, chats, knowledge bases, and agent configs — so the volume must exist from day one and be part of every backup (Module 8).
Why does the admin account matter at install time specifically?
Answer: The first registered account becomes admin — claim it immediately or someone else can.
5.2 — RAG: Knowledge Base Systems
Learning objectives
  • Trace the full RAG pipeline: chunk → embed → store → retrieve → generate
  • Tune chunk size, overlap, and top-K — and know the symptoms of each being wrong
  • Organize collections and evaluate retrieval quality with a test set

Lesson 2.9 placed RAG in the memory stack as Layer 3 — long-term knowledge. Now you build it. In Open WebUI the feature is called Knowledge (knowledge bases, or KBs): upload the client's documents, attach the KB to an agent, and the model answers from their material instead of its general training. This single capability converts "a chatbot" into "our company's assistant" — and converts your deployment from commodity to indispensable.

Walk the pipeline, because every tuning decision you'll ever make lives in one of its five stages. (1) Chunking: documents are split into pieces — the chunk size (commonly 500–1,500 tokens) with an overlap (50–200 tokens) so sentences straddling a boundary survive in at least one piece. (2) Embedding: each chunk goes through an embedding model — a small, specialized network (not your chat model) that converts text into a vector: a list of hundreds of numbers positioning the chunk's meaning in space, where related ideas land near each other. "Termination clause" and "ending the agreement" share no words but land close. (3) Storage: vectors go into a vector database (Open WebUI ships one embedded — Chroma — invisible until Phase 4 scale, where Module 10 upgrades it). (4) Retrieval: at question time, the question is embedded too, and the database returns the top-K nearest chunks (typically 3–10). (5) Generation: those chunks are pasted into the model's context with your question — Layer 1 and Layer 3 of the memory stack cooperating — and the model answers, citing sources Open WebUI displays.

Tuning is symptom-driven. Answers missing obvious facts? Retrieval failed — raise top-K, or your chunks are too large (one giant chunk dilutes its own meaning; the embedding averages everything and matches nothing). Answers technically correct but missing surrounding context? Chunks too small — facts arrived shorn of their setting; raise size or overlap. Answers slow or the model rambles? You're stuffing too many chunks into context — lower top-K. Start at the defaults (≈1,000-token chunks, ≈100 overlap, top-K 4–6), change one variable at a time, and test against a fixed question set — Lab 5A makes this rigorous. Also respect the quiet dependency from Lesson 4.1: retrieved chunks consume context window; an agent doing heavy RAG needs its model's num_ctx raised to fit question + chunks + answer.

Collections are an information architecture decision. One mega-KB containing everything retrieves noise — the HR handbook outranks the engineering spec for an engineering question because both mention "performance review." Segment by audience and topic: policies-hr, contracts-active, product-docs — and attach to agents accordingly (the support agent gets product docs; it cannot leak salary policy it was never connected to — which is also a security boundary, anticipating Module 8's department isolation). Garbage-in still applies: scanned PDFs without text layers, tables mangled to soup, obsolete drafts — cleaning the corpus before upload is unglamorous and is also why your RAG works when the client's DIY attempt didn't.

Walkthrough — the pipeline on one real question 
KB "contracts-active": 50 PDFs → 1,180 chunks (1,000 tok, 100 overlap)

User: "What's our notice period for terminating the Acme agreement?"

embed(question) → search vectors → top-4 chunks:
  #312  acme_msa.pdf §9.2 "…either party may terminate with 60 days
        written notice…"                       (similarity 0.89)
  #318  acme_msa.pdf §9.4 cure periods          (0.81)
  #207  acme_sow2.pdf renewal terms             (0.74)
  #1,022 vendor_policy.pdf generic termination  (0.69)

context = [4 chunks] + question → gemma4:12b (temp 0.2):
"Per the Acme MSA §9.2, either party may terminate with 60 days' written
notice. Note §9.4 requires a 30-day cure period for breach-based
termination. [Sources: acme_msa.pdf]"

Two seconds. Sourced. Nothing left the building.

KEY TERMS: knowledge base · chunking · overlap · embedding model · vector · vector database · top-K · retrieval · citation · collection

Common mistakes
  • One KB to rule them all. Mixed corpora cross-contaminate retrieval. Segment by audience/topic and attach per agent.
  • Uploading garbage and blaming the model. Scanned image-PDFs and stale drafts poison retrieval. Curate first — it's half the billable value.
  • Tuning two knobs at once. Change chunk size OR top-K, re-test the fixed question set, then move on. Otherwise you learn nothing from improvement.
Knowledge check
Why does RAG find "ending the agreement" when asked about "termination"?
Answer: Retrieval matches embedding vectors — positions in meaning-space — not keywords. Synonymous ideas land near each other.
Answers keep missing facts you know are in the documents. Two likeliest causes?
Answer: Top-K too low (the right chunk ranked just outside the cut) or chunks too large (diluted embeddings match poorly). Raise K or shrink chunks — one at a time.
How is KB segmentation also a security control?
Answer: Agents can only retrieve from KBs they're attached to — the support agent physically cannot surface HR salary content it has no connection to.
5.3 — Custom AI Agents
Learning objectives
  • Build an Open WebUI agent: system prompt, model, KB, temperature, welcome message
  • Write production-grade system prompts using the role–rules–format–examples pattern
  • Design an agent portfolio for an organization instead of one generic bot

A raw model is a generalist with no job description. An agent (Open WebUI calls them custom models; the industry says agents or assistants) is a packaged specialist: base model + system instructions + knowledge bases + parameters + a name and face. Agents are where everything from Semesters 1–2 converges into something a receptionist uses without training — and designing a portfolio of them is the visible craft clients pay Professional-tier prices for.

In Workspace → Models, each agent is six decisions. (1) Base model — match engine to job (Lesson 4.2): the support agent runs the fast friendly 12B; the contracts agent runs the long-context reasoner. (2) System instructions — the soul; next paragraph. (3) Knowledge — attach the right KB collections, and only the right ones (Lesson 5.2's security point). (4) Parameters — temperature per Lesson 2.5's cheat sheet (0.2 legal, 0.7 support, 0.9 marketing), plus num_ctx raised for RAG-heavy agents (Lesson 5.2). (5) Capabilities — web search (next lesson), vision, etc., per agent. (6) Presentation — name, avatar, welcome message, suggested starter prompts. That last item is adoption engineering: a blank chat box intimidates; "Hi, I'm the NorthStar Contracts Assistant — paste a contract or ask about any active agreement" with three example-question chips converts a skeptic in ten seconds.

The system prompt deserves a pattern, not vibes. Use role → rules → format → escalation → examples: who the agent is ("You are the support assistant for NorthStar Dental's front office staff"); hard rules, stated positively and negatively ("Answer only from the provided knowledge base. If the KB doesn't cover it, say so and name who to ask. Never give medical advice. Never quote prices not in the documents."); output format ("Answers under 150 words; bullets for procedures; always cite the source document"); escalation paths ("Billing disputes → refer to Maria x204"); then one or two worked examples — few-shot, from Lab 2A — showing a perfect answer. Write it in plain language; you are literally writing an employee handbook for a very fast, very literal new hire. Test adversarially before shipping: ask it things outside its KB, try to bait price quotes, paste an off-topic rant — Module 9 formalizes this as prompt-injection testing, but the habit starts here.

Portfolio design is an org-chart exercise: interview each department for their three most repetitive text tasks, then design one agent per job-to-be-done — not necessarily per department. Sales gets a proposal-drafter grounded in past proposals and the pricing sheet; support gets the product-docs assistant; legal gets the low-temperature contract analyst; HR gets the policy-handbook navigator with a hard "no individual advice" rule; marketing gets the high-temperature brand-voice writer. Five agents, five system prompts, five KB attachments — typically two days of your time, and the difference between "we installed AI" and "every department got a specialist." Lab 5B has you build exactly this; Exam 5 times you on it.

Walkthrough — one production system prompt, annotated 
ROLE      You are "Pearl," the front-office assistant for NorthStar Dental.
          You serve reception and billing staff — not patients.
RULES     Answer ONLY from the attached knowledge bases (policies-office,
          insurance-guides). If not covered: say "That's not in my
          documents" and suggest who to ask. NEVER give medical or
          dosage advice. NEVER quote prices except from fee_schedule.pdf.
FORMAT    ≤120 words. Procedures as numbered steps. Cite source doc
          in parentheses. Plain language — no jargon.
ESCALATE  Billing disputes → Maria (x204). Clinical questions → on-duty
          hygienist. Angry-patient situations → office manager, same day.
EXAMPLE   Q: "Patient says we overcharged a cleaning copay."
          A: "1) Pull the EOB in the patient file. 2) Compare to the
          insurance-guides table (Delta p.12). 3) If we erred, refund
          form FR-2; else share the EOB line. Disputes → Maria x204.
          (insurance-guides.pdf)"

Settings: gemma4:12b · temp 0.3 · num_ctx 8192 · KBs: 2 listed above
Welcome:  "Hi! I'm Pearl. Ask me about office policies, insurance, or
          billing procedures." + 3 starter chips

KEY TERMS: agent / custom model · system prompt · role–rules–format–escalation–examples · few-shot example · welcome message · starter prompts · agent portfolio

Common mistakes
  • One mega-agent with every KB and a 2,000-word prompt. Specialists beat generalists on accuracy, speed, and security. Split by job-to-be-done.
  • Rules without escalation paths. "Don't answer X" without "instead, do Y" produces dead-end conversations and frustrated staff.
  • Shipping untested prompts. Ten adversarial questions before launch catch 90% of embarrassments. Schedule them; don't hope.
Knowledge check
Name the six decisions that define an Open WebUI agent.
Answer: Base model, system instructions, attached knowledge, parameters (temperature/context), capabilities (search/vision), and presentation (name, welcome, starters).
What five sections make a production system prompt?
Answer: Role → rules (positive and negative) → output format → escalation paths → worked examples.
Why design agents per job-to-be-done rather than one company bot?
Answer: Specialists are more accurate (focused KB + prompt), faster (right-sized model), and safer (KB isolation) — and adoption is higher because each team sees its own work reflected.
5.4 — Web Search Integration
Learning objectives
  • Add live web search to local agents and explain the retrieval flow
