  • Choose a search backend (DuckDuckGo zero-config vs Google PSE keyed) deliberately
  • State the privacy boundary web search crosses — and configure agents accordingly

The standard knock on local AI: "its knowledge stops at the training cutoff" (Lesson 2.7's limits list). Web search integration removes it. Open WebUI can give any agent live search — and the mechanism will feel familiar, because it is RAG with the internet as the corpus: the system issues a search query, fetches the top results, extracts their text, and stuffs the relevant pieces into context exactly like KB chunks (Lesson 5.2's stage 4–5). The model still runs entirely on local hardware; only a search query travels.

Backends, configured in Admin Panel → Settings → Web Search. The pragmatic default is DuckDuckGo: zero configuration, no API key, no account — switch it on and agents can search. Its results are good-not-great and it rate-limits under heavy use, but for "what changed in the new tax year" office questions it's exactly right. The step up is Google PSE (Programmable Search Engine): you create a custom search engine in Google's console, get an API key and engine ID, and gain Google-quality results plus a powerful scoping trick — a PSE can be restricted to specific sites (only irs.gov, only the client's industry regulator, only their own website), which turns "web search" into "search of sources we trust." Free tier ~100 queries/day, then metered. Self-hosted SearXNG (a meta-search engine you run in Docker, fitting your stack philosophy) is the third option when clients want search without any third-party account. Per-agent, search is a capability toggle (Lesson 5.3's decision 5): the research agent gets it; the contracts agent — grounded in KBs and nothing else — pointedly does not.

Now say the quiet part loudly, because it's a consulting-grade distinction: web search punctures the privacy bubble — by design and by a measured amount. The user's question (or a model-generated condensation of it) leaves the building as a search query to DuckDuckGo or Google. Not the documents, not the KB, not the conversation — but the query itself can carry sensitive fragments ("merger terms for [ClientCo] acquisition"). Handle it like a professional: tell the client explicitly; disable search on agents that touch sensitive matter; scope PSE engines to trusted sites; and document the boundary in the deployment's data-flow diagram (Module 9 requires one). Sovereignty, as Lesson 4.4 framed it, is about who decides what crosses the wall — this lesson is you installing a gate and labeling it.

Practical behavior notes that save support tickets: searches add seconds (fetch + extract + bigger context = slower first token — Lesson 4.3's metrics predict it); result quality varies wildly by query phrasing, and models sometimes search when they shouldn't or skip it when they should — the per-chat toggle (the little globe in the message bar) lets users force it on or off, and your client training (Module 7) should demo exactly that. Citations appear like KB citations; teach staff to click them — a sourced answer is checkable, and checkable is the culture you're installing.

Walkthrough — scoped search for a tax practice 
Admin → Settings → Web Search:
  engine: google_pse
  api key: $GOOGLE_PSE_KEY        ← environment variable, naturally
  engine id: a1b2c3 (PSE console: restricted to irs.gov + state DOR sites)

Agent "TaxDesk" (capabilities: ✓ web search):
  Q: "Did the 2026 mileage rate change?"
  → search("2026 IRS standard mileage rate") → 3 pages fetched from irs.gov
  → context: [extracted passages] + question → local qwen3.6
  → "The 2026 standard mileage rate is ¢XX/mile, up from… [irs.gov]"

Agent "ClientFiles" (capabilities: ✗ web search):
  grounded in KB only — client financials never inspire an outbound query.
One deployment, two agents, one labeled gate in the wall.

KEY TERMS: web search integration · DuckDuckGo backend · Google PSE · SearXNG · scoped search · search-as-RAG · privacy boundary · data-flow diagram

Common mistakes
  • Enabling search globally "because it's useful." Per-agent toggles exist precisely so the sensitive agents stay sealed. Default off; enable with intent.
  • Not telling the client queries leave the network. They'll discover it in a firewall log and wonder what else you didn't mention. Disclose, document, diagram.
  • Blaming the model for bad search answers. Garbage results in, garbage answer out — scope the PSE, teach query phrasing, show users the toggle.
Knowledge check
"Web search is just RAG" — defend that claim in one sentence.
Answer: Results are fetched, text-extracted, and injected into context exactly like retrieved KB chunks — the corpus is simply the live web instead of stored documents.
What exactly leaves the network when a search-enabled agent answers, and what doesn't?
Answer: Only the search query (possibly model-condensed) goes out; documents, KBs, and the conversation stay local — the model runs on-premises throughout.
When is Google PSE worth the setup over DuckDuckGo?
Answer: When you need result quality at volume or — the killer feature — site-scoped search restricted to sources the client trusts (regulators, their own docs).
5.5 — Multi-User Access
Learning objectives
  • Administer users: roles, pending approval, groups, and what each role can touch
  • Map departments to groups, model visibility, and KB access — isolation by design
  • Use admin oversight features honestly: what to monitor, what to tell staff

One user is a demo; twenty-five users with different jobs, permissions, and sensitivities is a deployment. Open WebUI's multi-user system is how a single box serves a whole office safely — and configuring it well is most of the difference between Phase 1 and Phase 2 work (Module 8 scales these exact ideas).

Roles are three rungs. Admin — full control: settings, connections, users, every agent and KB; this is you, and at handoff (Module 7) one trained client champion. User — the population: they chat with agents made visible to them, create their own chats and folders, and (policy decision) may build personal KBs from documents they upload. Pending — the holding pen for new signups when you set registration to require approval (Lesson 5.1); nobody touches a model until an admin promotes them. The operational pattern for onboarding a 25-person office without chaos: bulk-create accounts yourself or temporarily open signup with required approval, promote in batches by department, then lock signup again.

Groups turn roles into architecture. Create one group per department — sales, support, legal, HR, marketing — and grant per-group: which agents are visible, which knowledge bases are accessible, even which raw models appear in the picker. This is where Lesson 5.2's collection design and 5.3's agent portfolio snap together into an access-control matrix: legal's contract analyst (and the contracts KB behind it) simply does not exist in a sales rep's interface. Note what this is and isn't: it's solid application-layer isolation — enforced by Open WebUI — which is the right tool for "support shouldn't browse HR policies." For genuinely hostile separation (subsidiaries, regulated walls), Module 8 adds OS- and network-layer separation on top. Defense in depth begins with knowing which layer you're standing on.

Oversight is a feature and an ethics conversation in one. As admin you can see usage patterns and configure what's logged; Open WebUI keeps users' chat histories in the volume (which you back up — Module 8 — so the backups contain conversations, a fact with Module 9 compliance weight). The professional posture: monitor aggregates for capacity planning and adoption (which agents get used? is 9 AM melting the box? — feeding Lesson 4.3's concurrency math), reserve content review for stated policies (compliance review in regulated shops, incident investigation), and — non-negotiable — tell staff what is logged and who can read it, in writing, during Module 7 training. Surveillance discovered is trust destroyed, and trust is the adoption engine. A one-paragraph acceptable-use note, co-written with the client, costs ten minutes and prevents the worst meeting of your engagement.

Walkthrough — a 25-person office as an access matrix 
GROUP      AGENTS VISIBLE                    KBs REACHABLE
sales      Proposal-Drafter, CompanyFAQ      proposals-past, pricing, faq
support    Pearl (product docs), CompanyFAQ  product-docs, faq
legal      Contract-Analyst, CompanyFAQ      contracts-active, faq
hr         Policy-Navigator, CompanyFAQ      policies-hr, faq
marketing  BrandVoice, CompanyFAQ            brand-assets, faq

Signup: approval-required · default role: pending
Admins: you + 1 trained client champion (named in handoff doc)
Logged: usage aggregates (all) · content review: compliance only, disclosed
Staff note: "Chats are stored on OUR server, never sent to any AI company.
Admins see usage stats; content is reviewed only per the attached policy."

KEY TERMS: role (admin/user/pending) · approval-required signup · group · model visibility · access-control matrix · application-layer isolation · acceptable-use policy

Common mistakes
  • Everyone's an admin "for now." Admin sees and changes everything; "for now" calcifies. One champion, trained, named — everyone else is a user.
  • Building agents before groups. Retrofitting isolation onto a flat deployment means re-auditing every agent-KB pairing. Matrix first, then build into it.
  • Silent logging. Undisclosed monitoring, once discovered, kills the deployment socially even when it's technically fine. Disclose in writing at training.
Knowledge check
What can a "pending" user do, and why is that the right signup default for offices?
Answer: Nothing until promoted — so an open registration page never becomes an open door; the admin approves each human deliberately.
How do groups, agents, and KBs combine into department isolation?
Answer: Groups gate which agents are visible and which KBs are reachable; an agent's KB attachments bound what it can retrieve — so sales literally has no path to HR content.
What's the professional monitoring posture in three clauses?
Answer: Aggregates for capacity/adoption, content only under a stated policy, and full written disclosure to staff at training.
5.6 — Advanced: TypingMind as Alternative
Learning objectives
  • Position TypingMind in the toolbox: what it is, what it isn't
  • Connect providers and local backends; use plugins, projects, and training files
  • Apply a decision rule: when TypingMind beats Open WebUI for a given client

Open WebUI is your default interface — self-hosted, multi-user, free. But a one-tool consultant is a hammer salesman. TypingMind is the strongest alternative interface, and knowing it earns you two things: a better fit for certain clients, and the credibility of having chosen your default rather than defaulted to it.

TypingMind is a commercial chat frontend (one-time license or subscription; a self-hosted edition exists for teams) with a design philosophy of bring-your-own-everything: you connect API keys for any provider — OpenAI, Anthropic, Google — and, crucially for you, any OpenAI-compatible endpoint, which after Lesson 4.1 means your Ollama box. Its interface polish is its calling card: side-by-side model comparison in one chat, beautiful organization, fast search across history. Its plugin system adds tool use to any connected model — web search, image generation, code execution, HTTP calls to arbitrary APIs (a lightweight cousin of the agent tooling from Lesson 2.8). Projects (folders with shared instructions and files) play the role Open WebUI's workspaces play; training files are its lighter take on knowledge — documents attached to an agent's context rather than a full chunk-embed-retrieve pipeline. That distinction matters: for a 30-page handbook, stuffing-into-context works fine and skips RAG tuning entirely (and with 2.4's million-token contexts, "just put it in context" covers more than it used to); for 5,000 pages, you need real retrieval — Open WebUI's lane.

So when does TypingMind win? Pattern one: the solo professional or tiny team on cloud models — a lawyer or marketer juggling GPT/Claude/Gemini by API key wants TypingMind's UX, has no server, needs no user management; selling them an Open WebUI deployment is overkill, and the consultant who right-sizes earns the referral. Pattern two: the hybrid power user — frontier cloud models AND your local Ollama in one picker, with the per-chat choice making the data boundary visceral (this chat goes to the cloud; this one stays home — Lesson 4.4's hybrid principle in interface form). Pattern three: the comparison shopper — side-by-side model output is a sales demo that closes deals on its own. When does Open WebUI win? Everything Phase 1+: multi-user roles and groups (5.5), real RAG at document scale (5.2), full self-hosting with no license dependency, the volume/backup story, and zero per-seat cost across 25 staff.

The meta-lesson — and write this one down — is that the interface layer is swappable by design. Because everything underneath speaks the OpenAI-compatible standard (the drum this course keeps beating: Lessons 3.3, 4.1, 4.5), TypingMind, Open WebUI, and whatever wins next year are skins over the same engine room. Your stack survives interface fashion. Demo both to technical clients; let them feel that the sovereignty lives in the layer they own, not the chrome they click.

Walkthrough — TypingMind pointed at your Mac 
TypingMind → Settings → Custom Models / API Endpoints → Add:
  name:      "Local Gemma (office Mac)"
  endpoint:  http://192.168.1.50:11434/v1     ← the Ollama box (Module 8 IP)
  api key:   (none)
  model id:  gemma4:12b

New chat → model picker now shows:
  GPT-5.x (cloud, $)  ·  Claude (cloud, $)  ·  Local Gemma (free, private)

Demo move: same prompt → side-by-side panes, cloud vs local.
Client watches a $0, on-premises model hold its own. You didn't pitch;
the interface did.

KEY TERMS: TypingMind · bring-your-own-key · custom endpoint · plugin system · projects · training files vs RAG · side-by-side comparison · interface layer swappability

Common mistakes
  • Forcing every client into your favorite tool. A solo cloud-model user needs TypingMind-class UX, not a server build. Right-size or lose the referral.
  • Confusing training files with RAG. Context-stuffing dies somewhere in the hundreds of pages; if the corpus is large or changing, you need retrieval — scope honestly.
  • Letting the interface own the architecture. Keys, endpoints, and data flows you control; the frontend stays replaceable. Never build sovereignty on chrome.
Knowledge check
Training files vs a knowledge base — what's the mechanical difference and the scale boundary?
Answer: Training files are stuffed directly into context (no retrieval); KBs are chunked, embedded, and retrieved per question. Tens of pages → stuffing is fine; thousands → you need RAG.
Give the three client patterns where TypingMind is the right call.
Answer: Solo/tiny-team cloud-API users wanting polish without a server; hybrid users mixing cloud + local per chat; and anyone the side-by-side comparison demo will close.
Why can you swap interfaces without rebuilding the stack?
Answer: Every layer beneath speaks the OpenAI-compatible API — Ollama, vLLM, cloud — so any frontend that talks that standard drops onto the same engines.
Lab 5A · RAG Knowledge Base

Build a real knowledge base from 50+ documents, then tune it with a fixed test set until retrieval is provably good.

  1. Assemble a corpus: 50+ real documents (your own files, or public docs — a city's policy PDFs, an open-source project's docs). Clean it: delete duplicates/drafts, confirm PDFs have text layers.Expected: a curated folder; you can say why each file belongs.
  2. Create KB "lab-corpus" in Open WebUI (Workspace → Knowledge), upload, note chunk count.Expected: processing completes; chunks number in the hundreds+.
  3. Write a 15-question test set with known answers: 10 direct facts, 3 synonym-phrased (no keyword overlap), 2 trick questions whose answers are NOT in the corpus.Expected: questions + expected answers in a file, committed to your repo.
  4. Baseline: attach KB to a temp agent (temp 0.2), run all 15, score: correct / partial / wrong / hallucinated-on-trick.Expected: a baseline scorecard — typically 9-12 of 13 answerables, and watch what it does on the 2 tricks.
  5. Tune one variable at a time: top-K 4→8 (rerun), chunk size 1000→600 with re-upload (rerun). Keep the best config.Expected: a results table across 3 configs; you can explain WHY the winner won in Lesson 5.2 terms.
  6. Write the accuracy report: corpus description, configs tested, scorecards, final settings, and a "known limits" paragraph (what it still misses and why).Expected: 1-2 pages, committed — this becomes your client-deliverable template.

You passed if: final config answers ≥12/13 answerable questions, declines (not hallucinates) both tricks, and your report shows a one-variable-at-a-time tuning trail.

Lab 5B · Department Agent Design

Design and build the five-agent portfolio for a fictional company — the exact artifact Exam 5 times you on.

  1. Invent "Meridian Services" (or reuse a realistic company): one paragraph of context, five departments — sales, support, legal, HR, marketing.Expected: enough fiction to make design decisions defensible.
  2. Build five mini-KBs (3–5 documents each — write or scavenge plausible content: a pricing sheet, product FAQ, a sample contract, an HR handbook chapter, brand guidelines).Expected: five named collections per Lesson 5.2's segmentation.
  3. Write five system prompts using role–rules–format–escalation–examples. Vary deliberately: legal at temp 0.2 with citations mandatory; marketing at 0.9 with brand voice.Expected: five prompts, no two interchangeable.
  4. Create the five agents: right model per job, right KB per agent, welcome message + 3 starter prompts each.Expected: five working agents in the workspace.
  5. Create five groups and wire the visibility matrix (Lesson 5.5). Make one test user per group and verify: the sales test user cannot see the legal agent.Expected: isolation verified by login, not by assumption.
  6. Adversarial pass: 3 hostile questions per agent (out-of-scope, bait, injection-flavored). Patch prompts where they fail.Expected: a 15-row test log with pass/fail and your fixes.

You passed if: five distinct agents work behind a verified group matrix, and your adversarial log shows failures found AND fixed.

Exam 5 · Interface DesignPractical, 90 minutes: from a bare Open WebUI install, deliver a working deployment with RAG (one tuned KB), five custom agents with distinct prompts/models/temperatures, group-based access, and branding. Graded on the Lab 5A/5B rubrics.
MODULE 6

The Intelligence Layer

6h lecture + 6h lab
Sources: Skill Guides 1, 12 — GHL Intelligence, Local Mountain
6.1 — Automation with n8n
Learning objectives
  • Deploy n8n with Docker and build a workflow: trigger → nodes → actions
  • Wire an AI step into a workflow against your local Ollama endpoint
  • Add error handling and retries so workflows survive the real world

Chat is AI you talk to; automation is AI that works while nobody's talking. n8n (pronounced "n-eight-n") is the open-source workflow engine that turns your local models into unattended labor — and because it self-hosts in Docker like everything else in your stack, it keeps the sovereignty story intact end to end. As of mid-2026 it's on the 2.x line (note for old installs: 2.0 broke compatibility with some 1.x workflows — check migration notes before upgrading a client).

Deployment is the familiar ritual: docker run -d --name n8n -p 5678:5678 -v n8n_data:/home/node/.n8n docker.n8n.io/n8nio/n8n — port 5678, a volume for your workflows (Lesson 3.7 reflexes), interface at localhost:5678. Inside, a workflow is a visual graph: boxes called nodes connected by arrows, executing left to right. The first node is always a trigger — what starts the run: a webhook (Lesson 3.4 made flesh: n8n hands you a URL; anything that POSTs to it starts the flow), a schedule (cron: every morning at 7), an app event (new row in a sheet, new email), or manual (a test button). After the trigger, nodes do work: HTTP requests, data transforms, conditionals (IF nodes routing items down different branches), and hundreds of prebuilt app integrations. Data flows between nodes as JSON items — Lesson 3.1's dictionaries, visible in the editor after every test run, which makes n8n the best API-learning environment ever accidentally built.

The AI step is the move that makes this your intelligence layer rather than generic plumbing. n8n ships AI/LLM nodes (and a whole "AI Agent" node family) that accept an OpenAI-compatible base URL — so you point them at http://<ollama-host>:11434/v1, pick your local model, and suddenly workflows can read, judge, extract, classify, draft, and summarize at zero marginal cost (Lesson 2.4 economics — which matters enormously here, because automations run hundreds of times a day; this exact workflow on cloud APIs would carry a permanent meter). Prompting inside workflows obeys everything from Lab 2A, plus one production rule: demand structured output. Tell the model to return JSON matching a schema ("Reply ONLY with JSON: {\"category\": ..., \"urgency\": 1-5, \"summary\": ...}"), low temperature, and parse it in the next node — because downstream nodes need fields, not prose. When the model fumbles the format (it will, sometimes), that's not a crisis; it's the next paragraph.

Error handling separates demos from deployments. Three layers, all checkbox-easy in n8n: per-node retry on fail (with wait — exponential backoff from Lesson 3.3, now a setting); per-node on-error routing (continue on a designated error branch instead of dying — catch the malformed JSON, loop it back for one re-ask, then escalate); and a workflow-level error workflow — a separate flow that fires whenever any production workflow fails, messaging you or the client champion with what broke and which item it was processing. The professional baseline you'll be graded on in Lab 6A: no silent failures. A lead that arrives while the CRM is down must end up retried, queued, or flagged to a human — never vaporized. Clients forgive a workflow that breaks loudly; they fire consultants over data that disappears quietly.

Walkthrough — your first intelligent workflow (15 minutes) 
[Webhook trigger]            n8n gives you: https://n8n.local:5678/webhook/intake
   ↓ POST {"email":"...","message":"Need pricing for 20-user AI setup ASAP"}
[AI node → local Ollama]     base URL: http://host.docker.internal:11434/v1
   model: gemma4:12b · temp 0.2
   prompt: 'Classify this inquiry. Reply ONLY JSON:
   {"category":"sales|support|spam","urgency":1-5,"one_line_summary":"..."}'
   ↓ {"category":"sales","urgency":4,"one_line_summary":"20-user deployment ask"}
[IF urgency >= 4]
   ↓ true                          ↓ false
[Send email to sales lead]    [Append to review sheet]

Node settings everywhere: Retry on Fail ✓ (3 tries, 2s wait)
Error workflow: "ops-alert" → messages you with payload on any failure.
Total cloud cost of running this 500×/day: $0.00

KEY TERMS: n8n · workflow · node · trigger · webhook trigger · cron schedule · IF node · structured output · retry on fail · error workflow

Common mistakes
  • Letting the AI node return prose. Downstream nodes need fields. JSON-only instructions + low temperature + a parse check, every time.
  • No error workflow. The flow that "always works" fails on a holiday weekend and nobody knows until Monday's leads are gone. Wire the alert before going live.
  • Skipping the volume on the n8n container. Update day arrives and every workflow the client paid for evaporates. Same lesson as 3.7 — it just hurts more here.
Knowledge check
Name the four trigger types and one real use for each.
Answer: Webhook (form submission starts lead intake), schedule (7 AM daily report), app event (new email starts triage), manual (testing during build).
Why do local models change the economics of automation specifically?
Answer: Workflows run constantly — hundreds of AI calls daily. Per-token cloud pricing meters that forever; local inference makes the marginal cost zero.
What's the "no silent failures" baseline in practice?
Answer: Every node retries with backoff, errors route to a handling branch, and a workflow-level error flow alerts a human — no item is ever lost without a trace.
6.2 — CRM Integration
Learning objectives
  • Explain what a CRM is and why it's the natural home for AI-processed business data
  • Wire the canonical loop: lead capture → AI processing → CRM update → automated follow-up
  • Authenticate to GoHighLevel, HubSpot, or Salesforce with the right credential type

A CRM (Customer Relationship Management system) is a business's memory of every customer: contacts, conversations, deals in progress, tags, and the pipeline — the named stages a prospect moves through from "new lead" to "closed." GoHighLevel (GHL), HubSpot, and Salesforce are the three you'll meet constantly; structurally they're the same animal: contacts with custom fields, a pipeline of opportunities, and triggers that fire messages. Why does the intelligence layer care? Because the CRM is where AI output becomes business action. A model that brilliantly analyzes a lead in a chat window has produced... a chat window. The same analysis written into the CRM — as a score, a tag, a pipeline stage — triggers the follow-up sequence, routes the salesperson, and shows up on the owner's dashboard. The CRM is the socket your intelligence plugs into.

The canonical loop you will sell over and over: capture → process → update → follow up. (1) A lead arrives — website form, Facebook ad, missed-call text-back — and the CRM or form tool fires a webhook (Lesson 3.4) to n8n. (2) Your local model processes: qualify against the client's ideal-customer profile, score 1–10 with reasons, extract entities (company, budget signals, timeline), draft a personalized first reply. (3) n8n writes back to the CRM via its API: updates fields, applies tags, moves the opportunity to the right stage. (4) The CRM's own automation does what CRMs do — sends the sequence, assigns the rep, books the calendar. Notice the division of labor: n8n thinks, the CRM acts. You're not replacing their marketing automation; you're giving it a brain. That framing also keeps your scope clean — the client's agency can keep building funnels; you own the intelligence underneath.

Authentication is where Lesson 3.5 pays off, because the three CRMs span the credential spectrum. GHL's modern API uses Private Integration Tokens (PITs) — scoped, key-style tokens created in the location's settings: you select exactly the permissions the workflow needs (contacts write, opportunities write — nothing more) and store the token as an environment variable or in n8n's credential vault. HubSpot mirrors this with private app tokens; Salesforce leans OAuth (the full Lesson 3.5 dance) and rewards you for already understanding refresh tokens. Two integration realities to design around, both learned the hard way so you don't have to: field mapping is the real work — "the AI works in one day; agreeing which custom field means 'AI score' takes a meeting" — so get a field inventory from the client before you build; and APIs have gaps — for example, GHL's API historically allowed reading and updating pipelines but not creating them, so pipeline structure gets built by hand in the UI while your automation moves deals through it. Always test the specific operations your workflow needs before you promise them in a proposal.

One ethics-and-deliverability note that belongs in every CRM engagement: the AI drafts, configured rules decide what sends. Auto-sent AI text to a mis-scored lead is how a client's number gets marked as spam and their domain reputation burns. Start every follow-up automation in "draft for human approval" mode (Lesson 2.8's approval gates, commercial edition); graduate individual message types to full-auto only after the client has watched a few hundred drafts go right.

Walkthrough — lead intelligence, end to end 
[GHL webhook: contact.created] → n8n
   ↓ {"name":"Maria Lopez","email":"...","message":"20-user AI setup, ASAP"}
[AI node — local qwen3.6, temp 0.2]
   'Score this lead 1-10 for an IT consultancy (ideal: 10-100 employees,
    budget authority, urgency). Reply ONLY JSON:
    {"score":n,"reasons":[...],"draft_reply":"..."}'
   ↓ {"score":9,"reasons":["explicit project","urgency","size fits ICP"],...}
[HTTP node → GHL API]
   PUT /contacts/{id}   headers: Authorization: Bearer $GHL_PIT
   body: customFields: {ai_score: 9, ai_reasons: "..."}, tags: ["hot-lead"]
[HTTP node → GHL API]
   POST /opportunities  → pipeline "Seeker Journey", stage "Qualified"
[GHL automation takes over]
   tag "hot-lead" → owner notified + draft reply queued FOR APPROVAL
Elapsed: ~4 seconds from form click. The salesperson opens a scored,
researched, pre-drafted lead. That's the product.

KEY TERMS: CRM · pipeline · opportunity · custom field · tag · Private Integration Token (PIT) · field mapping · lead scoring · approval-mode sending

Common mistakes
  • Skipping the field-mapping meeting. You'll build against fields that don't exist or mean something else. Inventory first, build second.
  • Assuming the API does everything the UI does. Test create/read/update for each object your workflow touches (pipelines are the classic gap) before the proposal promises it.
  • Full-auto outbound on day one. One bad AI message to a real customer costs more trust than fifty good ones earn. Drafts first; autonomy is graduated.
Knowledge check
Why write AI results into the CRM instead of a report or chat?
Answer: The CRM is where action triggers live — fields, tags, and stages drive sequences, routing, and dashboards. AI output becomes business behavior only once it lands there.
What's the n8n/CRM division of labor?
Answer: n8n (with local AI) thinks — qualify, score, extract, draft; the CRM acts — sequences, assignment, scheduling. You add the brain, not a rival automation stack.
What is a Private Integration Token and why prefer narrow scopes?
Answer: GHL's scoped API credential. Narrow scopes mean a leaked token can only do what the workflow needed — blast-radius control, same principle as Lesson 3.5.
6.3 — Workflow Design Patterns
Learning objectives
  • Recognize the five patterns that cover most paid automation work
  • Choose the right pattern from a client's plain-language problem statement
  • Combine patterns into pipelines without creating unmaintainable spaghetti

After enough engagements you discover a comforting secret: businesses are combinatorially different, but their automation needs collapse into about five shapes. Learn the shapes and scoping calls become fast, estimates become accurate, and your n8n folder becomes a library of reusable templates instead of fifty one-off art projects.

Pattern 1 — Document processing pipeline. Trigger: file arrives (email attachment, upload folder, scanner). Shape: extract text → AI structures it (the fields this business cares about) → validate → write to system of record → notify. Invoices to the accounting system, resumes to the ATS, intake forms to the case file. The AI step is extraction + classification (temp 0.1, JSON-only — Lesson 6.1); the value is measured in hours of data entry deleted per week. Pattern 2 — Customer service automation. Trigger: inbound message (email, SMS, chat widget). Shape: AI classifies intent and urgency → RAG-grounded draft answer (the Module 5 KB, reused — your stack layers compound) → route: simple cases auto-send-or-draft, complex or angry cases escalate to a human with a summary attached. The design parameter clients fixate on — rightly — is the confidence threshold: what the AI handles alone vs hands off. Start conservative; tune with data.

Pattern 3 — Lead scoring and routing — Lesson 6.2's loop, generalized: anything inbound (leads, applications, vendor pitches) gets scored against criteria, enriched, and routed by score. Pattern 4 — Content generation pipeline. Trigger: schedule or content brief. Shape: source material in (product updates, KB articles, past posts) → AI drafts in brand voice (higher temp, the marketing agent's prompt from Lab 5B) → always a human-approval gate → publish via API (GHL social posting, WordPress, email platform). The gate is non-negotiable and clients respect you for insisting. Pattern 5 — Scheduled digest. Trigger: cron. Shape: gather from several sources (CRM stats, support queue, yesterday's orders) → AI synthesizes a brief ("what changed, what needs attention, why") → deliver to inboxes at 7 AM. Cheapest to build, weirdly beloved by owners — it's often the workflow that renews the retainer, because the decision-maker feels it daily.

Combining patterns is where engagements grow: the service flow (2) that spots a sales question hands off to the lead flow (3); the document pipeline (1) feeds the morning digest (5). Two rules keep composites maintainable. Rule one: small workflows, chained — each flow does one job and triggers the next (n8n workflows can call workflows); a 60-node mega-flow is undebuggable at 11 PM, five 12-node flows are not. Rule two: log the decisions — every AI judgment (score, category, confidence) gets written somewhere queryable (a sheet, a DB table, a CRM note), because the first month of any automation is a calibration conversation, and you can't calibrate what you didn't record. That log also quietly becomes your case-study data: "94% of invoices straight-through, 41 staff-hours saved monthly" sells the next client (Module 11).

Walkthrough — scoping call, pattern-matched live 
Client says…                                  You hear…
"We re-type every supplier invoice"        →  Pattern 1, invoices → books
"Support drowns Mondays; 80% is the same   →  Pattern 2, RAG on product
 five questions"                              docs, threshold routing
"Web leads sit for days, reps cherry-pick" →  Pattern 3, score + route
"We never post; marketing has no time"     →  Pattern 4, weekly drafts
                                              to approval queue
"I find out about problems on Friday"      →  Pattern 5, 7 AM digest

Five sentences → five workflows → a Professional-tier engagement,
scoped in one meeting, estimated from your template library.

KEY TERMS: design pattern · document pipeline · intent classification · confidence threshold · human-approval gate · content pipeline · scheduled digest · workflow chaining · decision logging

Common mistakes
  • Custom-building what a pattern already solves. If the request doesn't map to a pattern, decompose it until its pieces do — they almost always do.
  • The 60-node monolith. Chain small flows; name them; let each be testable alone. Future-you is the maintenance contract.
  • No decision log. Without recorded AI judgments you can't tune thresholds, prove value, or debug "why did it route my best lead to spam?"
Knowledge check
Name the five patterns and the trigger type of each.
Answer: Document pipeline (file arrives), service automation (inbound message), lead scoring (inbound lead/webhook), content pipeline (schedule or brief), digest (cron schedule).
Which two patterns require a human-approval gate by default, and why?
Answer: Content generation (outbound brand voice) and any service auto-replies early on — outbound mistakes are public and trust-expensive; autonomy is earned with data.
Why log every AI decision a workflow makes?
Answer: Calibration (tune thresholds), debugging (trace a bad route), and proof of value (the metrics that sell case studies and renewals).
6.4 — AI Agent Orchestration
Learning objectives
  • Explain the coordinator–worker pattern and when multi-agent beats single-agent
  • Route work across model tiers to optimize cost, speed, and quality
  • Design handoffs: what context each agent gets, and how results compose

Lesson 2.8 introduced multi-agent systems as a species; this lesson is the husbandry. The honest first principle: one agent is better until proven otherwise. Multi-agent designs add latency, cost, and failure surface — you reach for them when a job has genuinely distinct phases (extract → analyze → write), when phases want different models (the cheap fast one vs the deep slow one), or when one context window can't hold the whole job (Lesson 2.4's budget, now an architectural constraint). When those conditions hold, orchestration is the difference between a clever demo and an assembly line.

The workhorse topology is coordinator–worker. A coordinator — which in n8n can be the workflow itself (deterministic routing: an IF node deciding which AI node runs) or an AI planning step (the model decides what's needed) — decomposes the job and dispatches workers: focused agents with narrow prompts, the Lesson 5.3 specialist principle applied to pipeline stages. Extractor (small model, temp 0.1, JSON-only) pulls the facts; analyst (the reasoning model) interprets them; writer (the fluent model, brand-voice prompt) composes the deliverable. Prefer deterministic coordination when the steps are known — it's debuggable, testable, boring in the best way; spend the AI-coordinator complexity only when the path genuinely varies per job. A second topology worth knowing: generator–critic — one agent produces, a second (different prompt, often different model) reviews against a checklist before anything ships; it's the cheapest quality upgrade in the catalog and a natural fit for Pattern 4's approval gates (the critic pre-screens, the human sees fewer, better drafts).

Model-tier routing is the economics of orchestration. Your fleet has tiers even fully local: the 3–4B that answers instantly, the 12B daily driver, the 27B+ reasoner that's slower and hogs memory (Lesson 4.3's loaded-set budget — three tiers resident is a real planning decision). Route by difficulty: classification and extraction → small; standard drafting → middle; multi-step judgment → large. A useful refinement is the escalation cascade: try the small model first with a confidence check ("also return confidence 0-1"); below threshold, re-run on the big model. Most items are easy; the cascade gives you big-model quality at small-model average cost — and the same shape extends to hybrid cloud (Lesson 4.4): local handles 95%, the rare frontier-requiring case escalates to an API by explicit policy, with the data rules written down.

Handoffs are where multi-agent systems actually fail, so design them like interfaces (because they are): each worker receives only what it needs — not the whole conversation; a focused brief beats a kitchen-sink context for both cost and accuracy — and returns structured output validated before the next stage consumes it (the n8n parse-and-check node between every pair of agents). Log every intermediate result (Lesson 6.3's rule, doubled: in a pipeline, the log is also your debugger — which stage degraded?). And cap the loops: any agent that can retry or escalate needs a maximum-attempts number, because two agents politely handing a malformed JSON back and forth forever is a very modern way to melt a Mac overnight. Lab 6B makes you build all of this; the capstone (Module 12) expects it as habit.

Walkthrough — contract intake, three agents, three tiers 
[trigger: contract PDF arrives]
[1 EXTRACTOR · llama3.2:3b · temp 0.1]
   brief: text only → JSON {parties, dates, amounts, renewal, termination}
   validate: parse JSON, required fields present? → fail = 1 retry, then flag
[2 ANALYST · deepseek V4-Flash · temp 0.3]
   brief: the JSON + firm's 12-point risk checklist → JSON
   {risks:[...], severity:1-5, confidence:0-1}
   confidence < 0.7 → escalate flag for partner review (cascade)
[3 WRITER · gemma4:12b · temp 0.6]
   brief: analyst JSON only (never the raw 40 pages — context discipline)
   → one-page memo in firm letterhead voice
[4 CRITIC · qwen3.6 · temp 0.2]
   checklist: every severity-4+ risk mentioned? numbers match extraction?
   pass → file to DMS + notify partner   fail → one regeneration, then human

Logged at every arrow. Max attempts: 2 per stage. Wall time: ~90 seconds.
Partner reads one page instead of forty. ×300 contracts/year = the invoice.

KEY TERMS: orchestration · coordinator–worker · generator–critic · deterministic vs AI coordination · model-tier routing · escalation cascade · confidence threshold · handoff · loop cap

Common mistakes
  • Multi-agent as a default. Every added agent is latency, cost, and a new failure mode. One good agent with one good prompt wins more often than architecture-astronauts admit.
  • Kitchen-sink handoffs. Passing full history to every stage bloats context, cost, and confusion. Each worker gets a brief, returns a structure.
  • Uncapped retry loops. Agent A rejects, agent B regenerates, forever. Max attempts + human escalation on every cycle, no exceptions.
Knowledge check
What three conditions justify multi-agent over single-agent?
Answer: Genuinely distinct phases, phases wanting different models/tiers, or jobs exceeding one context window. Absent these, stay single.
How does an escalation cascade cut costs without cutting quality?
Answer: The small model handles everything first with a confidence check; only low-confidence items re-run on the big model — big-model quality on the hard 10%, small-model speed on the easy 90%.
Name three handoff disciplines that keep pipelines debuggable.
Answer: Minimal briefs (not full history), validated structured outputs between stages, and logged intermediates with capped retries.
6.5 — Monitoring and Optimization
Learning objectives
  • Monitor the three layers: hardware, services, and AI quality
  • Track the metrics that matter and set alert thresholds that don't cry wolf
  • Decide upgrade-vs-optimize with data, and run lightweight model evaluations after every change

The intelligence layer you've built now runs unattended — which means problems also happen unattended. Monitoring is how a one-person consultancy supports ten client deployments without living in their server closets, and it's the operational backbone of every retainer you'll sell (Module 11). Think in three layers, because each fails differently.

Layer 1 — hardware. The vitals from Lesson 3.2's ten-minute inspection, made continuous: memory pressure (the one that kills local AI boxes — a model plus bloated KV caches plus a fat Docker container tips into swap, and tokens/sec falls off a cliff), disk space (models are huge; logs grow; a full SSD takes everything down at once), CPU/GPU load, and temperature. On a single Phase 1 Mac, a scheduled n8n flow running df -h, memory checks, and ollama ps — messaging you when thresholds trip — is genuinely enough; you already own every skill it uses. Phase 2+ graduates to Grafana dashboards (Module 8.5). Layer 2 — services. Is each piece alive and answering? Liveness is cheap: a cron flow curls Ollama (port 11434), Open WebUI (3000), n8n itself (5678) every five minutes. The senior move is checking function, not just pulse: send a tiny real prompt through the actual model hourly and assert a sane reply within a time budget — processes can be "up" while inference hangs. n8n's own execution history is your workflow-layer log: failures, durations, retry counts, reviewed weekly per client.

Layer 3 — AI quality, the layer ops tools don't see. Models don't crash into wrongness; they drift into it — a KB update changes retrieval patterns, a model upgrade subtly shifts tone, a new document type confuses the extractor. Defense is the golden set: 15–30 fixed input/expected-output pairs per critical workflow (you already built one in Lab 5A — that test set is this, formalized), re-run after every change — model swap, prompt edit, KB update, Ollama upgrade — and scored against the baseline. Twenty minutes, mostly automated (a critic-model can grade outputs against expectations — Lesson 6.4's pattern, turned inward), and it converts "the AI seems different lately" from a vibe into a diff. Track alongside it the business metrics the client actually feels: straight-through rate (items handled with no human touch), escalation rate, and the decision-log accuracy you committed to in 6.3.

Optimization order when something sags — cheapest lever first: (1) prompts — tighter instructions, better few-shots: free; (2) parameters — temperature, top-K, num_ctx: free; (3) model swap — a newer same-size model often beats yours: an afternoon with the golden set as referee; (4) load shaping — keep-alive tuning, tier routing (6.4), staggering scheduled jobs away from 9 AM: an evening; (5) hardware — only now, and with data: "memory pressure exceeds 85% for 3+ hours daily and queues form at peak; the next tier costs $X" is an upgrade memo that approves itself (and is precisely how Phase 1 clients become Phase 2 projects — Module 8). The anti-pattern is the reverse order — buying hardware to fix a prompt problem. The monitoring stack exists so you never have to guess which lever you're actually pulling.

Walkthrough — the self-watching deployment (all tools you already have) 
n8n flow "vitals" (cron */5 min):
  GET http://localhost:11434/api/tags     → fail? alert "Ollama down"
  GET http://localhost:3000/health        → fail? alert "WebUI down"
  exec: df -h /  → used > 85%?            → alert "disk"
  exec: memory_pressure | grep -q critical → alert "RAM"

n8n flow "pulse" (cron hourly):
  POST /v1/chat/completions {"model":"gemma4:12b","messages":[...2+2...]}
  no valid reply in 30s → alert "inference degraded"  ← catches the hangs

n8n flow "golden" (manual; run after ANY change):
  loop 20 test cases → model → critic grades vs expected → score sheet
  score < baseline-5% → DO NOT SHIP the change

Weekly (retainer ritual): n8n execution history + decision-log accuracy
+ straight-through rate → 5-line client email. That email IS the retainer.

KEY TERMS: memory pressure · liveness vs functional check · execution history · drift · golden set · straight-through rate · escalation rate · optimization order

Common mistakes
  • Alert spam. Ten daily false alarms train everyone to ignore the real one. Alert on actionable thresholds; digest the rest weekly.
  • Monitoring pulse but not function. "All services up" while inference hangs is the embarrassing Tuesday. Send a real prompt through, hourly.
  • Shipping changes without the golden set. The model upgrade that "should be strictly better" quietly breaks the extractor's JSON. Twenty minutes of regression beats a week of mystery.
Knowledge check
What are the three monitoring layers and one failure unique to each?
Answer: Hardware (memory pressure → swap-speed collapse), services (process up but inference hung), AI quality (silent drift after a KB or model change).
What is a golden set and when must it run?
Answer: Fixed input/expected-output test cases per critical workflow, re-run and scored against baseline after every change — model, prompt, KB, or stack upgrade.
Recite the optimization order, and the rule it protects.
Answer: Prompts → parameters → model swap → load shaping → hardware. Cheapest lever first; never buy hardware to fix a prompt problem.
Lab 6A · n8n Automation

Build the three workflows that anchor most client engagements — with production-grade error handling, because that's the graded part.

  1. Deploy n8n in Docker (Lesson 6.1 command, volume included). Create an "ops-alert" error workflow first — every later flow points at it.Expected: a deliberately-broken test node triggers an alert message to you.
  2. Workflow 1 — document intake: webhook receives text (simulate with curl) → local AI summarizes to JSON {title, summary, category} → email/message the result.Expected: curl a paragraph in; structured summary lands in your inbox.
  3. Workflow 2 — form qualification: webhook receives {name, email, message} → AI scores 1–10 with reasons (JSON-only, temp 0.2) → IF ≥7 alert "hot lead", else append to a sheet/CSV.Expected: a hot message routes loud; a cold one routes quiet; both logged.
  4. Workflow 3 — scheduled digest: cron 7 AM → gather two sources (e.g., the lead log + a folder's new files) → AI writes a 5-line brief → deliver.Expected: trigger manually and receive a coherent morning brief.
  5. Harden all three: retry-with-wait on every external node, JSON parse-check after every AI node with a re-ask branch (max 1), error workflow wired.Expected: kill Ollama mid-run — items end up retried or flagged, never lost; the alert fires.
  6. Export all workflow JSONs to your portfolio repo with a README describing each pattern used (Lesson 6.3 vocabulary).Expected: three committed, documented, reusable templates — your library begins.

You passed if: all three flows run end-to-end, the Ollama-kill test loses zero items, and your repo holds documented exports.

Lab 6B · Multi-Agent System

Build a three-agent pipeline — extractor, analyst, writer — with tiered models, validated handoffs, and a critic gate.

  1. Pick a meaty input: a long article, a contract, or a quarterly report PDF (text-extracted).Expected: an input too big/multi-phase for one comfortable prompt — the 6.4 justification test.
  2. Agent 1 extractor — smallest local model, temp 0.1: pull entities/facts to a JSON schema you define. Validate the parse in n8n; one retry then flag.Expected: clean JSON from messy text, reliably across 3 different inputs.
  3. Agent 2 analyst — your reasoning model: input is ONLY agent 1's JSON + a 5-point analysis brief; output JSON {findings, severity, confidence}.Expected: findings that reference the extracted facts; confidence present.
  4. Agent 3 writer — your fluent model, temp 0.6: input is ONLY the analyst JSON; output a one-page memo for a named audience.Expected: readable, structured, nothing invented beyond the findings.
  5. Add a critic node (any model, temp 0.2): checklist-grade the memo against the analyst JSON (numbers match? all severity-4+ findings present?). Fail → one regeneration → human flag.Expected: seed a deliberate mismatch and watch the critic catch it.
  6. Log every intermediate to a sheet/file; run the full pipeline on 3 inputs; write a half-page on where it broke and what you'd cap/tune.Expected: a debuggable trace per run and an honest postmortem.

You passed if: three tiered agents compose through validated JSON handoffs, the critic demonstrably catches an induced error, and full traces exist for three runs.

Exam 6 · Intelligence LayerPractical, 2 hours: build a complete pipeline connecting Open WebUI (or its KB), n8n, and a CRM — webhook intake, local-AI processing with structured output, CRM field/tag updates, error workflow, and a decision log. Graded on the "no silent failures" standard.

"Put it in the client's hands."

MODULE 7

The Single Machine (Phase 1 Deployment)

4h lecture + 8h lab
Source: Skill Guide 12, Chapter 3
7.1 — Client Needs Assessment
Learning objectives
  • Run a structured discovery interview that surfaces use cases, constraints, and risk
  • Classify the client's data by sensitivity and derive deployment requirements from it
  • Produce the needs assessment document — the artifact every later decision cites

Every failed deployment fails before the hardware ships — at discovery. Phase 1 engagements are won by the consultant who asks better questions, because the assessment determines the machine, the models, the agents, the price, and the definition of success. This lesson gives you the interview as a structure, not a vibe.

Block 1 — the work itself. "Walk me through the three most repetitive text-heavy tasks in your week." Not "what do you want AI to do" — clients answer that with science fiction or shrugs. You're listening for Lesson 6.3 patterns: re-typing documents (Pattern 1), answering the same questions (Pattern 2), leads going stale (Pattern 3). For each task, quantify: who does it, hours per week, what a mistake costs. Those three numbers become your ROI math in the proposal (Module 11) and your success metrics at handoff (7.5). Block 2 — the people. Headcount and the real user count (rarely equal); technical comfort spread (the bell curve of "lives in Excel" to "fears the printer"); and — critically — who will be the internal champion. A deployment with no named champion is an orphan; identify and recruit this person now, because 7.4's training and 7.5's handoff are built around them.

Block 3 — the data. This is where sovereignty consulting earns its name. Inventory what the AI would touch and classify each source into three tiers: Public (website copy, published prices — no constraints), Internal (procedures, non-PII operations — keep local by default), and Restricted (PII — personally identifiable information — health records, financials, client privileged material — local only, with Module 9 controls and possibly compliance frameworks attached). Ask directly: "Are you bound by HIPAA, PCI, GDPR, or industry rules?" — many small businesses genuinely don't know, and "let's find out together" is a trust-building moment, not a weakness. The classification drives concrete switches you already understand: which agents get web search (Lesson 5.4's gate), what the backup encryption story must be (Module 8), whether the engagement needs the Module 9 compliance documentation tier — and therefore the price.

Block 4 — constraints and environment. Budget range (ask as a range with anchors: "deployments like this run $5K–15K all-in — where should we aim?"); physical environment (where does the machine live? locked room or reception desk? — a Module 9.1 question asked early); network reality (who runs their router? is there an MSP — managed service provider — whose territory you're entering? Make them an ally now or an obstacle later); and existing systems for Module 6 integration (which CRM, what email, what file storage). The deliverable — a 3–5 page needs assessment document: client profile, the three quantified use cases, user census with champion named, data classification table, constraints, compliance posture, and your preliminary recommendation (machine tier + model lineup + agent list). The client signs it. That signature is scope protection — when feature requests bloom mid-project ("can it also do payroll?"), you amend the document and the price together, like a professional.

Walkthrough — the discovery interview, condensed 
BLOCK 1  "Three most repetitive text tasks?" → quantify each:
         intake forms: 2 staff × 6 hrs/wk · quotes: 1 × 4 hrs/wk
         FAQ email: everyone, ~10 hrs/wk pooled · error cost: rework + churn
BLOCK 2  14 staff, 9 real users · comfort: 2 power, 5 fine, 2 anxious
         champion: Dana (office mgr, respected, curious) — recruited ✓
BLOCK 3  data: website (public) · SOPs (internal) ·
         patient files (RESTRICTED — HIPAA applies → Module 9 tier)
         → web search OFF on clinical agents · encrypted backups · BAA talk
BLOCK 4  budget "under $12K" · machine in locked utility room ✓
         network run by an MSP (intro call booked) · CRM: GHL · email: Google
OUTPUT   5-page assessment, signed Thursday.
         Preliminary rec: Mac Mini M4 Pro 64GB · gemma4:12b + gpt-oss:20b ·
         4 agents · 2 n8n flows · Phase 1, $9,500.

KEY TERMS: discovery interview · use-case quantification · internal champion · data classification (public / internal / restricted) · PII · compliance posture · MSP · scope protection

Common mistakes
  • Asking "what do you want AI to do?" You'll get fantasies. Ask what work repeats; you map it to patterns — that's literally the expertise they're buying.
  • Skipping data classification because the client waves it off. "We're not regulated" is a claim to verify, not a fact. One HIPAA surprise mid-deployment re-prices everything.
  • Unsigned assessments. No signature, no scope baseline, no defense against feature creep. The signature is the point.
Knowledge check
What three numbers do you capture per use case, and where do they resurface?
Answer: Who does it, hours/week, cost of mistakes — they become the proposal's ROI math and the handoff's success metrics.
Name the three data tiers and one deployment switch each tier flips.
Answer: Public (no constraints), Internal (local by default), Restricted (web search off on touching agents, encrypted backups, Module 9 controls/compliance tier — and a higher price).
Why recruit the champion during discovery rather than at training?
Answer: The champion shapes adoption from day one — input on agents, early testing, peer enthusiasm — and the whole handoff model (7.5) assumes a trained named human inside the building.
7.2 — Hardware Specification
Learning objectives
  • Choose between Mac Mini, Mac Studio, and custom Linux from assessment data
  • Calculate RAM from the target model set plus context, users, and growth headroom
  • Spec the supporting cast: storage, network, UPS — and write the procurement plan

Hardware spec is where Semester 1 becomes a purchase order. The discipline: derive the machine from the workload — never the reverse — and write the derivation down, because a spec you can defend line-by-line is also a proposal that survives the client's nephew who "knows computers."

The RAM equation drives everything (Lessons 1.2, 4.1, 4.3 assembled): take the model set from your assessment's use cases — say a 12B daily driver (~7 GB at q4) plus a 20B reasoner (~11 GB) — sum the simultaneously loaded set, add KV-cache budget for real context use (2–6 GB across active sessions; more if RAG agents run num_ctx 8K+), add OS + Docker stack overhead (~6–8 GB with Open WebUI and n8n resident), then apply the planning rules: usable unified memory ≈ 70% of total on macOS defaults, and buy 50–100% headroom over today's need — models grow, use cases multiply, and RAM is the one thing you cannot add to Apple Silicon later. Worked: 7 + 11 + 4 + 7 = 29 GB working need → ÷0.7 ≈ 41 GB minimum → 64 GB is the spec. That arithmetic, shown in the proposal, is what "consultant" means.

The machine tiers. Mac Mini (M4/M4 Pro class, 16–64 GB): the $600–2,200 Phase 1 workhorse — silent, sips power, fits a shelf; right for ≤10 users on ≤27B-class models. Mac Studio (Max/Ultra, 64–512 GB): the $2K–10K+ office server — the bandwidth tiers from Lesson 1.3 (546–819+ GB/s) and memory ceilings that hold frontier-scale MoEs (a 512 GB M3 Ultra single-handedly runs 400B+ models quantized — the fact that re-priced this industry); right for Phase 2 head-ends and heavy-RAG shops. Custom Linux + NVIDIA: when the assessment shows real concurrency or fine-tuning ambitions (Lessons 1.4, 4.5 triggers) — more performance per dollar at the top, more administration forever after; usually a Phase 3 conversation, not a Phase 1 one. Default honestly: for the classic 5–15 user office, the Mac Mini Pro-class with 64 GB is the best deployment box in the business right now.

The supporting cast separates installations from infrastructure. Storage: internal SSD 1 TB minimum (models 50–150 GB, Docker volumes grow, logs accrete — Lesson 6.5's disk alerts exist because someone specced 256 GB), plus an external drive or NAS for local backups (Module 8.4's first tier). Network: wired Ethernet, always — Wi-Fi serving an office is a support-ticket subscription; request a static LAN IP (or DHCP reservation) from the client's router/MSP now, because Module 8.3's DNS and access stories build on it. Power: a small UPS (uninterruptible power supply — a battery that bridges outages and conditions brownouts) sized ~2× the machine's draw; a Mac Mini pulls ~40–150 W, so a modest 600–900 VA unit rides out the flickers that would otherwise corrupt a Docker volume mid-write. Peripherals: none — the box runs headless (no monitor; administered via SSH and screen sharing, Lesson 3.2), which surprises clients and quietly demonstrates competence. The procurement plan closes the loop: line-itemed quote (hardware, UPS, backup target, cables, your margin policy disclosed or built-in), lead times, and who purchases — client-buys (cleaner warranty story; you spec, they click) versus you-buy-and-resell (markup revenue, but you own returns). Either way: prices locked with an expiry date, per Lesson 1.5's supply-chain reality.

Walkthrough — assessment → spec sheet 
From 7.1 assessment: 9 users · 4 agents (1 heavy-RAG) · HIPAA-adjacent
Model set: gemma4:12b (daily) + gpt-oss:20b (reasoning) + embedding model

RAM:   7.1 + 11.2 + 0.6(embed) + 5(KV @ num_ctx 8k×agents) + 7(OS+stack)
     = 30.9 GB working → ÷ 0.70 = 44 GB → next tier with headroom = 64 GB ✓
BOX:   Mac Mini M4 Pro · 64 GB · 1 TB SSD            ≈ $2,199
SPEED: 273 GB/s ÷ 11.2 GB ≈ 24 tok/s ceiling on the big model → fluid ✓
NET:   Ethernet to closet switch · static IP 192.168.1.50 (MSP ticket #482)
POWER: 700 VA UPS                                     ≈ $130
BACKUP:2× 2 TB external SSD (rotating pair, Module 8) ≈ $260
TOTAL HW ≈ $2,589 · client purchases · quote valid 21 days
Headroom story: 64 GB serves Phase 2 (25 users) with model upgrades — the
box outlives the engagement. That sentence sells the spec.

KEY TERMS: RAM equation · loaded set · headroom · Mac Mini / Mac Studio tiers · headless · static IP / DHCP reservation · UPS · NAS · procurement plan

Common mistakes
  • Speccing for today's need exactly. The 32 GB box that fit perfectly in June is the emergency migration in January. 50–100% headroom, justified in writing.
  • Wi-Fi "because the closet has no jack." Pay for the cable run. Wireless model serving is intermittent-mystery-latency as a service.
  • Forgetting the UPS. One brownout during a Docker write corrupts a volume, and the restore test you hopefully did (Module 8) becomes load-bearing. $130 prevents the worst Tuesday.
Knowledge check
Reconstruct the RAM equation's five inputs.
Answer: Simultaneously-loaded model weights + embedding model + KV-cache/context budget + OS-and-stack overhead, all divided by ~0.7 usable fraction — then headroom on top.
What assessment findings would push you off Apple Silicon to Linux/NVIDIA?
Answer: Real concurrency at scale (dozens of simultaneous users → vLLM territory) or serious fine-tuning plans — the Lesson 1.4/4.5 triggers; otherwise Mac wins on simplicity and watts.
Why does the box run headless, and what does that require from Module 3?
Answer: No monitor/keyboard — administered remotely, fewer attack surfaces and casual fingers; it requires solid SSH (keys, not passwords) and comfort in the shell (Lesson 3.2).
7.3 — End-to-End Deployment
Learning objectives
  • Execute the full install order: OS → engine → models → interface → users → KBs → agents → automations
  • Verify each layer before building the next — the smoke-test discipline
  • Leave the machine documented and recoverable, not just working

Deployment day is Semesters 1–2 performed as choreography. Nothing here is new — that's the point. What's new is the order, the verification between steps, and the professional reality that you're building on a client's premises, on the clock, with an audience. The sequence below is the same one Exam 7 times you on; internalize it as a checklist you could run tired.

Stage 1 — the OS (60–90 min). Unbox, update macOS fully (firmware included), create the admin account with a vaulted password (the client's vault gets a copy at handoff — it's their machine). Then configure for server life: Energy/Power settings to never sleep with restart-after-power-failure on; auto-login to the service account (debatable on laptops, correct for a locked-room server that must self-recover after outages — note it in the security doc); enable Screen Sharing and Remote Login (SSH), then install your SSH key and disable password auth. Set the hostname (northstar-ai), confirm the static IP from 7.2 actually sticks, and apply firewall basics (Module 9 deepens this; today: on, with only your service ports allowed). Install the toolchain: Docker Desktop, Homebrew, Git. Verify: reboot it once, now — confirm it comes back to a working, SSH-able state unattended. A machine that survives reboot at noon survives the power blip at midnight.

Stage 2 — the engine (30–60 min, mostly download). Ollama installed, OLLAMA_HOST=0.0.0.0 set persistently (Lesson 4.1), models pulled per spec — start the big pulls first and parallelize the waiting (this is when you do Stage 3's Docker pulls and write doc skeletons). Verify: the Lab 4A trio — ollama list, a --verbose benchmark per model logged into the deployment doc (your baseline for Lesson 6.5 drift detection forever after), and the OpenAI-endpoint curl from another machine on the LAN — proving both inference and network reachability in one shot. Stage 3 — interface and intelligence (60–90 min). Open WebUI via compose file (volumes, naturally), admin claimed, signup locked, Ollama connected (host.docker.internal — Lesson 5.1's trap, pre-disarmed), branding applied. n8n likewise, with the ops-alert error workflow created first (Lab 6A's discipline). Verify: a browser on the client's network reaches both UIs by IP; a test chat streams.

Stage 4 — the knowledge and the workforce (2–3 hrs). KBs built from the corpus the client prepped (you sent the curation checklist two weeks ago — Lesson 5.2's garbage-in warning, scheduled away); agents created per the signed assessment (prompts drafted at your desk beforehand — on-site time is for wiring, not writing); groups and the visibility matrix wired and verified by logging in as a test user per group (Lab 5B's habit). User accounts created with first-login passwords. The n8n workflows from the assessment (typically 2–3 Lesson 6.3 patterns) connected to live credentials — CRM PITs scoped narrow (Lesson 6.2) — and run against test data, including one induced failure to watch the error workflow fire. Stage 5 — the safety net (45 min). Backup job configured and — run it now — one full backup completed and restored once to prove it (Module 8.4's law, applied on day one); monitoring vitals flow from Lesson 6.5 installed and alerting to you; the security checklist (Module 9's Phase 1 short form) walked and signed. Then the golden set: run your 15–20 case test bank against the live agents, score it, file it as baseline. The machine is now deployed — working, watched, recoverable, and documented. What remains is people: training (7.4) and handoff (7.5).

Walkthrough — deployment-day run sheet (the wall card) 
□ 1 OS      update · service acct · never-sleep + auto-restart · SSH keys
            static IP · hostname · firewall on · Docker/brew/git
            ✓ VERIFY: unattended reboot → SSH back in
□ 2 ENGINE  ollama + OLLAMA_HOST=0.0.0.0 · pull model set (parallelize!)
            ✓ VERIFY: list · --verbose benchmarks → doc · LAN curl to :11434/v1
□ 3 STACK   open-webui compose (volume!) · admin · signup locked · branding
            n8n compose (volume!) · ops-alert flow FIRST
            ✓ VERIFY: both UIs from client PC · test chat streams
□ 4 BUILD   KBs (pre-curated corpus) · agents (pre-written prompts) ·
            groups + matrix ✓ login-test per group · users · workflows + creds
            ✓ VERIFY: induced failure → error flow fires
□ 5 NET     backup job → run → RESTORE-TEST · vitals monitoring → test alert
            security short-form walked · golden set run → baseline filed
Time budget: 6-8 focused hours. Everything pre-writable was pre-written.

KEY TERMS: service account · auto-restart · headless administration · smoke test · deployment baseline · induced-failure test · restore test · golden-set baseline · run sheet

Common mistakes
  • Building layer N+1 on an unverified layer N. The agent that "won't answer" at 4 PM is the OLLAMA_HOST you didn't curl-test at 1 PM. Verify, then build.
  • Writing prompts and curating documents on-site. Deployment day is for wiring. Anything writable at your desk was due last week.
  • A backup that has never restored. Schrödinger's backup is not a safety net. Restore once, on day one, while the stakes are zero.
Knowledge check
Why reboot the machine during Stage 1, deliberately?
Answer: To prove unattended recovery — auto-restart, auto-login, services returning, SSH reachable — while you're standing there, not during a 2 AM outage.
What does the Stage 2 LAN curl prove that a local test doesn't?
Answer: Network reachability — OLLAMA_HOST, firewall rules, and the static IP all working together — which is exactly what the Docker container and office PCs depend on.
Name the three "safety net" artifacts Stage 5 files away, and which lesson each serves later.
Answer: A restore-tested backup (8.4), a firing monitoring/alert flow (6.5), and a golden-set baseline score (6.5 drift detection) — plus the signed security short-form (Module 9).
7.4 — Client Training
Learning objectives
  • Run the two-session training model: all-staff basics, then champion deep-dive
  • Teach the four user skills that drive adoption — and defuse the standard fears
  • Leave behind documentation people actually use: one-pagers, not binders

The deployment works; now make it adopted. Unused AI is a refund conversation waiting to happen, and adoption is a training problem, not a technology one. Your model is two sessions and a leave-behind, designed around one principle from your own course experience: people learn tools by doing one real task, not by hearing features.

Session 1 — all staff, 60–90 minutes, hands-on from minute five. Open with the two sentences that defuse the room's twin anxieties: "This runs on that machine in the closet — nothing you type leaves the building" (privacy fear, answered with the literal box) and "It drafts; you decide — it's a fast assistant, not your replacement" (job fear, answered with the approval-gate design you actually built — Lessons 2.8, 6.2). Then everyone logs in (first-login passwords from 7.3) and does their own work: reception asks Pearl a real policy question, sales drafts a real follow-up, each person leaves having completed one genuine task. Teach exactly four skills, no more: (1) pick the right agent — match the specialist to the job (the starter chips you configured in 5.3 carry this); (2) ask with context — "rewrite this for a worried patient, 3 sentences" beats "fix this" (Lab 2A, civilian edition); (3) iterate — the first answer is a draft; say "shorter," "warmer," "as a list"; (4) verify and escalate — check citations (5.2), and when the AI says "that's not in my documents," that's it working correctly, here's who to ask. Close with the honesty slide: it can be confidently wrong (Lesson 2.5, in one plain sentence), long chats forget (2.4 — "new task, new chat"), and what's logged and who sees it (5.5's disclosure, in writing).

Session 2 — the champion, 60 minutes, same week. Dana from your assessment now learns the admin layer: approving pending users and resets, adding documents to existing KBs (with your curation rules — the most common ongoing task by far), reading the n8n execution list well enough to say "workflow X failed on item Y" in a support ticket, what the monitoring alerts mean, and — the bright line — what not to touch: model settings, agent prompts, Docker, anything in a terminal. Champion empowerment with guardrails is what makes your support tier sustainable: they handle the daily 80%, you handle the 20% that's actually yours, and nobody "fixes" the temperature to 1.5 on the contracts agent on a Friday.

The leave-behind is three artifacts, none of them a binder: a laminated one-pager per desk (the four skills, the agent roster with one-line "use me for…", the escalation line); the champion runbook (the Session 2 tasks as numbered click-paths with screenshots, plus "call Jason when…" tripwires); and a 10-minute recorded walkthrough (screen capture of Session 1's demo — onboarding for the next hire, who arrives in month three when you're not there). Then schedule the two-week check-in before you leave the building: usage stats reviewed (5.5's aggregates — who's thriving, who never logged in), quiet re-onboarding for the stragglers, and the question that mines Phase 2 work: "what do you wish it did?" Adoption compounds or decays from week one; the check-in decides which.

Walkthrough — Session 1 agenda card 
:00 The two sentences (privacy: "that box, this building" · jobs: "drafts,
    you decide") + 60-second tour of the closet machine. Demystified.
:05 Everyone logs in. Password changed. Home screen tour: the agent roster.
:10 GUIDED REPS — each person, their real work:
      reception → Pearl: actual policy question → check the citation
      billing  → insurance lookup → iterate: "as numbered steps"
      sales    → paste a real inquiry → draft reply → "warmer, shorter"
:40 The 4 skills named on the one-pager (they just did all four).
:50 Honesty slide: confidently wrong sometimes → verify citations ·
    long chats forget → new chat per task · logging disclosure (in writing).
:60 Q&A. Plant: "Ask the AI itself how to phrase things — it's good at that."
LEAVE: one-pager per desk · check-in booked for the 24th.

KEY TERMS: adoption · the two sentences · guided reps · four user skills · honesty slide · champion runbook · bright line · leave-behind · two-week check-in

Common mistakes
  • Feature-tour training. Demos impress and evaporate. One completed real task per person beats forty slides — design the session around their work.
  • Skipping the fears. Unspoken "is this watching me / replacing me?" kills adoption silently. Answer both in the first two minutes, with the architecture you actually built.
  • Training everyone identically. The champion needs admin depth; staff need four skills. One-size sessions bore the one and overwhelm the other.
Knowledge check
What are "the two sentences," and which design facts back each?
Answer: "Nothing you type leaves the building" — backed by local inference on the closet machine; "it drafts, you decide" — backed by the approval gates built into every outbound workflow.
List the four user skills.
Answer: Pick the right agent · ask with context · iterate on drafts · verify citations and escalate when the AI declines.
What may the champion do, and where's the bright line?
Answer: Users, resets, KB document additions, reading n8n executions, interpreting alerts — but never model settings, prompts, Docker, or the terminal. Daily 80% theirs, engineering 20% yours.
7.5 — Handoff and Support
Learning objectives
  • Assemble the handoff package: the documents that make the system the client's
  • Structure a support agreement with tiers, response times, and update cadence
  • Run the 30-day review that closes Phase 1 and opens the relationship

An engagement ends; a system shouldn't. Handoff is where you convert a project into owned infrastructure — and, commercially, where one-time revenue becomes recurring (the retainer economics your Module 11 pricing depends on). The deliverable is a package, a signature, and a calendar.

The handoff package — in the client's Git repo (3.6) and a PDF set for humans — has six parts. (1) System documentation: hardware inventory with serials and warranty dates, network details (IP, hostname, ports), the compose files, the model lineup with the day-one benchmark baselines (7.3), every agent's configuration — model, temperature, KB attachments, full system prompt. This is the "any competent successor could run it" test: documentation that protects the client if you're unavailable is precisely what makes them comfortable depending on you. (2) Credentials transfer: every password, key, and token — including admin — delivered into the client's password vault, not an email. It's their system; holding credentials hostage is both unethical and, as positioning, the exact opposite of the sovereignty you sold. (3) The runbooks: champion's (7.4) plus an emergency one-pager — "if it's down: ① power/UPS ② reboot it ③ wait 5 min ④ call." (4) The security checklist as-built (Module 9 short form, signed). (5) The backup and restore procedure, with the date of the restore you actually performed. (6) The training materials — one-pagers and the recorded walkthrough.

The support agreement turns "call me if something breaks" into a structure both sides can live with. Tier it: Basic (email/next-business-day, monthly update window, monitoring included — your floor, priced ~$200–500/mo for a Phase 1 box); Standard (same-day response, monthly KB refresh and golden-set run, quarterly review call — the sweet spot, ~$500–1,500/mo); Priority (defined hours-level SLA, includes small change requests — for clients whose business runs through the box). Be explicit about what's included vs billable: included — updates (the 3.7 pull-remove-rerun ritual plus n8n/Ollama upgrades, golden-set verified per 6.5), monitoring response, champion support, minor prompt tuning; billable — new agents, new workflows, new integrations, Phase 2 scaling. The update cadence in writing: monthly maintenance window (e.g., first Tuesday, 7 AM), what gets updated, the golden-set gate before declaring success, rollback plan. Monitoring (6.5) already alerts you — the agreement just names what you do about it and how fast.

The 30-day review closes the project formally: usage stats vs the adoption goals, the workflow decision-logs vs the assessment's quantified use cases — "intake time down from 6 hours to 90 minutes weekly; 240 leads scored, 38 flagged hot, response time under 5 minutes" — and the wish-list harvested at the two-week check-in, now triaged into tuning (included), additions (quoted), and Phase 2 signals (more users, more load, the 6.5 upgrade-memo data starting to accumulate). Leave with three signatures: acceptance of deliverables, the support tier selected, and — if you've done all this well — a referral conversation that doesn't feel like asking. The Phase 1 playbook is complete; Lab 7 makes you run all of it, end to end, against the clock.

Walkthrough — handoff meeting agenda (45 minutes) 
:00 The package, walked: repo tour → docs, configs, runbooks, baselines
:10 Credentials ceremony: vault transfer, verified live — "it's yours."
:15 Support tiers on one page → client picks Standard ($900/mo):
    same-day response · monthly update window (1st Tue 7am, golden-set
    gated) · monthly KB refresh · quarterly review · new-work quoted
:25 30-day numbers: intake 6.0h→1.5h/wk · 240 leads scored, 38 hot ·
    adoption 8/9 weekly actives (1 re-onboarded at check-in)
:35 Wish list triage: 2 prompt tweaks (done free, today) ·
    1 new agent (quoted $1,400) · "sales team wants in" → Phase 2 seed 🌱
:42 Signatures: acceptance · support tier · "who else should see this?"
:45 Done. Project closed. Relationship open. Invoice: paid in 6 days.

KEY TERMS: handoff package · credentials transfer · runbook · support tier · SLA · included vs billable · maintenance window · golden-set gate · 30-day review · acceptance signature

Common mistakes
  • Holding credentials "for safety." It reads as hostage-taking and contradicts everything you sold. Transfer everything; your value is expertise, not lock-in.
  • Vague support ("just call me"). Unscoped support becomes unpaid employment. Tiers, response times, included-vs-billable — in writing, signed.
  • Skipping the 30-day review. It's where value gets proven with numbers, wishes become quotes, and Phase 2 is born. Closing without it leaves the relationship — and revenue — unfinished.
Knowledge check
What test should the system documentation pass, and why does that help you?
Answer: A competent successor could run the system from it alone. Clients depend confidently on consultants who don't engineer dependence — it's the trust that renews retainers.
Name four included items and four billable items under a Standard tier.
