What signal says you're underpriced, and what's the premium-solo response?
Answer: A persistent waitlist. Raise prices 20–30% per quarter until demand matches calendar, narrow to the richest tier, let retainers carry the base.
Recite the productization ladder.
Answer: Delivered twice → checklist; checklisted thrice → template; templated → junior's job or sellable product. (Your course artifacts already walked the first two rungs.)
Name the three channel tiers and the core risk of the deepest one.
Answer: Referral (finder's fee), co-delivery (split scopes), white-label (invisible wholesale delivery) — whose risk is brand anonymity plus reputation exposure without quality hooks.
11.7 — Financial Management
Learning objectives
  • Price your time from a target income, backwards — and know your true project costs
  • Manage consulting cash flow: payment structure, the tax vault, and the runway rule
  • Cover the liabilities: entity, contracts, and the insurance stack nobody tells freelancers about

Consultants fail financially in boring ways: underpricing from guilt, tax surprises in April, one slow quarter with no runway, one contract dispute with no paper. None of it requires an MBA to prevent — just this lesson, applied with the same checklist discipline as a deployment.

Price from the target, backwards. Decide the income ($150K year one — the curriculum's own math), then count the hours honestly: of a 2,000-hour year, perhaps half are billable — the rest is the 11.3 engine, admin, learning (this never stops; budget for it). $150K ÷ 1,000 billable hours = $150/hour floor — not your rate (you price by value, 11.2), but your sanity check: any project whose price ÷ realistic hours falls under the floor is charity with paperwork. Project costing has a twin discipline: before quoting, tally true costs — your hours at floor, hardware (pass-through or marked up, but never absorbed), subcontractors, the support tail (the dirty secret of fixed-price work: month-two questions are real hours; the retainer exists partly to contain them), and a 15% contingency because deployments meet reality. The quote that survives this math is a price; the one that doesn't is a hope.

Cash flow is survival mechanics. The structure you set in 11.2 (50% to schedule, milestones above $20K, retainers auto-billed on the 1st) exists because consulting's killer isn't profitability — it's timing: profitable practices die waiting on receivables. Rules that keep you alive: deposits are non-negotiable (a client who balks at 50% to start is showing you the collections fight ahead — believe them now); invoices go out the day the milestone lands (every day of your delay is a day added to theirs); net-14 terms with a late clause you actually enforce once (word travels). Then the two accounts that make you un-surprisable: the tax vault — move 25–30% of every receipt into a separate account on arrival, because as a self-employed earner nobody is withholding for you, and the IRS expects quarterly estimated payments (April surprise stories are all the same story: the vault didn't exist); and the runway account — build toward 6 months of lean expenses, because consulting revenue is lumpy by nature and the runway is what lets you decline bad clients and hold prices in slow months. The runway isn't savings; it's negotiating leverage stored in a bank account.

The protection stack. Entity: an LLC (or local equivalent) separates business liability from your house — cheap, fast, and the floor below which professionals don't operate; as profits rise past ~$60–80K, the S-corp election conversation with an accountant typically pays for itself in self-employment-tax savings (have the conversation; don't DIY the answer). The accountant is your first hire, before any employee — a few hundred dollars a quarter to be unsurprised is the best retainer you'll ever pay; you of all people understand selling retainers. Contracts: the SOW (11.2) plus a master services agreement covering the scary clauses: limitation of liability (capped at fees paid — this clause is why a $12K project can't become a $400K lawsuit), IP ownership (your templates and tooling stay yours; their data and deployment stays theirs — mirroring the 7.5 sovereignty handoff), confidentiality both ways, and termination mechanics. Pay a lawyer once for the template; amortize it across every deal forever. Insurance, the part nobody tells freelancers: professional liability (errors & omissions — "your advice/system caused loss"; in regulated niches like the healthcare-adjacent clients this course steers you toward, mandatory in spirit and often in contract), general liability (you physically work on client premises — you will be asked for a certificate before some buildings let you in), and cyber liability (you hold admin credentials to systems containing restricted data — 9.3's custody made you a target worth insuring; many enterprise clients now require proof). Budget $2–5K/year for the stack; it is, precisely, the cost of being hireable by the clients worth having.

Walkthrough — one project through the financial machine 
QUOTE CHECK — "Starter Plus" at $14,800:
  your hours: ~52 (assessment→handoff, realistic) × $150 floor = $7,800
  hardware: $2,589 pass-through (client buys direct — clean) → $0 absorbed
  support tail est: 8 hrs × $150 = $1,200 · contingency 15% ≈ $1,350
  true cost ≈ $10,350 → margin ≈ $4,450 + retainer attach ($900/mo) ✓ QUOTE OK
CASH CHOREOGRAPHY:
  signing: invoice 50% ($7,400) same hour · received before hardware ordered
  acceptance: invoice balance day-of (net-14) · retainer auto-bills 1st
  on receipt of each payment: 28% → TAX VAULT (auto-transfer rule)
  quarter-end: estimated payment from vault · accountant reviews · no April
YEAR-ONE LEDGER HABITS:
  runway: $1,500/mo until 6-month floor · insurance: E&O+GL+cyber $3,400/yr
  every invoice, receipt, mile: in the books THAT WEEK (n8n can nag you —
  you build automations for a living; build one for yourself)

KEY TERMS: billable fraction · hourly floor · true project cost · support tail · deposit discipline · tax vault · quarterly estimates · runway account · LLC / S-corp election · MSA · limitation of liability · E&O / general / cyber insurance

Common mistakes
  • Pricing from sympathy. The client's budget is an input to scope, not to your floor. Below-floor work breeds resentment that leaks into delivery.
  • Spending the tax money. It was never yours — 28% vaults on arrival, automatically, or April writes the lesson at penalty rates.
  • Working uninsured in regulated niches. One E&O claim, or one building that won't admit you without a GL certificate, costs more than a decade of premiums. The stack is a business license in practice.
Knowledge check
Derive the hourly floor for a $200K target with a 45% billable fraction.
Answer: 2,000 × 0.45 = 900 billable hours → $200K ÷ 900 ≈ $222/hour floor — the sanity check under every fixed-price quote.
What are the two protective accounts and what does each actually buy?
Answer: The tax vault (25–30% on receipt) buys an unsurprising April and clean quarterlies; the runway (6 lean months) buys the power to decline bad clients and hold prices.
Name the three insurance lines and the trigger that makes each non-optional.
Answer: E&O (your advice/system causes loss — regulated niches), general liability (working on client premises — certificate demanded), cyber (you hold credentials to restricted-data systems — increasingly contractually required).
11.8 — Tools for Your Practice
Learning objectives
  • Assemble the practice stack: workspace, CRM, payments, repos, and your own Local Mountain
  • Drink your own wine: run your operations on the systems you sell
  • Keep the stack boring — pick once, integrate well, revisit annually

The final lesson before the capstone is the quietest: the toolkit that runs your business. The principle above every pick: your practice is your best demo. Every system you operate is either evidence for your pitch or against it — a sovereignty consultant whose own operations run on scattered free tools and manual follow-up is a cobbler's barefoot child, and prospects notice through the cracks.

Operations: Google Workspace (Skill Guide 8) — professional email on your domain (the single highest-ROI $7/month in business: you@yourpractice.com closes deals that yourpractice@gmail.com loses), Drive as the client-file system (one folder per client: assessment, SOW, handoff package — mirroring the 7.5 structure), Calendar with booking links (the 11.3 exposure-check goes self-service), Docs/Sheets for the collaborative artifacts. Yes, it's a cloud service — and a deliberate one: your data rules (10.6) apply to you too; client restricted-tier material doesn't live in Drive — it lives where you tell clients theirs should. Practicing your own classification table is itself training. Pipeline: GoHighLevel (Skill Guide 1) — the CRM you integrate for clients (6.2) doubles as yours: pipeline stages from "identified" through "retained," sequences for the 11.3 outreach and post-talk follow-ups, forms feeding webhooks feeding — naturally — your own n8n, with your own local model scoring your own leads (6.2's loop, pointed at yourself). When a prospect asks "do you actually use this stuff?", your answer is a screen-share away, and that screen-share has closed deals on its own.

Money: Stripe — invoices with payment links (cards/ACH; the friction you remove from paying you is measured in days-sales-outstanding — 11.7's cash choreography appreciates it), subscriptions for retainers (auto-billing on the 1st was a policy; Stripe makes it a fact), and payment links inside proposals for deposit-on-signature. Delivery: GitHub (Skill Guide 7 / Lesson 3.6) — one private repo per client (configs, docs, runbooks — the 7.5 package's living home), your template library as the productization vault (11.6), and the portfolio repo as authority asset (11.1). And the centerpiece: your own Local Mountain — the Mac Studio (or Mini) running your full stack: every agent you sell, you run (a proposal-drafter grounded in your past proposals; a research agent for client industries; the contracts analyst that reads your SOWs), every workflow pattern (6.3) automating your own operations — lead scoring, the Friday-note skeleton drafted from your commit log, the morning digest of your pipeline + monitoring alerts across client retainers (the 8.5 dashboards of every client you support, on one screen — that screen is the retainer business, visible). It's simultaneously your demo unit (11.4's Wi-Fi-off moment), your test bench (every update lands on your box before any client's — you are your own staging environment), and your honest teacher: the rough edges you feel are the support tickets you'll prevent.

Stack discipline. Pick once, integrate deeply, revisit annually (the 11.6 re-choice, applied to tooling) — tool-hopping is procrastination wearing a productivity costume. Wire the seams with the skills you already have: GHL→n8n webhooks, Stripe→bookkeeping, GitHub→client repos, everything observable from the morning digest. Total stack cost: roughly $150–300/month plus hardware you'd own anyway — against which it runs your marketing, sales, delivery, support, and finances. You've now built, end to end, the same thing you sell: a business that owns its intelligence. The capstone asks you to prove it once more, for a stranger, under grading. Go.

Walkthrough — a Tuesday on the practice stack 
7:00  Morning digest lands (your n8n + local model): 2 hot leads scored
      overnight · client-fleet dashboards all green · 1 invoice cleared
      (Stripe → books) · "NorthStar fee-schedule still missing (day 3)"
8:30  Exposure-check call (booked self-serve via Calendar link) — notes
      dictated → your CRM, sequence advances automatically
10:00 Proposal time: YOUR proposal-drafter agent (grounded in 9 past
      proposals + the new assessment) produces the 80% draft · you spend
      40 minutes on the 20% that closes · payment link embedded (Stripe)
1:00  Delivery block: client #6's new agent built · tested on YOUR box
      first (staging = your own Mountain) · committed to their repo
3:00  Friday-note skeletons auto-drafted from this week's commits (n8n) —
      you edit 8 minutes each, Friday-you says thanks
4:30  GHL: pipeline review — 25 outreach touches current · 2 SOWs out
      The whole day ran on the thing you sell. That's the pitch.

KEY TERMS: drink your own wine · practice stack · domain email · client-folder system · self-serve booking · retainer auto-billing · client repo · template vault · staging-on-your-own-box · morning digest · stack discipline

Common mistakes
  • Selling automation while operating manually. Prospects feel the gap in your response times and follow-up. Your own pipeline is the first deployment of your career — treat it like a paying client.
  • Putting client restricted-tier data in your cloud drive. Your own classification table (7.1) governs you first. Hypocrisy here isn't just risk; it's the pitch, inverted.
  • Annual tool-hopping. Every migration costs a quarter of compounding. The stack is boring on purpose — pick, integrate, master, revisit once a year with 11.6's discipline.
Knowledge check
Name the five stack pillars and one practice-critical job of each.
Answer: Google Workspace (domain email + client files), GoHighLevel (pipeline + sequences), Stripe (deposits + retainer auto-billing), GitHub (client repos + template vault), your Local Mountain (demo, staging, and the automation engine running it all).
What three roles does your own Local Mountain play simultaneously?
Answer: Demo unit (the Wi-Fi-off close), staging environment (every update lands on you before any client), and operations engine (your own agents and workflows running the practice).
Why does "drink your own wine" close deals, mechanically?
Answer: "Do you actually use this?" becomes a live screen-share of your own scored pipeline and automated operations — proof by existence, the only objection-proof evidence there is.
Lab 11A · Pitch Workshop

Develop and deliver the 15-minute pitch with live demo — then iterate it under feedback, because the second delivery is the real one.

  1. Write the position sentence (11.1) and build the deck skeleton: their problem (shadow AI + the opener) → the demo → three options (11.2) → proof (portfolio artifacts) → the ask.Expected: ≤10 slides; the demo is the centerpiece, not an appendix.
  2. Prepare the demo unit: your Open WebUI branded for a chosen niche, KB loaded with realistic sanitized documents, agent tuned, the Wi-Fi-off beat rehearsed until casual.Expected: demo runs flawlessly offline — tested twice, cold.
  3. Deliver to a practice audience (peer, spouse, camera — someone): 15 minutes, timed, including the five-second silence after Wi-Fi-off.Expected: a recording; you held the silence without flinching.
  4. Collect structured feedback: what confused, what convinced, where attention dipped, one objection they'd raise. Handle that objection live, unrehearsed.Expected: written feedback + your objection response, recorded.
  5. Revise and re-deliver to a different listener. Compare recordings: tighter open? cleaner demo narration? better silence?Expected: visible improvement between takes — the iteration habit installed.

You passed if: two recorded deliveries exist, the demo ran offline both times, feedback was collected and visibly incorporated, and one cold objection was handled with an 11.4-grade response.

Lab 11B · Proposal Workshop

Write the complete proposal package for a fictional client — the document set Exam 11 grades and the capstone requires.

  1. Choose a client scenario from your niche (reuse Lab 5B's company or invent richer). Write the one-page assessment summary with three quantified use cases (7.1 discipline).Expected: every later number traces to this page.
  2. Build the three-option proposal (11.2's anatomy): scoped deliverables as countable nouns, anchored prices, the engineered middle, hardware passed through, retainer attached.Expected: a B option you'd genuinely defend, priced above your hourly floor (11.7 check shown).
  3. Write the SOW for option B: deliverables, exclusions, client obligations with dependency language, acceptance checklist (Exam-7 style), payment terms, amendment clause.Expected: the quiet sections (exclusions, dependencies) are present and specific.
  4. Add the architecture overview (one page + diagram, 10.1 labeling standards, scaled to the deal size) and a timeline with milestones and the Friday-note cadence named.Expected: a non-technical owner could follow it; a technical reviewer couldn't fault it.
  5. Run the financial check (11.7): true-cost tally, margin, support-tail estimate, contingency. Attach it as your private appendix.Expected: the quote survives the floor math — shown, not asserted.
  6. Peer review: one reader plays a skeptical owner ("too expensive"; "our IT guy could do this") — write your two responses into a final objections appendix. Commit the package to your portfolio.Expected: a complete, defensible proposal package — your reusable master template.

You passed if: the package contains assessment summary, three-option proposal, full SOW with quiet sections, architecture page, timeline, private financial check, and objection responses — all internally consistent and committed.

Exam 11 · Business Readiness AssessmentComprehensive review: deliver the Lab 11A pitch live (demo included), defend the Lab 11B proposal under questioning (pricing justification, scope challenges, two cold objections), and present your pipeline plan (11.3's cadence) and financial setup (11.7's accounts and protections). Pass = an examiner would hire you.
MODULE 12

Capstone — Live Deployment

20+ hours, self-directed with mentorship
12.1 — The Engagement: Finding and Framing Your Capstone Client
Learning objectives
  • Secure a real capstone client — or construct a rigorous simulated engagement
  • Frame the engagement honestly: what the client gets, what you get, what's graded
  • Scope the capstone to be completable, impressive, and true to the Phase 1–2 playbook

The capstone is not a test about the work — it is the work: a complete engagement, from discovery interview to handed-off system, performed for a real organization wherever possible. Every artifact you produce here doubles as your first portfolio case study and, frequently, your first paying client. Graduates regularly convert their capstone into their launch — design yours so it can.

Finding the client. The best capstone client is a small organization you already have a warm path to: your employer's department, a family business, your church or nonprofit, the gym owner who knows you. The pitch is the honest one: "I'm completing a professional certification in private AI systems. I'll deploy a complete system for you — normally a $5,000–15,000 engagement — at no charge (or hardware-cost-only), in exchange for being a real client: real meetings, real documents, real training, and a reference if you're happy." That trade is generous on both sides and almost always lands within three asks. Selection criteria: 5–25 staff (Phase 1–2 territory), at least one quantifiable repetitive text task (the 7.1 patterns), a reachable decision-maker, and a corpus of real documents they'll let you work with (sanitized where needed). Avoid for the capstone: regulated-restricted data on your first solo flight (HIPAA-adjacent is fine to design around; don't make a clinic's live patient records your training ground), and any organization where "free" means "nobody will show up to meetings" — a client with no skin needs a deposit of commitment instead: signed assessment, scheduled training dates, named champion, before you order anything.

The simulated alternative, when no live client exists, is legitimate but must be rigorous to grade equivalently: build a complete fictional organization (richer than Lab 5B's — 15+ staff with names and roles, an org chart, a real-looking corpus of 40+ documents you assemble from public/sample sources, a "decision-maker" played by a real human — a peer, mentor, or study partner who attends a real discovery call, makes real decisions, pushes back on price, and sits through real training). The simulation's discipline: you may not skip any step a real client would force — the discovery interview happens out loud, the training session gets delivered to actual humans and recorded, the handoff package gets reviewed by someone playing the successor. Half-simulated capstones (documents written to nobody, training delivered to a mirror) are the ones that fail review; the role-played meeting is non-negotiable.

Framing and scoping. Run the engagement under a real (if unpaid) SOW — Lab 11B's template, adapted: deliverables as countable nouns, client obligations with dates, the acceptance checklist. This protects the experience for both sides and produces graded artifact #1 before any hardware moves. Scope target: a full Phase 1 with at least two Phase 2 elements (e.g., department isolation + tested backup/DR, or monitoring dashboard + network hardening) — enough to demonstrate range, bounded enough to finish in 3–4 calendar weeks alongside life. The 7.2 hardware decision adapts to reality: client-purchased is ideal (they keep it — the gift that closes the reference); your own spare machine deployed on their premises-or-simulated-network is acceptable; pure-localhost-on-your-laptop is not (the network, training, and handoff steps must be real). Finally, set the grading frame with your mentor/reviewer at kickoff: share the deliverables checklist (12.2) and the rubric (12.3) before you begin, because professionals are graded against criteria they saw coming — that's the standard you'll hold for clients, applied to yourself.

Walkthrough — capstone kickoff, real-client version 
CLIENT   "Ridgeline Property Mgmt" — 11 staff · warm path: cousin's employer
TRADE    full Starter deployment, hardware-at-cost ($2,100, they keep it)
         ↔ real engagement + reference + case-study permission (in writing)
SOW      signed (no-fee variant): 3 agents · 1 KB (≤150 docs) · 2 workflows ·
         training (all-staff + champion) · handoff pkg · 30-day review
         client obligations: corpus by day 5 · champion named (Rosa) ·
         training dates booked at kickoff · decision-maker at 2 meetings
SCOPE+   Phase 2 elements chosen: encrypted backup w/ restore drill +
         vitals monitoring w/ alerts (range shown, finishable in 3 wks)
GRADING  rubric + checklist shared with mentor at kickoff · evidence
         folder started day one (photos, configs, recordings, signatures)
WEEK 0   discovery call done (recorded w/ permission) · assessment drafted
         → the engagement is real. Everything from here is Module 7 muscle.

KEY TERMS: capstone engagement · warm path · certification trade · commitment deposit · simulated client rigor · role-played decision-maker · no-fee SOW · evidence folder

Common mistakes
  • Choosing an impressive client over a committed one. The enterprise contact who can't make meetings sinks more capstones than any technical gap. Commitment criteria first.
  • Letting "free" erase the structure. No SOW, no obligations, no acceptance checklist = no engagement, just a favor with hardware. Run the paper like money changed hands.
  • Scoping a Phase 3 fantasy. The capstone proves the playbook, not the ceiling. Phase 1 + two Phase 2 elements, finished and polished, beats half a GPU cluster every time.
Knowledge check
What makes a simulated capstone gradeable as equivalent to a real one?
Answer: No skipped human steps — a real person plays the decision-maker in live meetings, training is delivered to actual humans and recorded, and the handoff package is reviewed by someone playing the successor.
What's the certification trade pitch in one sentence?
Answer: A normally $5–15K deployment at no charge (or hardware cost) in exchange for being a genuinely engaged client — real meetings, real documents, real training — plus a reference if satisfied.
Why share the rubric with your reviewer at kickoff?
Answer: Professionals are graded against known criteria — it forces evidence collection from day one and mirrors the acceptance-checklist standard you set for clients.
12.2 — Execution: The Ten Deliverables
Learning objectives
  • Map each of the ten deliverables to the module that taught it — and to its evidence
  • Sequence the capstone across 3–4 weeks without losing momentum or quality
  • Build the evidence folder that makes grading (and your future portfolio) effortless

The capstone's ten deliverables are the course's table of contents, performed. Nothing here is new — that's the design. What's graded is the integration: every piece present, every piece consistent with the others, every piece evidenced. Walk them with their lineage.

The documents (deliverables 1–3). #1 Client needs assessment — the 7.1 instrument, signed: three quantified use cases, data classification table, champion named. #2 Hardware specification and procurement plan — 7.2's derivation shown (the RAM equation, the bandwidth estimate, the supporting cast), priced with validity dates. #3 Architecture document with network diagram — scaled to the engagement (this is a Phase 1–2 capstone, so two pages and one diagram with 10.1's labeling discipline: every arrow a protocol, port, and source rule — not Lab 10's twenty pages). These three must agree with each other: the spec derives from the assessment's models and users; the diagram matches the spec's machine and the firewall rules you'll actually write. Cross-document consistency is the most common silent failure reviewers catch — re-read all three in one sitting before deployment day.

The build (deliverables 4–5). #4 Complete working system — the 7.3 run-sheet executed: OS hardened, engine benchmarked, stack deployed with volumes, KBs from the curated corpus, agents per the assessment, workflows with error handling, the whole thing verified at each stage (your reboot test, LAN curl, isolation logins, induced workflow failure — each one photographed or screen-recorded for the evidence folder). #5 Security audit and hardening report — Lab 9's binder, right-sized: the physical one-pager, the firewall ruleset as built, secrets inventory, encryption verification (the backup drive that demands its key), and a red-team pass against your agents with findings fixed and re-tested. If your client's data classification surfaced a framework (9.4), include the one-page compliance mapping — even "PCI: out of scope by architecture" is a graded sentence.

The human layer (deliverables 6–8). #6 User training session, recorded — 7.4's two-session model; the all-staff session is the recording that gets reviewed (the two sentences, guided reps on real work, the four skills, the honesty slide). #7 Client documentation package — 7.5's six parts in the client's repo: system docs that pass the competent-successor test, credentials transferred, runbooks, the one-pagers. #8 Support plan and monitoring setup — the tiered agreement (even at $0, write the SLA you'd charge for), the 6.5 vitals/pulse flows alerting to you, backup running with the restore drill performed and stopwatched. The proof layer (deliverables 9–10). #9 The 15-minute presentation — next lesson's subject. #10 Peer review and instructor evaluation — your reviewer walks the rubric against your evidence folder; you receive it the way you'll one day receive a client's security audit: gratefully, with a notebook.

Sequencing and the evidence habit. Week 1: discovery, assessment signed, spec + architecture docs, hardware ordered, corpus requested (the dependency email — 11.5). Week 2: pre-writes (prompts, one-pagers, training deck), staging on your own machine, then deployment day(s) on theirs. Week 3: agents tuned with the golden set, security pass, training delivered and recorded, monitoring live. Week 4: handoff meeting, signatures, presentation built from the evidence folder, review scheduled. The evidence folder is the meta-deliverable: one organized directory (mirroring the ten numbers) collecting every artifact as it's produced — the discovery recording, the signed assessment, benchmark logs, stage-verification screenshots, the restore-drill stopwatch, the red-team log, the training video, the signed acceptance. Populated daily, it makes deliverable #9 a selection exercise instead of an archaeology dig — and it is, byte for byte, your first client case study, which is the point of everything.

Walkthrough — deliverables → lineage → evidence, on one card 
#   DELIVERABLE                  TAUGHT IN   EVIDENCE THAT GRADES IT
1   needs assessment             7.1         signed PDF + discovery recording
2   hardware spec + procurement  7.2 (1.2-3) derivation shown · quote w/ dates
3   architecture doc + diagram   10.1 scaled labeled arrows = firewall reality
4   working system               7.3 (4-6)   stage-verification screenshots:
                                             reboot · LAN curl · isolation ·
                                             induced failure · benchmarks
5   security audit + hardening   M9 / Lab 9  binder + red-team before/after
6   training session (recorded)  7.4         the video + one-pagers
7   client documentation pkg     7.5         repo passing successor test
8   support plan + monitoring    7.5 / 6.5   SLA doc · alert test · RESTORE
                                             DRILL STOPWATCH (the favorite)
9   15-min presentation          12.3        deck + delivery
10  peer/instructor evaluation   12.3        rubric, walked vs this folder
CONSISTENCY CHECK before deploy day: docs 1-2-3 read in one sitting —
  same models, same users, same machine, same ports. They must agree.

KEY TERMS: ten deliverables · cross-document consistency · evidence folder · stage verification · right-sized security binder · competent-successor test · restore-drill stopwatch · case-study conversion

Common mistakes
  • Evidence archaeology in week 4. Reconstructing proof after the fact loses half of it. The folder fills daily, numbered 1–10, from the first recording onward.
  • Documents that contradict each other. The assessment says 9 users, the spec sized for 5, the diagram shows ports the firewall never opened. One sitting, three documents, before hardware moves.
  • Polishing deliverable 4 while 6–8 starve. A beautiful system with thin training and no tested restore fails the rubric — the human and proof layers carry 45% of the grade (12.3).
Knowledge check
Which single piece of evidence appears in almost every reviewer's favorite-artifact list, and why?
Answer: The restore-drill stopwatch log — it proves the backup, the runbook, and the RTO in one number, and almost nobody outside this course ever produces it.
What is the cross-document consistency check and when does it run?
Answer: Reading deliverables 1–3 (assessment, spec, architecture) in one sitting to confirm they agree on users, models, machine, and ports — before deployment day.
Map deliverables 6, 7, and 8 to their source lessons.
Answer: #6 training → 7.4 · #7 documentation package → 7.5 · #8 support plan + monitoring → 7.5 and 6.5 (with 8.4's tested backup inside it).
12.3 — Defense, Grading, and the CSAA
Learning objectives
  • Build and deliver the 15-minute engagement presentation that doubles as your sales asset
  • Understand the grading weights and pass the review like a professional
  • Convert certification into launch: the CSAA, the case study, and your first ninety days

The final deliverables are performances: present the engagement, stand for review, and walk out certified. Treat this lesson as choreography for all three — and notice, one last time, that every skill involved is one you'll bill for within the month.

The presentation (deliverable #9) is fifteen minutes, structured like the results memo from 11.5 grown a spine: the client and the problem (two minutes — who they are, the three quantified use cases, what was at stake); the design (three minutes — assessment → spec → architecture, with the diagram on screen and one honest tradeoff you made and why: reviewers probe tradeoffs, and "I chose X over Y because Z" is the sound of a professional); the build (four minutes — the deployment narrated by its verification evidence: the reboot test, the benchmarks, the induced failure, the restore stopwatch — show the screenshots, not slides about them); the human layer (three minutes — a sixty-second clip of the actual training session, the handoff package on screen, the champion's name and what she now owns); results and reflection (three minutes — the measured deltas against the assessment's numbers, what you'd do differently, and the one thing that surprised you — the reflection question is graded, and "nothing surprised me" is the only failing answer). Rehearse it twice, timed; the Lab 11A iteration habit applies. Then keep the deck forever: swap the client's name for "a regional property-management firm" and this presentation is your sales-call case study — deliverable #9's second life is closing your first paid deal.

The grading (deliverable #10) weights the whole professional, not just the technician: Technical execution 40% — the system works, verified at every stage, secure by the Lab 9 standard; Documentation quality 20% — the ten artifacts, consistent and successor-proof; Security compliance 15% — hardening evidenced, red-team run, classification respected; Client communication 15% — the recordings and Friday notes show a professional the client understood and trusted; Presentation 10% — the fifteen minutes above. Passing is 80%, and the weights tell you where capstones actually fail: rarely on the build (you've deployed this stack a dozen times by now), usually on the 35% that is documentation-plus-communication — which is why 12.2 hammered the evidence folder. In the review itself, your reviewer walks the rubric against your folder and asks the questions a skeptical client would ("why this model?", "what happens when the SSD dies?", "show me the restore"). Answer from evidence, concede gaps without flinching, and write down every finding — the review is your last free consulting audit; every one after this, someone pays you to survive.

The CSAA and the launch. Upon passing: AI Impact Pros Certified Sovereign AI Architect — the designation that certifies, in the only way that can't be faked, that you have actually done the entire thing: assessed, specced, built, secured, trained, documented, handed off, and defended a sovereign AI deployment. Put it everywhere (11.1's authority assets), but understand what it really is: a structured story backed by a folder of proof. Then execute the first ninety days, which the curriculum has already scheduled for you: convert the capstone client (the 30-day review you owe them is a retainer conversation — even a small one starts the spine); publish the case study (permission was secured in 12.1; the presentation becomes the written version — 11.3's content engine gets its first real fuel); ask for the two referrals (11.5's yield, now yours to collect); book the first talk (the presentation, again — one artifact, four lives); and open the pipeline cadence (11.3's weekly rhythm, starting Monday). The Graduate's First Year roadmap on this very site — months 1–2 build, months 3–4 first paid Starters, the course paid for — assumed exactly this launch sequence. You're no longer studying it. You're on it. They'll never see you coming.

Walkthrough — the 15-minute deck, slide by slide 
1  THE CLIENT      11 staff · 3 use cases worth $41K/yr (assessment p.2)
2  THE DESIGN      assessment→spec→architecture · diagram · "I chose the
                   Mini-64GB over the Studio because [shown math] — the
                   Studio is the documented Phase 2 path"
3  THE BUILD       evidence montage: reboot test ✓ · 26 tok/s benchmark ·
                   isolation logins ✓ · induced failure → alert fired ✓
4  THE PROOF       restore drill: 19 minutes, stopwatched · red-team:
                   3 findings → fixed → re-run clean
5  THE HUMANS      60-sec training clip · handoff repo tour · "Rosa now
                   owns users, KB updates, and first-line triage"
6  RESULTS         intake 5.5h→1.2h/wk · 100% staff activated ·
                   client quote on screen (permission on file)
7  REFLECTION      "What surprised me: the corpus took 9 days, not 5 —
                   my next SOW's dependency clause has teeth"
8  [closing]       the rubric, self-scored, evidence-linked — handed over
REVIEW Qs survived: "why not cloud?" (TCO table) · "SSD dies?" (drill log)
AFTER: deck → anonymized → sales asset #1. Same fifteen minutes, billed.

KEY TERMS: engagement presentation · honest tradeoff · evidence-narrated build · reflection question · grading weights · rubric walk · CSAA · capstone conversion · first ninety days

Common mistakes
  • Slides about evidence instead of evidence. "We tested the backup" is a claim; the stopwatch screenshot is a grade. Show the artifacts.
  • Defending gaps instead of conceding them. "You're right — that's in my next-time list" scores higher than any improvised excuse, here and in every client review after.
  • Framing certification as the finish line. The CSAA is the starting gun — the 90-day launch sequence (convert, publish, refer, speak, pipeline) begins the week you pass.
Knowledge check
Recite the five grading weights and where capstones usually fail.
Answer: Technical 40 · documentation 20 · security 15 · client communication 15 · presentation 10 (pass = 80%). Failures cluster in documentation + communication — the 35% the evidence folder exists for.
What are the four afterlives of the capstone presentation?
Answer: Graded deliverable #9 → anonymized sales case study → written content-engine piece → the lunch-and-learn talk. One artifact, four lives.
List the first-ninety-days launch sequence.
Answer: Convert the capstone client to a retainer at the 30-day review, publish the case study, collect two referrals, book the first talk, and start the weekly pipeline cadence.

05 · Drill the Concepts

Flashcards

Forty-eight core concepts across all four semesters. Click any card to flip it. Drill until every flip is boring — that's mastery.

M1 · Hardware
Name the five components of every computer.
click to flip
CPU (general brain), GPU (parallel math), RAM (workbench — models must fit here), SSD (filing cabinet), Neural Engine/NPU (low-power AI block).
M1 · Hardware
Why is Apple Silicon uniquely suited for local AI?
click to flip
SoC design with unified memory — CPU, GPU, and Neural Engine share one big pool, so the GPU can use nearly all system memory; plus high memory bandwidth, which sets token speed.
M1 · Hardware
The 4-bit memory rule of thumb?
click to flip
A 4-bit quantized model needs roughly (parameters ÷ 2) GB plus 1–3 GB overhead. An 8B model ≈ 4 GB; a 27B ≈ 14 GB.
M1 · Hardware
Estimate token speed from a spec sheet.
click to flip
Memory bandwidth ÷ model size in memory = tokens/sec ceiling. 273 GB/s ÷ 9 GB ≈ 30 tok/s. Real-world lands at 50–80% of ceiling. Above ~15 feels fluid.
M1 · Hardware
Why does NVIDIA dominate AI training?
click to flip
CUDA — two decades of software ecosystem lock-in — plus Tensor Cores, HBM memory (B300: 288 GB at ~8 TB/s), and NVLink fusing GPUs into one fabric.
M1 · Supply Chain
Fabless vs foundry — and the two chokepoints?
click to flip
Fabless (Apple, NVIDIA) design; foundries manufacture. Chokepoints: TSMC (~90% of advanced chips, Taiwan) and ASML (world's only EUV lithography maker).
M2 · AI Theory
What is the transformer, and which paper introduced it?
click to flip
Stacked blocks of self-attention (every token weighs every other) + feedforward layers. "Attention Is All You Need," Vaswani et al., 2017 — the engine of every modern LLM.
M2 · AI Theory
What are queries, keys, and values?
click to flip
Three learned projections per token: query = "what am I looking for," key = "what I contain," value = "what I contribute." Query–key matches decide how values blend.
M2 · AI Theory
What does backpropagation do?
click to flip
Traces prediction error backward through every layer and tells each individual weight which direction to nudge to reduce the error — repeated trillions of times = training.
M2 · Tokens
Token math: words → tokens, and why context costs money.
click to flip
1 token ≈ ¾ word (×1.33 to convert). The whole chat history is re-sent as input every turn, so long conversations cost more per message. Local inference makes marginal tokens ≈ $0.
M2 · Generation
Temperature settings cheat sheet?
click to flip
0.0–0.3 code/extraction/legal · 0.6–0.8 chat/support · 0.9–1.2 brainstorm/marketing. Top-p ~0.9 as the fence. Temp 0 = deterministic greedy.
M2 · Training
The three training stages before release?
click to flip
Pre-training (trillions of tokens, $10M–100M+ → base model) → supervised fine-tuning (instruction following) → RLHF (human preference alignment). You live downstream: inference + LoRA.
M2 · LLMs
Dense vs MoE — the consultant's sizing rule?
click to flip
Memory follows total parameters (all experts load); speed follows active parameters. Llama 4 Scout: 109B total / 17B active → needs ~55 GB, runs like a 17B.
M2 · Agents
The agent loop, and where security lives?
click to flip
Perceive → plan → act → observe. The model only emits text; the harness executes tools — so the blast radius is exactly the tools you granted. 0.95²⁰ ≈ 36%: gate irreversible actions.
M2 · Memory
The five-layer memory stack?
click to flip
This conversation → context. Identity/rules → system instructions. Reference knowledge → RAG. Evolving preferences → state files. Permanent behavior → fine-tuning.
M3 · APIs
Status codes that matter: 200, 401, 429, 500?
click to flip
200 success · 401 bad/missing key · 429 rate-limited (exponential backoff: 1s, 2s, 4s) · 5xx their server broke. 4xx = your fault; 5xx = theirs.
M3 · APIs
Why is the OpenAI-compatible format strategically vital?
click to flip
Ollama (11434), vLLM (8000), and the cloud all serve the same /v1/chat/completions shape — swap cloud for local by changing one URL. That URL swap is the sovereignty migration path.
M3 · Webhooks
Webhook vs polling, and the three hardening rules?
click to flip
Webhook = push (they POST you on events) vs polling = pull. Harden: verify signatures, process idempotently (events arrive twice), respond 200 fast, work after.
M3 · Git/Docker
The two unforgiving laws of Git and Docker?
click to flip
Git never forgets — a committed secret is compromised forever (rotate first, .gitignore before first commit). Containers are disposable — data survives only in volumes.
M4 · Ollama
The four Ollama verbs and the two ports of the stack?
click to flip
pull · run · list · rm. Ollama API: 11434; Open WebUI: 3000 (n8n: 5678, vLLM: 8000). OLLAMA_HOST=0.0.0.0 to serve the network — always with firewall rules.
M4 · Models
The five-question model selection checklist?
click to flip
Fit (memory at q4) → license (Apache/MIT clean; Llama: read it) → task strength (Qwen code, DeepSeek reasoning, Gemma general) → speed (active params vs bandwidth) → your own evidence.
M4 · Models
What flipped in open-model licensing in 2025–26?
click to flip
Permissive won: Gemma 4 → Apache 2.0, DeepSeek MIT, Qwen/Mistral Apache, OpenAI's gpt-oss Apache. Meta's Llama community license became the restrictive outlier.
M4 · Ops
The three benchmark metrics and what drives each?
click to flip
Load time (SSD→RAM) · time to first token (prompt reading — grows with input) · eval tok/s (bandwidth ÷ size). From ollama run --verbose; file as the client baseline.
M4 · vLLM
vLLM's two superpowers?
click to flip
Continuous batching (interleaves many generations — GPU never idles, hundreds of concurrent users) + PagedAttention (KV-cache in on-demand pages). Plus tensor parallelism across GPUs.
M5 · RAG
The five RAG pipeline stages?
click to flip
Chunk (500–1500 tok + overlap) → embed (meaning-vectors) → store (vector DB) → retrieve (top-K nearest) → generate (chunks + question in context, cited).
M5 · RAG
RAG symptom → tuning move?
click to flip
Missing known facts → raise top-K or shrink chunks. Right but context-starved → bigger chunks/overlap. Slow/rambling → lower top-K. One variable at a time, fixed test set.
M5 · Agents
The production system-prompt pattern?
click to flip
Role → rules (do and don't) → format → escalation paths → worked examples. An employee handbook for a very fast, very literal new hire. Test adversarially before shipping.
M5 · Access
How do groups + agents + KBs create isolation?
click to flip
Groups gate which agents are visible; agents' KB attachments bound what's retrievable — sales has no path to HR content. Application-layer isolation: stops colleagues, not admins (that needs OS/network layers).
M6 · n8n
The "no silent failures" standard?
click to flip
Every external node retries with backoff · AI outputs are JSON-validated with a re-ask branch · a workflow-level error workflow alerts a human. No item is ever lost without a trace.
M6 · Patterns
The five workflow design patterns?
click to flip
Document pipeline (file arrives) · service automation (inbound message) · lead scoring (webhook) · content pipeline (schedule + human gate) · digest (cron). Most client asks map to these.
M6 · Orchestration
When does multi-agent beat single-agent?
click to flip
Only when: distinct phases, phases wanting different model tiers, or the job exceeds one context window. Then: minimal briefs, validated JSON handoffs, logged intermediates, capped retries.
M6 · Monitoring
What is a golden set and when must it run?
click to flip
15–30 fixed input→expected-output cases per critical workflow, scored against baseline after every change: model swap, prompt edit, KB update, stack upgrade. Drift detection made cheap.
M7 · Assessment
The three data tiers and what each flips?
click to flip
Public (no constraints) · Internal (local by default) · Restricted (PII/PHI: web search off, encrypted backups, compliance tier, higher price). Classified at discovery, enforced everywhere.
M7 · Spec
The RAM equation for hardware spec?
click to flip
(Loaded model weights + embedding model + KV/context budget + OS/stack overhead) ÷ 0.7 usable → next tier up with 50–100% headroom. Show the math in the proposal.
M7 · Deploy
The five-stage deployment order with verification?
click to flip
OS (reboot test) → engine (LAN curl + benchmarks) → stack (UIs reachable) → build (isolation logins, induced failure) → safety net (restore-tested backup, alerts, golden baseline).
M7 · Training
"The two sentences" that open staff training?
click to flip
"Nothing you type leaves the building" (privacy — backed by the closet machine) and "It drafts; you decide" (jobs — backed by approval gates). Then four skills: pick agent, ask with context, iterate, verify.
M8 · Scale
The three Phase 2 trigger signals?
click to flip
Sustained memory pressure >80% at peak · rising P95 first-token / queueing (not averages!) · adoption growth toward headcount. Tune first (parallelism, tiers, load shaping), then the memo writes itself.
M8 · Network
Remote access: the one professional answer?
click to flip
VPN in, never ports out. WireGuard or mesh overlay (Tailscale-class), per-device keys, revocable. A port-forwarded UI is scanned within hours and kills the sovereignty story.
M8 · Backup
3-2-1, RPO, RTO — define all three.
click to flip
3-2-1: 3 copies, 2 media, 1 offsite (encrypted). RPO: max data loss (backup frequency). RTO: max downtime (recovery tier). An untested backup is a hypothesis — drill quarterly, stopwatch it.
M9 · Security
Zero trust in one sentence?
click to flip
Never trust by network location — verify every request. The LAN is just another untrusted network: scoped ports, per-person credentials, per-device keys, no shared logins.
M9 · Compliance
Match the five frameworks to their domains.
click to flip
HIPAA US healthcare/PHI · SOC 2 business trust audits · GDPR EU personal data · PCI DSS payment cards (architect it out of scope) · FedRAMP US federal cloud. You build controls + evidence; lawyers determine compliance.
M9 · IR
The incident containment golden rule?
click to flip
Isolate, don't destroy: pull the network cable, never wipe/reinstall — preserve evidence. Recover on the standby in parallel. Then the blameless post-mortem, sent unprompted.
M9 · AI Safety
The lethal trifecta — and how to break it?
click to flip
Private data + untrusted input + outbound channel in one agent = exfiltration risk. Break a leg: no web on data-rich agents, no restricted KBs on web agents, or human-gated outbound. Red-team quarterly; a model swap is a security event.
M10 · Architecture
The nines: 99.9% vs 99.99%?
click to flip
~8.8 hours vs ~53 minutes allowed downtime/year — and each nine multiplies cost. Price the client's downtime per hour; sell a point on the curve, not a slogan.
M10 · Fine-tune
The fine-tuning decision ladder?
click to flip
Prompt → RAG → few-shot → fine-tune. Survivors: style/voice at scale, structured-output reliability, domain dialect, capability compression. The work: 70% dataset, 20% eval, 10% training. Never fine-tune facts.
M10 · Knowledge
Hybrid search + re-ranking — why both?
click to flip
Vectors catch meaning; BM25 keywords catch exact strings (codes, citations). Then a re-ranker reads query+chunk together and keeps the best 5–8 — the highest-ROI retrieval upgrade. ACLs filter inside the search.
M10 · Hybrid
The four legitimate cloud lanes in a sovereignty practice?
click to flip
Frontier capability (capped, logged) · burst scaling (own the base, rent the peak — same vLLM image) · geographic reach · managed convenience for non-sensitive loads. Every lane: a written data rule.
M11 · Pricing
The four service tiers and price bands?
click to flip
Starter $5–15K (Phase 1) · Professional $15–40K (Phase 2) · Enterprise $50–200K+ (Phase 3–4, gated) · Retainer $2–10K/mo — the recurring spine. Three options per proposal; anchor high; trade scope, never price.
M11 · Sales
The opener and the close that define the practice?
click to flip
Opener: "Where does your data go when your team uses AI?" Close: the live demo — real question, cited answer, then Wi-Fi off, same answer, five seconds of silence. Witnessed facts get budgeted.
M12 · Capstone
Capstone grading weights and pass mark?
click to flip
Technical 40 · documentation 20 · security 15 · communication 15 · presentation 10 — pass at 80%. Failures cluster in docs+comms — the evidence folder, filled daily, is the defense. Then: CSAA, and the 90-day launch.
06 · The Vocabulary

Searchable Glossary

Every load-bearing term in the program — 110+ entries. Type to filter instantly.

3-2-1 rule
Backup doctrine: three copies of the data, on two different media, one offsite — all encrypted in this practice.
Access-control matrix
The grid mapping every knowledge base to every user group — designed on paper, signed by the client, verified by test logins.
Agent
An AI system that accomplishes goals through the perceive → plan → act → observe loop, acting via granted tools.
Agent loop
The cycle an AI agent runs: perceive → plan → act → observe, repeated until the task is done or escalated.
Anchoring
Pricing psychology: presenting the high option first so the middle option lands as accessible. Three options convert "yes/no" into "which."
Ansible
Infrastructure-as-code tool that configures machines from repeatable "playbooks" pushed over SSH — the furnishing to Terraform's house.
Apache 2.0 / MIT
The permissive open-source licenses most 2026 model families ship under (Gemma 4, Qwen, Mistral, gpt-oss / DeepSeek) — clean for commercial deployment.
API (Application Programming Interface)
A service's front door for software: structured requests to published URLs, JSON in and out, authenticated by keys or tokens.
API key
A long secret string identifying and authorizing a caller — stored in environment variables or vaults, rotated on schedule and on events, never committed to Git.
Approval gate
A human checkpoint before an irreversible action (sending, paying, deleting) — both quality control and a prompt-injection defense.
Attention / Self-attention
The transformer mechanism letting every token weigh its relationship to every other token at once — multi-head attention runs many in parallel.
Audit logging
Retained records of who logged in, what admins changed, and what automations touched — the proof layer behind "we protect data."
BAA (Business Associate Agreement)
The HIPAA contract a vendor signs when its systems touch protected health information — which local AI architecture makes unnecessary for the model itself.
Backpropagation
The training algorithm that traces error backward through a network and nudges every weight in the direction that reduces it.
Bit / Byte
A bit is one on/off value; eight bits make a byte (one character). Model files are billions of bytes of weights.
BM25
The classic keyword-scoring algorithm — the exact-string half of hybrid search that vector embeddings can't replace.
Branch / Merge
Git's parallel timeline: experiment on a branch while main stays deployable; merge back when proven.
Chunking
Splitting documents into pieces (typically 500–1,500 tokens with overlap) for embedding — structural chunking keeps headings, clauses, and tables intact.
CI/CD
Continuous integration/delivery: the automated pipeline taking a Git change through tests and gates (golden set, red team, approval) into production with rollback.
Compliance mapping document
The table translating framework requirements → implemented controls → evidence locations. Your deliverable in regulated deals; the legal determination stays the lawyer's.
Context window
The model's working memory: maximum tokens (instructions + conversation + documents + reply) considered at once. Overflow silently drops the oldest content.
Continuous batching
vLLM's core trick: interleaving many users' token-by-token generations through the GPU so it never idles — hundreds of concurrent chats per server.
CPU / GPU / NPU
The general-purpose brain (sequential, flexible) / the parallel math engine AI runs on / the low-power neural block (Apple's Neural Engine).
CRM
Customer Relationship Management system (GoHighLevel, HubSpot, Salesforce): contacts, pipelines, tags — the socket where AI output becomes business action.
Cross-document consistency
The capstone check: assessment, hardware spec, and architecture diagram must agree on users, models, machine, and ports — read in one sitting.
CSAA
Certified Sovereign AI Architect — the AI Impact Pros designation earned by completing and defending a full live deployment at 80%+.
CUDA
NVIDIA's GPU programming ecosystem — nearly two decades of AI software built on it form the moat behind NVIDIA's training dominance.
Data classification
The discovery-time sort of client data into Public / Internal / Restricted tiers — driving web-search toggles, encryption, compliance scope, and price.
Default deny
The firewall principle: block all inbound, then allow named ports from named sources only — the highest-value security idea in the course.
Dense vs MoE
Dense models use every parameter per token; Mixture-of-Experts routes each token through a subset. Memory follows total params; speed follows active params.
DHCP reservation
The router permanently mapping a machine's hardware address to one IP — the clean way to give a server an unchanging address.
Docker
Container technology: package an app with its dependencies (image) and run it identically anywhere (container). The delivery format of your whole stack.
Docker Compose
A YAML file declaring services, ports, volumes, and environment in one reviewable document — your deployment as version-controlled code.
Drift
Quality degrading without a crash — after a KB update, model swap, or upgrade. Caught by golden sets, not by ops dashboards.
Embedding / Embedding model
A vector (list of numbers) positioning text by meaning, produced by a small specialized model — the heart of RAG retrieval. Changing models means re-embedding everything.
Emergent capabilities
Abilities (arithmetic, coding, reasoning) that appear with model scale without being explicitly trained — present in large models, absent in small siblings.
Environment variable
A named value the shell holds for programs to read — the professional home of API keys, never source code.
Error workflow
The n8n flow that fires when any production workflow fails, alerting a human with the payload — the heart of "no silent failures."
Escalation cascade
Try the small model first with a confidence check; re-run low-confidence items on the big model — big-model quality at small-model average cost.
EUV / ASML
Extreme-ultraviolet lithography, the only way to print leading-edge chips — and ASML of the Netherlands is the only company on Earth that builds the machines.
Evidence folder
The capstone's meta-deliverable: every artifact (recordings, screenshots, stopwatch logs, signatures) filed daily under the ten deliverable numbers.
Exponential backoff
Retry discipline for 429/500 errors: wait 1s, then 2s, 4s, 8s — never hammer a struggling API in a tight loop.
FileVault / LUKS
Full-disk encryption on macOS / Linux — free, one checkbox, and the only security layer that follows stolen hardware.
Fine-tuning
Further training that changes a model's weights — for permanent behavior (voice, format), never for fast-changing facts (that's RAG).
Friday note
The weekly five-line status email (done / next / status / needs / one number) — eight minutes that manufacture the client's sense of control.
Function calling / Tool use
The model emits a structured JSON request; the surrounding harness executes it and returns results as context. The model writes; the harness does.
GDPR
Europe's rights-based privacy law — access, correction, erasure. Local processing sidesteps its hardest problem (transatlantic data transfers).
Golden set
Fixed input→expected-output test cases per critical workflow, re-run and scored against baseline after every change — the regression gate for AI quality.
GPU memory utilization
vLLM's dial for how much VRAM is claimed for weights + KV cache — the headroom knob of production serving.
Grafana / Prometheus
The open-source monitoring pair: Prometheus scrapes time-series metrics from exporters; Grafana draws the dashboards and alerts on trajectories.
Hallucination
Confident, plausible, wrong output — inherent to plausibility-driven generation. Engineered around with RAG grounding, citations, and human review.
Handoff package
The six-part transfer making the system the client's: documentation, credentials, runbooks, security checklist, backup procedure, training materials.
HBM
High Bandwidth Memory — stacked beside data-center GPUs (B300: 288 GB at ~8 TB/s), several times faster than any desktop memory.
Headless
A server with no monitor or keyboard, administered via SSH and screen sharing — fewer attack surfaces, fewer casual fingers.
HIPAA / PHI
The US healthcare privacy framework and the protected health information it governs — prime sovereignty territory, since local models transmit PHI to no one.
Hybrid search
Semantic vectors + BM25 keywords, merged by reciprocal-rank fusion — meaning and exact strings, both caught.
Idempotency
Processing duplicate webhook deliveries safely by tracking event IDs — because providers retry, and three welcome emails is two too many.
IDS / IPS
Intrusion detection / prevention systems — traffic watchers for attack patterns. Phase 3+ equipment; before that, firewall logs and anomaly-visible dashboards.
Incident response plan
Two pages in the drawer: roles with phone numbers, three severities, and the first-hour checklist. Golden rule: isolate, don't destroy.
Inference
Running a trained model on frozen weights to generate output — your entire business lives on this side of the training/inference divide.
Input / Output tokens
What you send vs what the model generates — billed separately (output 3–5× pricier), with the whole history re-sent as input every chat turn.
Internal DNS
Naming the server (ai.company.local) so the service decouples from the hardware — migrations become a one-record repoint.
JSONL
JSON Lines — one JSON object per line; the standard format for fine-tuning datasets (instruction → ideal response pairs).
Key management
Three rules: custody (every secret has a named home), rotation (calendar + event triggers), separation (the key never lives with what it locks).
Kubernetes (K8s)
The cluster operating system: declare desired state (replicas, GPU resources) and it makes reality match. Join the client's cluster; don't build one for three boxes.
KV cache
The attention memory each conversation holds — grows with context length and parallel sessions, and is the hidden RAM cost beyond model weights.
Lethal trifecta
Private data + untrusted input + an outbound channel in one agent = exfiltration risk under prompt injection. Break any leg, per agent, by design.
Load balancer
The traffic distributor in front of duplicated services — health-checks backends and routes around failures (failover) invisibly.
Local Mountain
The deployment playbook name for a sovereign, locally-hosted AI system — Phase 1 (single machine) through Phase 4 (enterprise fortress).
Logic gate / Transistor
A microscopic electrical switch, and the tiny circuits (AND/OR/NOT) built from them — all computation, assembled from switches.
LoRA / QLoRA
Low-Rank Adaptation: training small adapter matrices on a frozen base model — hours and dollars instead of weeks and clusters. QLoRA does it on a quantized base.
MCP (Model Context Protocol)
The standard for connecting AI models to tools: one MCP server per capability, usable by every MCP client — N+M instead of N×M integrations.
Memory bandwidth
GB/s the memory delivers to processors — the number that sets local token speed (bandwidth ÷ model size ≈ tok/s ceiling).
Memory pressure
The hardware metric that kills local AI boxes: weights + KV caches + containers tip into swap and speed falls off a cliff. Alert before it.
MSA (Master Services Agreement)
The standing contract over your SOWs: limitation of liability (capped at fees), IP ownership, confidentiality, termination.
MSP
Managed Service Provider — the client's existing IT vendor. Channel, not competitor: court them with the network change-request document.
n8n
The open-source workflow engine (port 5678): triggers, nodes, AI steps against local models, error workflows — the intelligence layer's automation backbone.
Needs assessment
The signed discovery document: quantified use cases, user census, data classification, constraints — the artifact every later decision cites.
Neural network / Parameters
Layers of multiply-and-add neurons whose learned weights (parameters) are the model — "8B" means eight billion adjustable numbers.
NVLink
NVIDIA's private high-speed bridge letting GPUs in a server pool memory and act as one accelerator — what makes multi-GPU models servable.
OAuth
The authorization standard where users grant scoped, expiring, revocable tokens at the provider's own login — your app never sees the password.
Ollama
The local model engine (port 11434): pull, run, list, rm — plus an OpenAI-compatible API. The engine room of Phases 1–2.
OpenAI-compatible API
The de-facto standard request shape (/v1/chat/completions) served by OpenAI, DeepSeek, Ollama, and vLLM alike — swap cloud for local by changing one URL.
Open WebUI
The self-hosted chat interface (port 3000): users, groups, knowledge bases, custom agents, web search — the product layer clients actually see.
Open weights
Models whose parameters are freely downloadable (Gemma, Llama, DeepSeek, Qwen, Mistral, gpt-oss) — the supply side of the sovereignty business.
Overfitting
Training too long until the model memorizes instead of generalizes — visible when training scores climb while held-out scores fall.
P95 latency
The time the slowest 5% of requests exceed — the honest user-pain metric; averages hide the 9 AM agony.
PagedAttention
vLLM's memory manager: KV cache allocated in small on-demand pages like OS memory — eliminating reservation hoarding, multiplying concurrent sessions.
PCI DSS
The payment-card security framework — best handled by scope avoidance: card data lives only in the processor's certified systems, never your stack.
Permission-aware retrieval
ACL filtering inside the vector search — unauthorized chunks never enter candidates or context, across every app, by construction.
PIT (Private Integration Token)
GoHighLevel's scoped API credential — select only the permissions the workflow needs; store in the credential vault; rotate quarterly.
Port mapping
Docker's -p host:container flag forwarding a host port into the container — host side first; the #1 "can't reach the app" suspect.
Pre-training / RLHF
The monster first stage (trillions of tokens → base model) and the preference-alignment polish that makes assistants helpful rather than merely fluent.
Prompt injection
Hostile instructions riding in the single text channel — direct (chat) or indirect (documents, emails, web pages your automations process). Mitigated by architecture, never eliminated.
Quantization
Reducing weight precision (FP16 → 4-bit) to shrink memory ~75% for a few percent quality — the technology that makes local AI on normal hardware possible.
RAG (Retrieval-Augmented Generation)
Chunk → embed → store → retrieve → generate: the model answers from your documents, with citations, without retraining.
RAM equation
(Loaded weights + embeddings + KV budget + OS/stack overhead) ÷ 0.7 usable, then 50–100% headroom — the spec derivation you show in proposals.
Rate limit / 429
The API's "slow down" — N requests or tokens per window. Handle with exponential backoff, never tight-loop retries.
Red-team set
The standing adversarial test plan: direct prompts, poisoned documents, hostile emails, trap webpages — run quarterly and on every agent, tool, or model change.
Re-ranker
A small cross-encoder reading query and chunk together to re-order a generous candidate set — the highest-ROI retrieval upgrade.
Restore drill
Quarterly: restore last night's volumes to a scratch container, log in, see real data, stopwatch it. The number it produces IS your RTO.
Retainer
The recurring support tier ($2–10K/mo): monitoring response, gated updates, KB refresh, reviews — the spine of consulting revenue.
Reverse proxy
A small front container (Caddy, nginx) terminating TLS (https) for the web UI — non-optional for restricted data or Phase 2+.
RPO / RTO
Recovery Point Objective (max acceptable data loss → backup frequency) and Recovery Time Objective (max downtime → recovery tier). The client sets both, explicitly.
Runway account
Six months of lean expenses banked — the leverage to decline bad clients and hold prices through slow quarters.
Shadow AI
Staff already pasting company data into free chatbots without policy — nearly universal, and the risk your opener question surfaces.
SOC 2
The audit standard for business trust (security, availability, integrity, confidentiality, privacy) — your documentation habit is its evidence folder.
SoC / Unified memory
System-on-a-Chip: CPU, GPU, and NPU on one die sharing one memory pool — why a Mac's GPU can use nearly all system RAM for models.
SOW (Statement of Work)
The engagement's constitution: countable deliverables, exclusions, client obligations with dependency language, acceptance checklist, payment terms, amendment clause.
SSH / SSH keys
Encrypted remote terminal control — key-based, never passwords; how headless servers are administered from anywhere (via VPN).
Stateless
The model remembers nothing between requests — every "memory" is application engineering re-sending or retrieving context.
Straight-through rate
The share of items an automation handles with zero human touch — the business metric that proves workflow value and sells renewals.
System prompt / instructions
The privileged text silently prepended to every request — identity, rules, format, escalation; the cheapest highest-leverage memory layer.
Tax vault
The separate account receiving 25–30% of every payment on arrival — because nobody withholds for the self-employed and April keeps receipts.
Temperature / Top-p
The randomness dials: temperature reshapes the next-token distribution (low = focused, high = creative); top-p fences sampling to the probable nucleus.
Tensor Core
NVIDIA circuitry doing nothing but matrix multiplication at reduced precision — the operation that dominates neural-network compute.
Tensor parallelism
Splitting every layer's weights across multiple GPUs that compute each token cooperatively — how models bigger than one GPU get served.
Terraform
Infrastructure-as-code for what exists: servers, networks, firewall rules — declared, diffed against reality, applied, versioned.
Time to first token
The wait before text starts appearing — dominated by prompt reading, grows with input length; the responsiveness half of benchmark speed.
TLS / https
Transport Layer Security — encryption on the wire, delivered to your stack by a reverse proxy so office chats can't be sniffed.
Token / Tokenization
The chunks models read and bill by (≈¾ of a word) — text broken into vocabulary pieces so anything can be expressed.
Training cutoff
The date a model's knowledge freezes — bridged at runtime by web search or RAG, never by wishful thinking.
TSMC
Taiwan Semiconductor Manufacturing Company — the foundry making ~90% of the world's most advanced chips, including Apple's and NVIDIA's.
UPS (uninterruptible power supply)
The battery that bridges outages and brownouts — sized ~2× the machine's draw, paired with auto-restart for self-recovering servers.
Value-based pricing
Pricing against the client's quantified gain (recovered labor, error costs) instead of your hours — how you capture your own learning curve.
Vector database
Storage that finds nearest-meaning matches among embeddings — embedded (Chroma) at Phase 1; Weaviate/Milvus/Qdrant/pgvector at enterprise scale.
VLAN
Virtual LAN — the router carving one physical network into isolated segments (office / guest / IoT) so the guest Wi-Fi can't route to the AI server at all.
vLLM
The production inference server (port 8000): continuous batching, PagedAttention, tensor parallelism, native Prometheus metrics — Phase 3's engine.
Volume
Docker's persistent storage mounted into disposable containers — users, chats, KBs, and workflows survive updates only because they live here.
VPN / WireGuard
The encrypted tunnel placing remote devices inside the office network — per-device keys, revocable; the only professional remote access (never port-forwarding).
Webhook
Push-based eventing: the service POSTs your URL the moment something happens — n8n's first-class trigger and the spine of event-driven workflows.
Wi-Fi-off demo
The sales close: a real question answered from local documents, then the same answer with the internet visibly off — sovereignty as a witnessed fact.
Zero trust
Never trust by network location; verify every request — scoped ports, per-person credentials, per-device keys, no shared logins.

No terms match that search.

09 · Prove It

Final Review Quiz

Twenty-six questions spanning all twelve modules — the certification exam's study bank. Answer in your head, then expand to check.

Q1Name the five hardware components, and state which one decides whether a model can run at all.
Answer: CPU, GPU, RAM, SSD, Neural Engine. RAM (or VRAM) decides — a model must fit entirely in memory to run; the SSD only stores it.
Q2How much memory does a 14B model need at 4-bit quantization, and on what machine does it comfortably run?
Answer: ~7 GB for weights (params ÷ 2) plus 1–3 GB overhead — comfortable on a 16 GB machine.
Q3Estimate the token-speed ceiling for a 546 GB/s Mac Studio running a 20 GB model.
Answer: 546 ÷ 20 ≈ 27 tokens/second — well above the ~15 tok/s fluid-feel threshold; real-world lands at 50–80% of ceiling.
Q4What is NVIDIA's deepest moat, and name the current shipping flagship's memory spec.
Answer: CUDA — two decades of software ecosystem. The B300 "Blackwell Ultra" ships with 288 GB of HBM3e at roughly 8 TB/s per GPU.
Q5Which paper introduced the transformer, and what two problems did attention solve at once?
Answer: "Attention Is All You Need" (Vaswani et al., 2017). It solved long-range forgetting (every token sees every other) and lack of parallelism (all positions process simultaneously on GPUs).
Q6Why does the same prompt produce different answers, and how do you make outputs reproducible?
Answer: Sampling randomness over the next-token distribution. Set temperature to 0 (greedy) and/or fix the random seed for reproducibility.
Q7An MoE model lists "284B total / 13B active." What does each number determine?
Answer: 284B sets the memory requirement (all experts load); 13B sets per-token compute — it generates at 13B-class speed. Quote both when sizing hardware.
Q8A client wants the AI to "know" their 2,000-page manual AND "always sound like us." Which memory layer serves each?
Answer: The manual → RAG (knowledge). The voice → fine-tuning or system instructions (behavior). Facts are retrieved; behavior is trained or instructed.
Q9You get a 401, then a 429. Decode each and state the correct response.
Answer: 401 = authentication failed (fix the key/header). 429 = rate-limited (exponential backoff: 1s, 2s, 4s — never tight-loop retries).
Q10You accidentally committed and pushed an API key. What's the first action, and why isn't deletion enough?
Answer: Rotate (revoke + regenerate) the key immediately — Git history preserves the old commit forever, and scrapers find public keys within minutes.
Q11Decode docker run -d -p 3000:8080 -v open-webui:/app/backend/data flag by flag.
Answer: -d detached (background) · -p host port 3000 forwards to container port 8080 · -v named volume "open-webui" mounted at the data path so users/chats/KBs survive container replacement.
Q12Why can't the Open WebUI container reach Ollama at localhost:11434, and what's the fix?
Answer: Inside a container, localhost is the container itself. Use host.docker.internal:11434 and set OLLAMA_HOST=0.0.0.0 so Ollama accepts non-local connections.
Q13RAG answers keep missing facts you know are in the documents. Name the two likeliest causes and the tuning discipline.
Answer: Top-K too low, or chunks too large (diluted embeddings). Change one variable at a time and re-score against a fixed question set.
Q14List the five sections of a production system prompt.
Answer: Role → rules (positive and negative) → output format → escalation paths → worked examples (few-shot).
Q15What does web search re-open in the privacy bubble, and how do you handle it professionally?
Answer: The search query leaves the network (documents and chats don't). Disclose it, disable search on sensitive agents, scope a PSE to trusted sites, and draw it in the data-flow diagram.
Q16Define the "no silent failures" standard for n8n workflows in three clauses.
Answer: Retries with backoff on every external node; error-branch routing with JSON validation after AI nodes; a workflow-level error flow alerting a human — no item lost without a trace.
Q17Recite the optimization order when a deployment slows, and the rule it protects.
Answer: Prompts → parameters → model swap → load shaping → hardware. Cheapest lever first: never buy hardware to fix a prompt problem.
Q18Reconstruct the Exam 7 Phase 1 deployment checklist (the 10 items, 4 hours).
Answer: OS configured · Ollama network-exposed · 3 models benchmarked · Open WebUI (volume, admin, locked signup) · 5 users in ≥2 groups · RAG with 20 docs · 3 custom agents · 2 n8n workflows + error flow · backup executed AND restore-tested · security checklist + golden-set baseline.
Q19Why is P95 latency the honest metric for the Phase 2 upgrade memo?
Answer: Averages hide peak pain — P95 captures what the slowest 5% of requests (the 9 AM pile-up) actually feel, which is what users complain about and what capacity must serve.
Q20Sort into recipe / state / re-fetchable: compose files, chat history, model weights, KB source docs, n8n workflows — and state what each implies for backup.
Answer: Recipe: compose files (Git is the offsite). State: chat history, n8n workflows, KB docs (nightly encrypted volumes). Re-fetchable: model weights (skip; re-pull on restore). Small backups run; run backups survive.
Q21Define RPO and RTO, and explain why the quarterly drill produces "the only real RTO."
Answer: RPO = max acceptable data loss; RTO = max acceptable downtime. Only a stopwatched actual restore measures recovery — everything undrilled is an estimate wearing a number.
Q22Why can't prompt injection be patched like SQL injection, and what are the four layers of the defense stack?
Answer: LLMs have one undifferentiated text channel — no syntax boundary between instructions and data. Defense: least-privilege tools, the lethal-trifecta test per agent, input/output handling (sanitize, validate structurally), and model safety configs — plus a quarterly red-team set.
Q23What may you claim in a HIPAA engagement, and what must you never claim?
Answer: Claim: controls built to the Security Rule with documented evidence (the compliance mapping). Never claim: "this makes you HIPAA compliant" — compliance is organizational (policies, training, BAAs) and the determination belongs to their compliance officer/counsel.
Q24vLLM: name the two inventions that enable hundreds of concurrent users, and the three flags that set the capacity envelope.
Answer: Continuous batching and PagedAttention. Flags: --tensor-parallel-size, --max-model-len, --gpu-memory-utilization.
Q25Recite the four service tiers with price bands, and the two "quiet" SOW sections that protect every engagement.
Answer: Starter $5–15K · Professional $15–40K · Enterprise $50–200K+ · Retainer $2–10K/mo. Quiet sections: exclusions (kills "I assumed it was included") and client-obligation dependency language (client delays shift the timeline day-for-day, not your margin).
Q26Capstone: the five grading weights, the pass mark, and the first-ninety-days launch sequence after the CSAA.
Answer: Technical 40 / documentation 20 / security 15 / communication 15 / presentation 10 — pass at 80%. Then: convert the capstone client to a retainer, publish the case study, collect two referrals, book the first talk, start the weekly pipeline cadence.
